The Six Types of Personal Propositions
Table and truth conditions for six proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six types of personal propositions (simple affirmative/negative, modified affirmative/negative, privative affirmative/negative) and state their truth conditions.

Core Idea
Simple, modified, and privative propositions map all logical possibilities for a subject.

The author introduces six proposition types using the example 'Zaid is just'. Understanding these types is essential before exploring their logical relationships. The table shows how each type's truth depends on the subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three pairs
The six propositions come in three pairs: simple (affirmative/negative), modified (affirmative/negative), and privative (affirmative/negative). Note how the predicate changes in each: positive, negated, or privative.
Simple: 'Zaid is just' vs. 'Zaid is not just'. Modified: 'Zaid is non-just' vs. 'Zaid is not non-just'. Privative: 'Zaid is unjust' vs. 'Zaid is not unjust'.
2
Examine truth conditions for simple propositions
A simple affirmative (e.g., 'Zaid is just') is true if Zaid is just; a simple negative ('Zaid is not just') is true if Zaid is not just. The author also notes conditions when the subject does not exist.
For personal propositions, existence of subject matters.
3
Analyze modified propositions
Modified propositions involve a negated predicate. 'Zaid is non-just' (modified affirmative) means Zaid lacks justice; 'Zaid is not non-just' (modified negative) means Zaid is just. Their truth conditions are more complex and given in the table.
There is a shift alert: in later logic, 'modified' may refer to negated copula, but here it is negated predicate.
4
Understand privative propositions
Privative propositions use a privative term like 'unjust'. 'Zaid is unjust' (privative affirmative) implies Zaid has the opposite of justice; 'Zaid is not unjust' (privative negative) denies privation. Truth conditions depend on the nature of the predicate.
Privative terms are often used in Kalam for attributes.

The First Table: On the explanation of the judgments of personal [propositions]. Let its layout be as follows:
Zaid is just.
Zaid is not just.
It is true if he is just, and false in what its opposite is true in, that is, when the subject does not exist while justice exists, and it is true in every [case] and the existence of the opposite and the intermediate, and when it is false in its opposite, it is true with the non-existence of what does not exist in the subject potentially, and with the subject, and the existence of the opposite and the intermediate, the subject being not receptive to it, and consider and when the subject is receptive to it potentially, or [derive] its examples yourself.

Zaid is unjust.
Zaid is not unjust.
It is true if he is unjust, and false in the rest.
Every layer along the horizontal line: two are contraries, they do not combine in truth nor in falsehood. So the contrary of the simple affirmative is the simple negative, and the contrary of the modified negative is the modified affirmative, and the contrary of the privative negative is the privative affirmative.
And every layer along the vertical line from the right side: it is more general in truth than what is above it and more specific in falsehood. So the privative negative is more general in truth than the modified negative, because it is true in what it is true in and more; and the modified negative is more general in truth than the simple affirmative, because it is true in what it is true in and more.
As for the vertical layers from the left side, it is the opposite. So every layer from it is more general than what is below it, and what is below it is more specific. So the simple negative is more general in truth than the modified affirmative, because it is true in what it is true in and more; and the modified affirmative is more general in truth than the privative affirmative, because it is true in what it is true in and more.
So for the more general, from its falsehood follows the falsehood of the more specific, and its truth follows from the truth of the more specific, and not vice versa. So from the truth of the simple affirmative follows the truth of the modified negative, and from the truth of the modified negative follows the truth of the privative negative, and from the falsehood of the privative negative follows the falsehood of the modified negative, and from the falsehood of the modified negative follows the falsehood of the simple affirmative. And from the truth of the privative affirmative follows the truth of the modified affirmative, and from the truth of the modified affirmative follows the truth of the simple negative, and from the falsehood of the simple negative follows the falsehood of the modified affirmative, and from the falsehood of the modified affirmative follows the falsehood of the privative affirmative.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the square of opposition for personal propositions. The author first presents a table (اللوح الأول) for propositions with the subject 'Zaid' and predicates 'just' (عدل) and 'unjust' (جائر). The table lists four propositions for each predicate: simple affirmative (Zaid is just), simple negative (Zaid is not just), modified negative (Zaid is not non-just), and modified affirmative (Zaid is non-just). The truth conditions are given for each, though the OCR makes some details unclear. After the table, the author generalizes the logical relationships. He states that along the horizontal line, contrary pairs (متقابلان) cannot both be true nor both false. The pairs are: simple affirmative vs. simple negative, modified negative vs. modified affirmative, and privative negative vs. privative affirmative. Along the vertical line, from the right side (likely the right column of the table), each lower proposition is more general in truth (أعم صدقاً) than the one above, meaning it is true in all cases the above is true and more. Thus, the privative negative is more general than the modified negative, which is more general than the simple affirmative. From the left side, the relationship is reversed: the higher proposition is more general. So the simple negative is more general than the modified affirmative, which is more general than the privative affirmative. The author then explains the logical consequences: if the more general is false, the more specific is false; if the more specific is true, the more general is true (but not vice versa). He then lists all specific implications between the six propositions. For example, if the simple affirmative is true, then the modified negative is true; if the privative negative is false, then the modified negative is false, etc. This completes the description of the square, showing the systematic relations of truth and falsehood among these proposition types.

Immediate Inference

Logical Relations: Contrariety and Subalternation
Explanation of logical relations among six proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which propositions are contraries and which are in a subalternation relationship, and apply entailment rules between them.

Core Idea
Horizontally contraries; vertically, lower propositions are more general in truth.

After defining the six proposition types, the author explains their logical connections. Horizontally, each pair (simple, modified, privative) are contraries: cannot both be true or both false. Vertically, there is a hierarchy: lower propositions are more general in truth. This lesson unpacks those relationships and the rules of inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify horizontal contraries
The passage states: 'كل طبقة في العرض فمتقابلان لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب'. So each row contains a contrary pair: simple affirmative vs. simple negative, modified negative vs. modified affirmative, privative negative vs. privative affirmative.
Contrariety means they cannot be both true or both false. If one is true, the other is false; if one is false, the other is true? Actually, contraries cannot both be false in classical logic, but the author says 'neither in truth nor in falsehood', meaning they are contradictory? Wait, 'لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب' means they cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false. So it's actually contradiction, not contrariety. But the author calls them mutaqabilān, which in this system is contradiction. However, the subsequent vertical relations show they are not simple contradictories because they can both be false? The passage says 'لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب' which is definition of contradictory. But then vertical subalternation suggests that the affirmatives and negatives are connected. Actually, the author says each pair is 'متقابلان' meaning they are contradictories (opposites). But then the vertical relations show that, for example, simple affirmative and simple negative cannot be both true nor both false only if subject exists? The source earlier mentioned truth conditions with non-existence. So it's consistent: for personal propositions with existential import, contrariety holds only when subject exists? The passage doesn't explicitly say. But the description 'لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب' is exact definition of contradictory. So I think the author considers each horizontal pair as contradictory. But then the vertical relations show subalternation. I'll treat them as contradictories for the guided path, but note the nuance. Actually, reviewing: In the table, the simple affirmative and simple negative: if Zaid exists and is just, affirmative true, negative false; if Zaid is not just, affirmative false, negative true; if Zaid does not exist, both are false? That would contradict 'لا يجتمعان في الكذب'. So perhaps the author assumes subject exists for personal propositions? The truth conditions earlier mention when subject does not exist for some propositions. So maybe the rule holds only when subject exists. But the commentary says 'لا يجتمعان في الصدق ولا في الكذب' which is exactly contradictory. I'll present as contradictory pairs, but add a note about existential import. Actually, the common misunderstanding field mentions that students confuse with simple contradiction. So it's fine.
2
Understand vertical gradation
Along the right vertical line, each lower proposition is more general in truth (أعم صدقاً) than the one above. Example: privative negative is more general than modified negative, which is more general than simple affirmative.
'More general in truth' means it is true in all cases where the above is true, and additionally in some cases.
3
Apply the entailment rules
From truth of the more specific (above) follows truth of the more general (below). From falsehood of the more general follows falsehood of the more specific. The passage lists specific chains: e.g., if simple affirmative is true, then modified negative is true; if privative negative is false, then modified negative is false.
This subalternation is a type of immediate inference.
4
Practice with concrete example
Take the proposition 'Zaid is just' (simple affirmative). If it is true, then 'Zaid is not non-just' (modified negative) must also be true, and 'Zaid is not unjust' (privative negative) must also be true. If 'Zaid is not unjust' is false, then 'Zaid is not non-just' is false, and 'Zaid is just' is false.

Contradiction Rules for Quantified Propositions
Author defines truth conditions for contradictory quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify contradictory pairs among universal and particular affirmative and negative propositions, including privative (ma'dula) forms.

Core Idea
Contradictories differ in both quantity and quality; their truth values are always opposite.

In previous lessons, we learned about singular propositions and their contradictories. Now the author extends this to quantified propositions. Understanding these rules is essential for constructing valid arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh, where contradiction is used to refute positions and test consistency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
The passage lists universal affirmative, particular negative, universal affirmative privative (ma'dula), and particular negative privative ('adamiyya). Notice the pattern: each type has a simple and a privative version.
2
Find the contradictory pairs
A contradictory pair must differ in quantity (universal vs particular) and quality (affirmative vs negative). For example, 'Every human is just' and 'Not every human is just' are contradictories.
The passage explicitly states these as examples.
3
Check truth conditions
For each pair, verify that they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. The author gives conditions: e.g., the universal is true iff all individuals satisfy the predicate; the particular is false iff all satisfy it.
4
Apply to privative propositions
When the predicate includes negation (e.g., 'not-just') or privation (e.g., 'tyrannical'), the same contradictory relation holds, but truth conditions adjust. Study the given examples carefully.
Privative propositions are called ma'dula or 'adamiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كانت المحصورات إما متناقضة أو متضادة أو داخلة تحت المتضادة وكانت أحكامها مما تختلف وجب أن نرسم لبيان كل قسم منها لوحاً، ولتكن البداية بالمتناقضة منها، وهي إما أن تكون الكليات منها موجبات أو سوالب فإن كانت موجبات فأحكامها في التقابل والتعاند والتلازم طولاً وعرضاً وقطراً كأحكام الشخصية هكذا
كل إنسان هو عدل
ليس كل إنسان عدلاً

سالبة جزئية بسيطة تكذب إذا كان الكل عادلين وتصدق في البواقي.

كل إنسان هو غير عادل

ليس كل إنسان هو جائراً
كليّة موجبة عديمة تصدق إذا كان الكل جائرين وتكذب في البواقي.
جزئية سالبة عديمة تكذب إذا كان الكل جائرين وتصدق في البواقي.
واعتبر أحكامها بنفسك تجده على ما أسلفناه في الشخصيات.
And since the quantified propositions are either contradictory or contrary or falling under the contrary, and their rulings differ, we must draw a table to explain each type. Let the beginning be with the contradictory ones. They are either universal affirmatives or negatives. If they are affirmatives, then their rulings in terms of opposition, contrariety, and entailment along length, width, and diagonal are like the rulings of singular propositions, as follows:
Every human is just.
Not every human is just.
Universal affirmative simple: true if all are just, false otherwise.
Particular negative simple: false if all are just, true otherwise.
Not every human is not-just.
Every human is not-just.
Universal affirmative privative: false if they are all non-existent or some are just or all are just, true in other cases.
Particular negative privative: false if all are tyrannical, true otherwise.
Every human is tyrannical.
Not every human is tyrannical.
Universal affirmative privative: true if all are tyrannical, false otherwise.
Particular negative privative: false if all are tyrannical, true otherwise.
And judge their rulings yourself; you will find them as we have stated for singular propositions.

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for contradictory (متناقضة) quantified propositions. The author begins by noting that quantified propositions (المحصورات) are divided into contradictories, contraries (متضادة), and those falling under contraries. Here the focus is on contradictories. Two propositions are contradictory if they differ in both quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). The author provides a table showing four types: universal affirmative simple (كل إنسان هو عدل) and its contradictory particular negative simple (ليس كل إنسان عدلاً), with truth conditions: the universal is true iff all humans are just, and false otherwise; the particular is false iff all are just, and true otherwise. Then come the 'privative' (معدولة) forms: 'not every human is not-just' (which is a particular affirmative privative) and 'every human is not-just' (universal affirmative privative). Their truth conditions are given. Finally, 'universal affirmative privative' with 'tyrannical' (جائر) is described. The author concludes by saying the reader can verify these against the earlier treatment of singular propositions. The lesson reinforces the square of opposition for modal or privative terms.

Rules of Contradiction for Categorical Propositions
Author explains conditions for contradiction and lists four contradictory pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four contradictory pairs of categorical propositions and state the conditions under which two propositions are contradictories.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires opposite quality and opposite quantity: one universal, one particular.

In this passage, the author explains the precise conditions under which two categorical propositions are true contradictories (naqid). He clarifies common pitfalls, such as when both universals can be false or both particulars true. Understanding these rules is essential for valid immediate inference in logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition for contradiction
Two propositions are contradictories if they differ in both quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular). Mere difference in quality is insufficient, as two universals (A and E) can both be false and two particulars (I and O) can both be true.
2
Distinguish contradictories from contraries
Contraries are pairs of universals (A and E) that cannot both be true but can both be false. The author gives the example 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human' – both false.
3
Recognize that two particulars can both be true
Particulars (I and O) can be simultaneously true, as in 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human'.
4
Identify the four contradictory pairs
From the text: contradictory of universal affirmative (A) is particular negative (O); of particular affirmative (I) is universal negative (E); of universal negative (E) is particular affirmative (I); of particular negative (O) is universal affirmative (A).
5
Apply the rule to any categorical proposition
Given any categorical proposition, change the quantifier (universal ↔ particular) and flip the quality (affirmative ↔ negative). This yields the contradictory.

But it is possible for both to be false; because the attribution of 'doctor' to Zayd might be by possibility, both in negation and affirmation, not by necessity, while the mode in both is by necessity for negation and affirmation. They are only contradictories if the negation particle enters the mode and the predicate, as in our saying: 'Zayd is not necessarily a doctor.' The second is as in our saying in the affirmative 'Zayd is a skillful doctor and advisor' and in the negative 'Zayd is a doctor, not skillful nor advisor' or 'Zayd is a skillful doctor, not an advisor' — then there is no contradiction between them; because the falsity in both may be due to the attribution of 'doctor' to him, and 'doctor' is attributed to him in both. But they are only contradictories if the negation particle enters everything, as in our saying: 'Zayd is not a skillful doctor advisor' even if it is not determined what the negation denies — is it all of that affirmative or part of it? That is, is he not skillful or not an advisor or both? Rather, the predicate with its attributes is made one thing, then it is said that this predicate is not for the subject. All of this does not differ in any of the singular, indefinite, and quantified propositions. Rather, if they are quantified, add as we indicated the difference in quantity; otherwise, the two universal contraries can both be false when the predicate is true of some of the subject by affirmation and false of all of it, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human, no animal is a human.' The two particulars under the contraries can both be true for the meaning we indicated in the universals, as when we say 'Some animal is a human, some animal is not a human.' Therefore, their division of truth and falsity only occurs if they differ in quantity such that one is universal and the other particular. For when the universal is false in affirmation or negation, its falsity may be due to the falsity of every single individual, or it may be due to the falsity of some. Thus falsity is certain whether the other part is false or true, since it is not necessary from the falsity of the whole the falsity of the part, nor from the falsity of the part the falsity of the whole. Rather, from the falsity of the part when the universal is affirmative follows the truth of the negation about the part not about the whole, and from the falsity of the part when the universal is negative follows the truth of the affirmation about the part not about the whole. This if the proposition is declarative; then from its falsity, if it is affirmative, follows the truth of the universal negative, otherwise the affirmative part would not be false; and if it is negative, from its falsity follows the truth of the universal affirmative, otherwise the negation about the part would not be false. Thus the contradictory of the universal affirmative is the particular negative, and the contradictory of the particular affirmative is the universal negative, and the contradictory of the universal negative is the particular affirmative, and the contradictory of the particular affirmative is the universal negative.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for two categorical propositions to be true contradictories (naqid). The author begins by discussing how negation interacts with modality and predicates: two propositions can both be false if the negation does not properly govern the whole sense. Using the example of 'Zayd is a doctor', the author shows that if the predicate is qualified by attributes, the negation must apply to the entire predicate to produce a contradictory. The author then generalizes: for universal and particular propositions, the rule depends on quantity. Two universals (like 'every animal is human' and 'no animal is human') can both be false if the predicate applies to some but not all. Two particulars can both be true. The only way to guarantee that two propositions cannot both be true and cannot both be false (i.e., are contradictories) is to have one universal and one particular with opposite quality. The author then explicitly states the four rules: contradictory of universal affirmative = particular negative; of particular affirmative = universal negative; of universal negative = particular affirmative; of particular negative = universal affirmative. These rules are foundational for immediate inference in the Aristotelian syllogistic.

Key Terms
نقيض (naqid): The contradictory proposition; one that always differs in truth value from the original. [Note: Stable term across logic tradition.]
تناقض (tanaqud): The relation between two propositions that cannot both be true and cannot both be false. [Note: Standard definition in logic.]
كلي (kulli): Universal proposition; applies to all individuals of the subject. [Note: No significant shift.]
جزئي (juz'i): Particular proposition; applies to some individuals. [Note: No significant shift.]

Contradictories of Absolute ('Ammi) Propositions
Author explains the true contradictory of absolute propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of an absolute proposition of the common kind ('ammiyya) and state its necessary consequence.

Core Idea
An absolute 'ammi proposition has no absolute contradictory; its contradictory negates the absolute mode itself.

This passage tackles a subtle point in propositional logic: how to properly contradict an absolute proposition. The key insight is that an absolute proposition with no temporal specification can be true at some times and false at others, so simply reversing its truth value doesn't give a valid contradictory. Instead, we must negate the absolute mode, yielding a proposition that is permanently true. This lesson will walk you through the author's reasoning and show how to apply it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of absolute proposition
The author classifies absolute propositions into three types: 'ammi (common), khassi (specific), and waqti (temporal). For 'ammi, the judgment has no fixed time. Recognize that this is the type under discussion.
2
Understand why 'ammi has no contradictory of its kind
Two absolute propositions of opposite quality (e.g., 'Every human is sleeping' and 'Not every human is sleeping') can both be true if the judgment holds at different times. Thus they are not true contradictories, since contradictories cannot both be true.
The author gives the example of 'Every human is sleeping' and 'Not every human is sleeping' being both possibly true.
3
Formulate the true contradictory
The true contradictory of an absolute affirmative is a proposition that negates the absolute mode itself. For 'Zayd is a writer absolutely', the contradictory is 'Not absolutely is Zayd a writer'.
4
Determine the necessary consequence
The necessary outcome (lazim) of the contradictory is a permanent (daim) proposition: 'Zayd is always not a writer'. This permanence may be accidental or necessary; only permanence is required, not necessity.
The author warns against taking the necessary as the lazim, because that could allow both propositions to be false.
5
Apply the pattern to other cases
The same reasoning applies to quantified and particular propositions. Practice by constructing the contradictory for another absolute proposition, e.g., 'Some people are writing'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حقق شروط التناقض مما أشرنا إليه هانت عليه معرفة النقائض الحقيقية ولوازمها في الحَمْليات والشرطيات، لكنه لما كان ذلك صعباً على غير المتدرب وجب أن نشير إلى تحقيق نقيض كل قضية وما يلزم منه، فنقول القضية إما أن تكون مطلقة أو موجهة وإن كانت مطلقة فإطلاقها إما بالمعنى العامي أو الخاصي أو الوقتي، فإن كان إطلاقها عامياً فلا نقيض لها من جنسها كما ظن، وسواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، شخصية أو مهملة أو محصورة، إذ لا امتناع لصدقهما معاً؛ لجواز أن يكون الحكم غير دائم ولا في واحد، بل في وقت ما غير معين، وأن يكون الحكم بالإيجاب لا في وقت الحكم بالسلب والحكم بالسلب لا في وقت الحكم بالإيجاب كما في قولنا كل إنسان نائم ليس كل إنسان نائماً أو لا واحد من الناس نائماً بعض الناس نائماً وعلى هذا النحو في ما بقي من المحصورات والمخصوصات، فإذن النقيض الحقيقي لما أثبت أو سلب بالإطلاق بالمسبة إلى موضوع ما هو بالذات سالب للإطلاق عن ذلك الموضوع، ولازمه ما يدل على نفي ما أثبته الإطلاق أو إثبات ما نفاه في كل وقت صالح أن يتناوله الإطلاق لتحقيق ذلك في المخصوصات والمحصورات، أما المخصوصة فإن كانت موجبة مطلقة كقولنا: (بالإطلاق) زيد (كاتب فنقيضها الحقيقي شخصي سالب للإطلاق، وهو :قولنا: ليس بالإطلاق زيد كاتباً ولازمه زيد) دائماً ليس بكاتب إذ هو الدال على نفي ما أثبت في النقيض في كل وقت صالح لحكم النقيض، ولما كان السلب الدائم عن الشخصيات قد يتفق أن يكون اتفاقياً من غير ضرورة وقد يكون ضرورياً، وكل ضروري دائم، وليس كل دائم ضرورياً، كان اللازم هو الدائم دون الضروري، بل لو أخذ الضروري لازماً لما امتنع الاجتماع في الكذب؛ لجواز أن يكون المحمول مسلوباً عن الموضوع دائماً من غير ضرورة كما إذا قيل (بالإطلاق زيد
And whoever realizes the conditions of contradiction that we have indicated will find it easy to know the true contradictories and their necessary consequences in categorical and conditional propositions. However, since that is difficult for the unpracticed, we must indicate the verification of the contradictory of every proposition and what follows from it. So we say: a proposition is either absolute or modal. If it is absolute, its absoluteness is either in the common sense, or in the particular sense, or in the temporal sense. If its absoluteness is common, then it has no contradictory of its kind, as was thought, whether it is affirmative or negative, singular, indefinite, or quantified; because there is no impossibility of both being true simultaneously; since the judgment may be neither permanent nor in one [time], but in some unspecified time, and the judgment by affirmation may be not at the time of the judgment by negation, and the judgment by negation not at the time of the judgment by affirmation, as in our saying: 'Every human is sleeping, not every human is sleeping,' or 'no human is sleeping, some human is sleeping.' And so on for the rest of the quantified and particular propositions. Thus, the true contradictory of what is affirmed or negated absolutely with respect to a subject is essentially a negative of the absolute mode from that subject, and its necessary consequence is what indicates the negation of what the absolute mode affirmed or the affirmation of what it negated in every time that is suitable for the absolute mode to encompass. To clarify this for particulars and quantifieds: as for the particular, if it is an absolute affirmative like our saying: 'Absolutely, Zayd is a writer,' then its true contradictory is a singular negative of the absolute mode, which is our saying: 'Not absolutely is Zayd a writer,' and its necessary consequence is 'Zayd is always not a writer,' because it indicates the negation of what was affirmed in the contradictory in every time suitable for the judgment of the contradictory. And since the permanent negation of singulars may happen accidentally without necessity or may be necessary, and every necessary is permanent, but not every permanent is necessary, the necessary consequence is the permanent, not the necessary. Indeed, if we took the necessary as a consequence, it would not prevent both from being false; because the predicate may be negated from the subject permanently without necessity, as when it is said 'Absolutely, Zayd...'
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage addresses the challenging topic of finding the true contradictories for absolute (mutlaqa) propositions — those without any modal or temporal qualification. The author first reminds the reader that understanding the conditions of contradiction makes it easy to identify true contradictories and their entailments in both categorical and conditional propositions. However, because this is difficult for beginners, the author explains it in detail. A proposition is either absolute or modal. If absolute, the absoluteness can be of three types: 'ammi (common), khassi (particular/specific), or waqti (temporal). If the absoluteness is 'ammi, there is no contradictory of the same kind (i.e., another absolute proposition) because both could be true simultaneously: the judgment might hold at some unspecified time but not at another. For example, 'Every human is sleeping' and 'Not every human is sleeping' could both be true if some are sleeping at one time and not at another. Therefore, the true contradictory of an absolute proposition is not another absolute proposition but rather a negation of the absolute mode itself. Specifically, the contradictory of an absolute affirmative is a proposition that negates the absolute mode, and its necessary consequence is a permanent (daim) negation — e.g., 'Zayd is always not a writer'. The author emphasizes that the necessary consequence must be permanent, but not necessarily necessary (i.e., the permanence might be accidental). If we mistakenly required necessity, the two propositions could both be false (e.g., if the predicate is actually permanently absent but not necessarily). This careful distinction illustrates the precision required in modal logic.

Key Terms
مطلقة (mutlaqa): An absolute proposition without modal or temporal specification; can be 'ammi, khassi, or waqti. [Note: In later logic texts, 'mutlaqa' often means 'absolutely without condition', but here it is contrasted with 'muwajjaha' (modal).]
عامية ('ammiyya): Absolute in the common sense; no restriction on time or mode. [Note: This specific classification of mutlaqa into 'ammi, khassi, waqti is particular to this text.]
خاصية (khassiyya): Absolute with a specific time or condition.
وقتية (waqtiyya): Absolute with a temporal condition.
لازم (lazim): The necessary consequence of the contradictory, which is a permanent (daim) affirmative or negative. [Note: Here 'lazim' is the logical entailment, not the accidental attribute in ontology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Zayd is necessarily not a writer

Key Takeaway
The true contradictory of an absolute 'ammi proposition is not an absolute opposite but a proposition that negates the absolute mode, with a permanent necessary consequence.

Finding the True Contradictory of Absolute Propositions
Explains the rules for finding the true contradictory for different types of absolute propositions and critiques a proposed method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true contradictory of any absolute categorical proposition and explain why a simple quality opposite fails.

Core Idea
An absolute proposition's true contradictory negates its absoluteness, not just its quality.

You already know the standard rules: 'Every B is C' contradicts 'Some B is not C'. But these rules assume the truth is fixed at one time. What if the judgment is not perpetual? For absolute propositions—those without modal qualification—two apparent opposites can both be true at different times. This lesson shows you how to find a genuine contradictory that eliminates this temporal loophole, a skill essential for airtight reasoning in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall standard contradictory rules
Write down the four standard pairs: universal affirmative vs. particular negative, etc. These work for propositions that are explicitly or implicitly perpetual.
2
Identify the problem with absolute propositions
An absolute proposition like 'Every human is sleeping' can be true at one time and 'Not every human is sleeping' at another. Both can be true together in different times. So the standard pair is not contradictory.
The absoluteness ('itlaq) can be common, specific, or temporal.
3
Find the true contradictory for singular absolutes
For 'Absolutely, Zayd is a writer', the true contradictory is 'Not absolutely, Zayd is a writer', which entails perpetual negation: 'Zayd is perpetually not a writer'. This removes the temporal flexibility.
The consequence is perpetual (daim) not necessarily (daruri), because accidental perpetuality still contradicts.
4
Extend to quantified absolutes and critique the 'as long as' trick
For a universal absolute like 'Absolutely, every B is C', the true contradictory is 'Absolutely, not every B is C' with the consequence 'perpetually not every B is C'.
The trick of adding 'as long as it is B' fails because both propositions can be false or both true; genuine contradiction requires the contradictory to be absolute common with a condition of persistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية. كاذباً، وإن كانت سالبة لزم من كذبها صدق الإيجاب الكلي، وإلا ما كان السلب عن البعض كاذباً، فإذن نقيض الكلية الموجبة جزئية سالبة، ونقيض الجزئية كليّة موجبة ونقيض الكلية السالبة جزئية موجبة ونقيض الجزئية الموجبة كليّة سالبة. ومن حقق شروط التناقض مما أشرنا إليه هانت عليه معرفة النقائض الحقيقية ولوازمها في الحَمْليات والشرطيات، لكنه لما كان ذلك صعباً على غير المتدرب وجب كقولنا : (بالإطلاق زيد ليس بكاتب فنقيضها الحقيقي (زيد ليس بالإطلاق ليس بكاتب بل دائماً زيد كاتب، وإن كانت المطلقة محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فنقيضها الحقيقي ما عرف ولازمه دائماً ليس كل "ب" "ج") إن كانت القضية بالإطلاق ليس كل "ب" "ج"). والمهملات حكمها حكم الجزئيات، قال أفضل المتأخرين فإن أردنا أن نجد للمطلقة نقيضاً من جنسها كانت الحيلة فيه أن نجعل القضية أخص مما يوجبه نفس الإيجاب أو The first art: on categorical propositions. …false, and if it was negative, from its falsehood follows the truth of the universal affirmative, otherwise the negation of some would not be false. Thus the contradictory of the universal affirmative is a particular negative, and the contradictory of the particular is a universal affirmative, and the contradictory of the universal negative is a particular affirmative, and the contradictory of the particular affirmative is a universal negative. Whoever verifies the conditions of contradiction from what we have indicated, the knowledge of true contradictories and their consequences in categoricals and conditionals becomes easy for him. But since that is difficult for one not trained, we must indicate the verification of the contradictory of every proposition and what follows from it. We say: the proposition is either absolute or modal. If it is absolute, its absoluteness is either in the common sense, or the specific sense, or the temporal sense. If its absoluteness is common, then it has no contradictory of its own kind, as was thought. And whether it is affirmative or negative, singular, indefinite, or quantified, for there is no impossibility of both being true together, because the judgment may be neither perpetual nor at one time, but at an unspecified time, and the judgment of affirmation may not be at the time of the judgment of negation, and the judgment of negation may not be at the time of the judgment of affirmation, as in our saying: 'Every human is sleeping' 'Not every human is sleeping' or 'No human is sleeping' 'Some human is sleeping.' And similarly for the rest of the quantified and individuated propositions. So the true contradictory of what is affirmed or negated absolutely with respect to a subject is essentially the negation of absoluteness from that subject, and its necessary consequence is what indicates the negation of what the absoluteness affirmed or the affirmation of what it negated in every time suitable for the absoluteness to encompass it, to achieve that in individuated and quantified propositions. As for the individuated, if it is an absolute affirmative like our saying: 'Absolutely, Zayd is a writer', then its true contradictory is a singular negation of absoluteness, which is our saying: 'Not absolutely, Zayd is a writer', and its necessary consequence is 'Zayd is perpetually not a writer', because it indicates the negation of what was affirmed in the contradictory in every time suitable for the judgment of the contradictory. And since perpetual negation of singulars may be accidental without necessity or may be necessary, and every necessary is perpetual but not every perpetual is necessary, the necessary consequence is the perpetual without the necessary. Rather, if the necessary were taken as the consequence, non-contradiction in falsehood would not be prevented, because the predicate may be negated of the subject perpetually without necessity, as when it is said 'Absolutely, Zayd is white' when [Zayd] is perpetually black, 'Zayd is necessarily not white'. And the accidental perpetual, although possible, and the possible is more specific than the absolute universal, and from the falsehood of the more general follows the falsehood of the more specific, yet non-contradiction between the perpetual and the absolute universal is prevented only if absolute possibility is included under the absolute universal, for then from the falsehood of absolute possibility would follow the falsehood of the possible perpetual, but it is not so. Rather, what is included under the absolute of the possible is only what must exist, not what may or may not exist. And then contradiction occurs inevitably. This if it is affirmative. If it is negative, like our saying: 'Absolutely, Zayd is not a writer', then its true contradictory is 'Zayd is not absolutely not a writer, but Zayd is perpetually a writer.' And if the absolute proposition is quantified, it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, its true contradictory is what is known, and its necessary consequence is 'perpetually not every B is C' if the proposition is 'Absolutely, not every B is C'. The indefinite propositions have the same rule as the particulars. The most excellent of the later scholars said: if we want to find for the absolute a contradictory of its own kind, the trick is to make the proposition more specific than what the absolute affirmation or negation itself necessitates, such that the judgment of the predicate on the subject holds as long as it is characterized by the condition taken with the subject. This is in the universal affirmative by our saying: 'Every B is C as long as it is B', and similarly for the rest of the quantified propositions. But there is a consideration: for the contradictory, if it is conventional of the kind of the mentioned proposition, they do not contradict each other, because it is possible for them to both be false if the predicate is established for the subject in some times of its being characterized as described, not perpetually, as in our saying: 'Every person with pleurisy coughs as long as he has pleurisy' and 'Some person with pleurisy does not cough as long as he has pleurisy'. And if it is absolutely common, there is also no contradiction, because it is possible for them to both be true, as the affirmative may be true at the time of the persistence of the attribute, and the negative true at its absence. Contradiction only occurs when the contradictory is absolutely common with the condition of the persistence of its subject in the conventional manner, such that we say the contradictory of 'Every B is C as long as it is B' is 'Some B, as long as it is B, is not C' absolutely commonly. And although they differ in type, they agree in genus because the common absolute is true of both. And the statement of the most excellent of the later scholars must be interpreted accordingly.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the nature of contradictories (naqaid) for absolute (mutlaqa) categorical propositions. The author begins by reiterating the standard rules of contradiction for quantified propositions: the contradictory of 'Every B is C' is 'Some B is not C', and so on. He then notes that mastering the conditions of contradiction makes it easy to know true contradictories, but since this is difficult for beginners, he explains in depth.

The key insight is that an absolute proposition—one without explicit modal qualification—can have its absoluteness in different senses: common (ammi), specific (khassi), or temporal (waqti). If the absoluteness is in the common sense, the proposition has no contradictory of its own kind because both it and its supposed opposite could be true at different times. For example, 'Every human is sleeping' and 'Not every human is sleeping' can both be true if the first is true at one time and the second at another. Therefore, the true contradictory of an absolute proposition must negate the absoluteness itself: it asserts that the predicate is perpetually (daim) affirmed or denied. This is the 'true contradictory' (al-naqid al-haqiqi).

The author distinguishes between singular (shakhsiyya) and quantified (mahsura) absolute propositions. For a singular affirmative absolute, 'Zayd is absolutely a writer', the true contradictory is 'Zayd is not absolutely a writer', which entails 'Zayd is perpetually not a writer'. The necessary consequence is perpetual negation, not necessary negation, because the perpetual could be accidental. He explains that if we took necessary negation as the consequent, it would fail to contradict when the predicate is perpetually but not necessarily absent.

He then introduces a trick by 'the most excellent of the later scholars' (probably Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or similar) to find a contradictory of the same type by adding a condition that the predicate holds 'as long as' (ma dama) the subject has a certain attribute. But the author critiques this: such a proposition and its supposed contradictory may both be false or both be true, as illustrated with the medical example of pleurisy (majnub). The only way to achieve genuine contradiction is to make the contradictory an absolute common proposition conditional on the subject persisting conventionally. He concludes that the trick must be understood in that light.

نقيض
مطلقة
إطلاق عامي

Key Takeaway
The true contradictory of an absolute proposition negates its absoluteness, resulting in a perpetual judgment, not a simple quality opposite.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
8 min
Contradictory of Particular Propositions under Temporal Qualification
Derives contradictory and necessary consequence for particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the proper contradictory and necessary constant consequence of a particular affirmative proposition under restricted temporal qualification.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'exists as' forces a constant alternative: always or always not.

When a proposition is qualified as 'exists as' (bi al-wujud), it means 'at some time, not always'. Its proper contradictory must differ in both quantity and quality, and its falsity forces a constant truth — either the constant affirmative or constant negative. This lesson walks through the derivation for particular (makhsusa) propositions using the example of 'Zayd exists as a writer'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage first considers particular (makhsusa) propositions under restricted temporal qualification. Example: 'Zayd exists as a writer' (بالوجود زيد كاتب). Note that 'exists as' means 'at some time, not always'.
2
State the proper contradictory
The proper contradictory (naqid haqiqi) of the affirmative is: 'Zayd does not exist as not a writer' (ليس بالوجود زيد ليس بكاتب). It differs in quality (negative instead of affirmative) but remains particular, and the temporal operator 'exists as' is negated as a whole.
The contradictory flips both the copula and the predicate negation.
3
Analyze the meaning of 'exists as'
The phrase 'exists as' (بالوجود) means 'at some time' (في وقت ما) and 'not always' (لا دائماً). So the proposition asserts that Zayd is a writer at some time but not at all times.
This is crucial for deriving the falsity conditions.
4
Derive the two possible falsity conditions
The falsity of the original proposition can be due to either: (a) 'at a time' is false, meaning the opposite constant holds (e.g., always not a writer if original was affirmative), or (b) 'not always' is false, meaning the same quality is constant (e.g., always a writer). Thus two possibilities emerge.
The passage states: 'فكذبهما إما لقولنا في وقت فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم... أو لقولنا لا دائماً فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم...'
5
Conclude the necessary constant consequence
From the falsity conditions, the necessary consequence (lazim) of the contradictory is either 'always Zayd is a writer' or 'always Zayd is not a writer'. Constancy (dawam) is sufficient for truth; it need not be necessary (daruri).
The author emphasizes that every necessary is constant but not every constant is necessary.

Source Text

وأما إن كان الإطلاق بالمعنى الخاصي فإما أن تكون القضية أيضاً مخصوصة أو محصورة فإن كانت مخصوصة فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها وهي زيد بالوجود كاتب) (زيد ليس بالوجود ليس بكاتب ولازمه في كلا الحالين إما دائماً زيد كاتب، وإما دائماً زيد ليس كاتباً، لأنا إذا قلنا بالوجود زيد (كاتب كان معناه وقت ما لا دائماً، وكذا إذا قلنا بالوجود ليس بكاتب) أي في وقت ما لا دائماً، فكذبهما إما لقولنا في وقت فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم إن كانت سالبة، أو السلب الدائم إن كانت موجبة، وإما لقولنا لا دائماً فيصدق الإيجاب الدائم إن كانت موجبة أو السلب الدائم إن كانت سالبة. والدوام في المخصوصات، وإن اتفق إن كان ضرورياً، فقد يكون اتفاقياً، وقد بان أن كل ضروري دائم، وليس كل دائم ضرورياً، فكان الدوام هو الصادق دون الضروري، وإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة أو جزئية، فإن كانت كلية فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها، وهو (بالوجود كل "ب" "ج" ) (ليس بالوجود كل "ب" "ج") ولازمه في كلا الحالتين أيضاً إما (دائماً بعض "ب" "ج") وإما (دائماً ليس بعض "ب" "ج") إذ من ضرورة الكذب في البعض الكذب في الكل كما عرف فالبعض متيقن، ولما كان الكذب في البعض متيقناً فإما أن يكون الكذب لقولنا: (في وقت) أو لقولنا: (لا) دائماً) ويلزم من ذلك أن يكون الصادق إما دوام الإيجاب في البعض أو دوام السلب في البعض كما سبق، وقد بان أن الدوام في البعض قد يكون ضرورياً، وقد يكون اتفاقياً، فكان الدوام أيضاً متيقناً وكافياً عن الضرورة. وإن كانت جزئية فنقيضها الحقيقي لموجبتها وهي (بالوجود بعض "ب" "ج") ليس بالوجود بعض "ب" "ج") ولسالبتها، وهي (بالوجود ليس بعض "ب" "ج") (ليس بالوجود ليس بعض "ب" "ج") ولازمه في كلا الحالتين أيضاً دائماً إما كل) اب" "ج") وإما (دائماً لا شيء من "ب" "ج" ) أي كل واحد واحد لا الكل جملة، إذ هو قد يكذب مع فرض كذب النقيض كما في قولنا : بالوجود بعض العدد زوج) فإنه يكذب لكونه ضرورياً، ومع كذبه يكذب كل عدد زوج) و(كل عدد ليس بزوج) بل اللازم دائماً (كل) (عدد) أي كل واحد واحد لا الكل الجملة إما زوج وإما فرد؛ لضرورة أن ما عرف من لزوم دوام الإيجاب أو دوام السلب لكذب الوجود من كذب البعض إيجاباً أو سلباً صدق السلب أو الإيجاب في الكل أي في كل واحد واحد.
And as for if the qualification is with the restricted meaning, then either the proposition is also particular or quantified. If it is particular, then its proper contradictory for its affirmative, which is 'Zayd exists as a writer' is 'Zayd does not exist as not a writer', and its necessary consequence in both cases is either 'always Zayd is a writer' or 'always Zayd is not a writer', because when we say 'Zayd exists as a writer' its meaning is 'at some time, not always', and similarly when we say 'exists as not a writer', i.e., at some time not always. So their falsity is either by our saying 'at a time', then the constant affirmative is true if it was negative, or the constant negative if it was affirmative, or by our saying 'not always', then the constant affirmative is true if it was affirmative, or the constant negative if it was negative. And constancy in particulars, even if it happens to be necessary, may be accidental, and it has been shown that every necessary is constant, but not every constant is necessary, so the constant is the true without the necessary. And if it is quantified, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, then its proper contradictory for its affirmative, which is 'by existence every B is C' is 'not by existence every B is C', and its necessary consequence in both cases also is either 'always some B is C' or 'always some B is not C', because from the falsity of the particular, the falsity of the universal is necessary, as known, so the particular is certain. And when the falsity in the particular is certain, then either the falsity is due to our saying 'at a time' or due to our saying 'not always', and it follows from that that the true is either constant affirmation in the particular or constant negation in the particular, as before. And it has been shown that constancy in the particular may be necessary and may be accidental, so constancy is also certain and sufficient instead of necessity. And if it is particular, then its proper contradictory for its affirmative, which is 'by existence some B is C' is 'not by existence some B is C', and for its negative, which is 'by existence some B is not C' is 'not by existence some B is not C', and its necessary consequence in both cases also is always either 'every B is C' or 'always no B is C', i.e., each and every one, not the whole collectively, because it may be false while assuming the contradictory is false, as in our saying: 'by existence some number is even', for it is false because it is necessary, and with its falsity, 'every number is even' and 'every number is not even' are false, rather the necessary is always 'every number', i.e., each and every one not the whole collectively, either even or odd; because of the necessity that what is known of the necessity of constant affirmation or constant negation from the falsity of existence from the falsity of the particular in affirmation or negation, the truth of the negation or affirmation in the universal, i.e., in each and every one.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage defines the proper contradictory (naqid haqiqi) and its necessary consequence (lazim) for categorical propositions under the restricted temporal qualification (al-Itlaq bi al-Ma'na al-Khasi). It covers three types: particular (makhsusa), universal (kulliyya), and particular (juz'iyya) quantified. The author explains that for a particular proposition like 'Zayd exists as a writer' (meaning at some time, not always), its contradictory is 'Zayd does not exist as not a writer', and the necessary consequence is either constant affirmation or constant negation of the subject's being a writer. This follows from analyzing the truth conditions: the falsity of the 'at some time' proposition can be due to either 'at a time' (implying the opposite constant) or 'not always' (implying the constant of the same quality). The author distinguishes constancy (dawam) from necessity (darura), stating that every necessary is constant but not vice versa, and constancy suffices for truth. For quantified propositions, the same reasoning applies: for universal affirmative, the contradictory is 'not by existence every B is C', and the necessary consequence is constant particular affirmation or negation. For particular affirmative, the contradictory is 'not by existence some B is C', and the necessary consequence is constant universal (each and every one) either affirmative or negative. The author uses an example from numbers: 'some number is even' is false because it is necessarily false (since all numbers are not even), and its falsity forces the conclusion that each number is either even or odd. The entire derivation relies on the logic of immediate inference through contradictories and the equivalence of falsity conditions under temporal qualification.

دائم
ضروري

Key Takeaway
For a particular proposition under restricted temporal qualification, the contradictory's necessary consequence is a constant proposition (dawam), which suffices for truth without requiring necessity (darura).

Contradictory of Universal Quantified Propositions under Temporal Qualification
Derives contradictory and necessary consequence for universal quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper contradictory and necessary constant consequence of a universal quantified proposition under restricted temporal qualification.

Core Idea
For a universal 'exists as' proposition, the contradictory forces a constant particular — either some always are or some always are not.

When a quantified proposition is universal (kulliyya) under the restricted temporal qualification, its contradictory is 'not by existence every B is C'. The falsity of this contradictory leads to a necessary constant consequence regarding some members of the subject class. This lesson unpacks the derivation, which mirrors the particular case but with a shift from universal to particular quantity in the consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal proposition
The passage considers 'by existence every B is C' (بالوجود كل ب ج) as the universal affirmative under restricted temporal qualification.
2
State the proper contradictory
The proper contradictory is 'not by existence every B is C' (ليس بالوجود كل ب ج). This negates the whole proposition: it is not the case that every B exists as C.
The contradictory differs in quality but remains universal in form? Actually, the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative in standard logic, but here the author treats it as a negated universal. The necessary consequence clarifies the quantity.
3
Derive the necessary consequence
The falsity of the original universal 'by existence every B is C' necessarily implies the falsity of the corresponding particular 'by existence some B is C'. Since the particular is false, its two possible falsity conditions (as in the particular case) apply, leading to either 'always some B is C' or 'always some B is not C'.
The passage says 'من ضرورة الكذب في البعض الكذب في الكل' meaning the falsity of the particular necessitates the falsity of the universal. So the particular is certain (متيقن).
4
Apply the two falsity conditions to the particular
The falsity of the particular 'by existence some B is C' can be due to 'at a time' (implying the opposite constant for that some) or 'not always' (implying the same constant for that some). Thus the consequence is a constant particular: either always some B is C or always some B is not C.
As before, constancy (dawam) is sufficient; it may be necessary or accidental.
5
Conclude the constant particular
Therefore, the necessary consequence (lazim) of the contradictory for a universal affirmative under restricted temporal is a constant particular proposition. The passage emphasizes that constancy is certain and sufficient, not requiring necessity.
The same reasoning applies to universal negative mutatis mutandis.

Commentary
This passage defines the proper contradictory (naqid haqiqi) and its necessary consequence (lazim) for categorical propositions under the restricted temporal qualification (al-Itlaq bi al-Ma'na al-Khasi). It covers three types: particular (makhsusa), universal (kulliyya), and particular (juz'iyya) quantified. The author explains that for a particular proposition like 'Zayd exists as a writer' (meaning at some time, not always), its contradictory is 'Zayd does not exist as not a writer', and the necessary consequence is either constant affirmation or constant negation of the subject's being a writer. This follows from analyzing the truth conditions: the falsity of the 'at some time' proposition can be due to either 'at a time' (implying the opposite constant) or 'not always' (implying the constant of the same quality). The author distinguishes constancy (dawam) from necessity (darura), stating that every necessary is constant but not vice versa, and constancy suffices for truth. For quantified propositions, the same reasoning applies: for universal affirmative, the contradictory is 'not by existence every B is C', and the necessary consequence is constant particular affirmation or negation. For particular affirmative, the contradictory is 'not by existence some B is C', and the necessary consequence is constant universal (each and every one) either affirmative or negative. The author uses an example from numbers: 'some number is even' is false because it is necessarily false (since all numbers are not even), and its falsity forces the conclusion that each number is either even or odd. The entire derivation relies on the logic of immediate inference through contradictories and the equivalence of falsity conditions under temporal qualification.

Contradictory of Particular Quantified Propositions under Temporal Qualification
Derives contradictory and necessary consequence for particular quantified propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the proper contradictory and necessary constant universal consequence of a particular quantified proposition under restricted temporal qualification.

Core Idea
For a particular 'exists as' proposition, the contradictory forces a constant universal — each and every individual either always is or always is not.

The final case covers particular quantified propositions (juz'iyya) like 'by existence some B is C'. Its proper contradictory flips the quantity to universal in the consequence? Actually, the consequence is a constant universal, but not in the collective sense: it applies to each and every individual. This lesson unpacks the derivation using the example of 'some number is even' to illustrate how a necessary false particular forces a constant universal distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular quantified proposition
Consider 'by existence some B is C' (بالوجود بعض ب ج) as a particular affirmative under restricted temporal qualification.
There is also a negative counterpart: 'by existence some B is not C'.
2
State the proper contradictory
The proper contradictory of the affirmative is 'not by existence some B is C' (ليس بالوجود بعض ب ج). For the negative, it is 'not by existence some B is not C'.
As before, the contradictory negates the entire 'exists as' operator.
3
Derive the necessary consequence using an example
The passage gives the example 'by existence some number is even' (بالوجود بعض العدد زوج). This proposition is false because it is necessarily false (since all numbers are not even? Actually, some numbers are even, but the point is that it is false under the temporal qualification? Wait: The passage says 'فإنه يكذب لكونه ضرورياً' meaning it is false because it is necessary? Actually, the example: 'بالوجود بعض العدد زوج' – some number is even. This is false because it is necessarily? No, 'some number is even' is true in reality. The passage says it is false because it is necessary? This is confusing. Reread: 'كما في قولنا : بالوجود بعض العدد زوج) فإنه يكذب لكونه ضرورياً' – It says it is false because it is necessary. That means under the temporal qualification 'by existence', the claim 'some number is even' is false because it is not merely 'at some time' but necessary? Actually, the idea: 'some number is even' is necessarily true (since numbers are necessarily even or odd), but the temporal 'by existence' implies contingency. So the claim is false because it is presented as contingent but it is actually necessary. So the falsity of the particular forces a universal constant: either always every number is even or always no number is even (i.e., every number is odd). But since 'every number is even' is false and 'every number is odd' is false, the consequence is the disjunction of the two constants: each number is either always even or always odd? The passage says: 'بل اللازم دائماً (كل) (عدد) أي كل واحد واحد لا الكل الجملة إما زوج وإما فرد' – the necessary is always every number, i.e., each and every one, either even or odd. That is, for each individual number, it is either always even or always odd (which is true by necessity of identity). So the consequence is a constant universal in the distributive sense (each one individually) not collective.
4
Generalize the reasoning
The falsity of a particular 'exists as' proposition can be due to the same two conditions (at a time / not always), but because the quantity is particular, the falsity of the particular forces a constant conclusion about each individual member of the subject class: either each individual always is C or each individual always is not C. In other words, a constant universal proposition about every single member.
The passage says 'لضرورة أن ما عرف من لزوم دوام الإيجاب أو دوام السلب لكذب الوجود من كذب البعض إيجاباً أو سلباً صدق السلب أو الإيجاب في الكل أي في كل واحد واحد.' So from the falsity of the particular (khuluw or jam?), we get truth of negation or affirmation in the universal (each one).
5
Distinguish distributive from collective
The consequence is 'always every B is C' or 'always no B is C', but these are understood distributively: each individual B is either always C or always not C. The collective universal (all together) may be false, as in the number example.
This subtlety is important: the constant universal here is not a proposition about the whole set at once, but about each individual separately.

Contradictories of Temporal Propositions
Derives the contradictory and necessary consequence for temporal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the proper contradictory and necessary consequence of any universal or particular temporal proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a temporal proposition negates the predicate while preserving the temporal qualification, leading to a necessary consequence.

This passage explains how to form the proper contradictory and necessary consequence for temporal propositions (al-waqtiyya). Temporal propositions are those where the predicate holds for the subject in a specific assumed time without further temporal stipulation. Understanding this is crucial for constructing and critiquing arguments in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) when time-based conditions are involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the temporal proposition type
Determine whether the temporal proposition is quantified (universal/particular) or unquantified (makhsusa). The passage focuses on quantified ones.
The temporal proposition is defined as one where the predicate's relation to the subject is considered in an assumed time without reference to necessity or possibility.
2
For universal affirmative temporal propositions
The proper contradictory is 'not by temporal qualification every B is C'. Its necessary consequence (lazim) is that some B is not C in that assumed time. Verify that this is a particular negative.
The necessary consequence is a particular negative within the same temporal frame.
3
For particular affirmative temporal propositions
The proper contradictory is 'not some B is C by temporal qualification'. Its necessary consequence is 'every B is C in all the assumed time'.
This flips the quantifier and quality, leading to a universal affirmative in the same temporal scope.
4
Apply to universal negative and particular negative
The text indicates the universal negative follows analogously. For particular negative, the author leaves it as an exercise: work out the contradictory and necessary consequence by parity of reasoning.

وأما إن كان الإطلاق بالمعنى الوقتي فإما أن تكون الوقتية مخصوصة أو محصورة، فإن كانت محصورة فإما كليّة وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة كقولنا بالإطلاق) الوقتي كل "ب" "ج") فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس بالإطلاق الوقتي كل "ب" "ج")، وأما لازم نقيضها فيجب أن يعلم أن القضية الوقتية هي ما كان المحمول فيها في نسبته إلى موضوعه في الوقت المفروض كحاله في نسبته إليه من غير اشتراط وقت وذلك من جهة عدم الالتفات في نسبته إلى الضرورة والإمكان أو أنه في جميع ذلك الوقت أو في بعضه، وعند ذلك فنقول اللازم بعض ما هو "ب" ليس هو "ج" في ذلك الوقت المفروض إما أنه جزئي سالب فالضرورة أن نقيضه كلي موجب لما عرف وإما أنه في جميع الوقت المفروض ليقع التكاذب على ما عرف في المطلقة العامة، وعلى هذا لا يخفى أمر السالبة الكلية، وإن كانت جزئية فإما موجبة وإما سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا : (بعض "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق الوقتي فنقيضها الحقيقي (ليس بعض " "ج" بالإطلاق (الوقتي ولازمه كل "ب" "ج" في جميع الوقت المفروض، وتحققه على ما عرف وعليك باعتبار السالبة الجزئية وما بقي من المخصوصة بنفسك. القضايا وأما إن كانت موجهة فالموجهة إما جهة الضرورة أو الإمكان، فإن كانت جهة الضرورة فإما أن تكون مطلقة أي غير مشروطة أو مشروطة، فإن كانت مطلقة فالقضية إما كليّة أو جزئية أو مخصوصة، فإن كانت كليّة فإما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا: بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج")، فنقيضها الحقيقي هو رفع محمولها عن موضوعها من جهة ما هو موجه من غير التفات إلى أنه هل الصادق رفع النسبة الإيجابية مع الجهة أو الجهة فقط، إذ من ضرورة الكذب في إثبات المحمول للموضوع بجهة صدق سلبه من جهة ما أثبت وهو أن يقال (ليس بالضرورة كل
And as for if the qualification is with the temporal meaning, then either the temporal proposition is particular or quantified. If it is quantified, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is universal affirmative, like our saying 'by temporal qualification every B is C', then its proper contradictory is 'not by temporal qualification every B is C'. And as for the necessary consequence of its contradictory, it must be known that the temporal proposition is one where the predicate in its relation to its subject in the assumed time is like its state in relation to it without stipulation of time, and that from the aspect of not paying attention in its relation to necessity and possibility, or that it is in all that time or in some of it. Upon that, we say: the necessary consequence is that some of what is B is not C in that assumed time; either it is a particular negative, then its contradictory is necessarily a universal affirmative as known, or it is in all the assumed time such that the contradictory occurs as known in the absolute general proposition. On this, the matter of the universal negative is not hidden. And if it is particular, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying 'some B is C by temporal qualification', then its proper contradictory is 'not some B is C by temporal qualification', and its necessary consequence is 'every B is C in all the assumed time', and its verification is as known, and it is upon you to consider the particular negative and what remains of the particular by yourself. The propositions: And as for if they are modal, the modal is either a modality of necessity or possibility. If it is a modality of necessity, then either absolute i.e., unconditional, or conditional. If it is absolute, then the proposition is either universal, particular, or restricted. If it is universal, then either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, like our saying 'by necessity every B is C', then its proper contradictory is the raising of its predicate from its subject from the aspect that it is modal, without consideration of whether the true is the raising of the affirmative relation together with the modality or the modality only, because from the necessity of falsity in affirming the predicate to the subject with a modality, the truth of its negation from the aspect that it was affirmed is by saying 'not by necessity every'.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but related topics: the proper contradictory and necessary consequence for temporal propositions (al-waqtiyya) and for modal propositions (al-muwajjaha). For temporal propositions, the author defines the temporal proposition as one where the relation between subject and predicate holds in a specific assumed time without further specification. For universal affirmative temporal propositions, the contradictory is 'not by temporal qualification every B is C', and the necessary consequence is a particular negative in that same time, which then creates a universal affirmative as its own contradictory. For particular affirmative temporal propositions, the contradictory is 'not some B is C by temporal qualification', and its necessary consequence is 'every B is C in all the assumed time'. The author leaves the particular negative and remaining cases as exercises for the learner. The second part begins modal propositions (qadaya muwajjaha). It introduces modalities of necessity (darura) and possibility (imkan). For absolute necessary universal affirmative, the contradictory is obtained by removing the predicate from the subject while preserving the modal aspect, without considering whether the truth lies in negating the entire proposition including modality or only the modality. The author states that since affirming the predicate with necessity is false, the true contradictory is to deny the predicate of the subject under that same modality, which is expressed as 'not by necessity every...' (the passage cuts off). The analysis shows the careful distinction between different kinds of temporal and modal operators and their impact on contradictory relations.

محمول
موضوع
نقيض حقيقي
لازم

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the necessary consequence (lazim) of the proper contradictory of a universal affirmative temporal proposition (e.g., 'by temporal qualification every B is C')?
A) Some B is not C at that time
B) Every B is not C at that time
C) Not every B is C by temporal qualification
D) Some B is C at that time

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a temporal proposition negates the entire quantified statement, and its necessary consequence is a particular proposition in the same temporal frame.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the temporal proposition 'Every fasting person must refrain from eating during daylight hours in Ramadan'. Form its proper contradictory and necessary consequence, then verify that the necessary consequence is a particular negative.
?
Consider the particular temporal proposition 'Some obligatory prayers are shortened during travel'. Derive its contradictory and necessary consequence, and explain how the necessary consequence relates to an absolute universal.

Contradictories of Modal Propositions (Necessity)
Derives the contradictory for modal propositions of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proper contradictory of a necessary universal affirmative modal proposition.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a necessary proposition denies the predicate under the same modality, not the modality itself.

This passage introduces the proper contradictory for modal propositions (al-muwajjaha), specifically those with the modality of necessity (darura). It explains that the contradictory of a necessary universal affirmative is formed by removing the predicate from the subject while preserving the modal aspect, without determining whether the truth lies in negating the entire proposition or just the modality. This distinction is vital for theological and philosophical arguments such as those in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Classify the modal proposition
Modal propositions are divided into those with necessity (darura) and possibility (imkan). Those with necessity can be absolute (mutlaqa) or conditional (mashruta). The passage focuses on absolute necessary propositions.
Absolute means no additional condition on the subject.
2
For universal affirmative absolute necessary propositions
The proper contradictory is formed by 'raising' (raf') the predicate from the subject from the aspect that it is modal. The author says: 'not by necessity every B is C'.
The exact expression is 'not by necessity every B is C' (the passage is cut off but this is implied).
3
Understand the modal negation
The contradictory does not simply negate the modality (e.g., 'it is not necessary that every B is C') but rather negates the predicate under that modality. This is because the falsity of the necessary claim entails the truth of its negation under the same modal aspect.
This avoids the fallacy of confusing 'not necessary' with 'necessarily not'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
