The Real Contradictory of a Universal Affirmative: Basic Case
Author enumerates the real contradictory and necessary entailments for universal affirmative propositions with various conditions and modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the real contradictory of a universal affirmative modal proposition and list its necessary entailments when no condition is attached to the predicate.

Core Idea
The contradictory of 'Every B is A' is 'Not every B is A', which entails 'Some B is not A'.

In this lesson, we learn how to find the real contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative proposition when no condition is attached to the predicate. The author shows that the contradictory entails a set of particular negative propositions. This is the foundation for understanding contradiction in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original is a universal affirmative of the form 'Every B is J D A' with the predicate stripped of conditions. The modality is permanence (دائماً).
2
State the real contradictory
The contradictory is 'Not every B is J D A', which is a particular negative with the same permanence: 'Some B is not permanently J D A'.
3
Derive the entailments
The author lists all possible specific negations: not permanently J, not permanently D, not permanently A, and combinations. These are the immediate entailments of the contradictory.
4
Recognize the pattern
The contradictory yields a particular negative that can be broken down into component parts. This pattern will repeat with variations in the following sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يلزمه عند تجرد المحمول فقد عرف وهو أنه جزئي دائم إيجاباً أو سلباً، فاللازم هاهنا إما دائماً بعض ما هو "ب" لا دائماً "ج" "د" "أ" أو دائماً ليس "ج" أو دائماً ليس "د" أو دائماً ليس "أ" أو دائماً ليس "ج" "د" أو دائماً ليس "ج" "أ" أو دائماً ليس "د" "أ".
وإن كان الشرط هو شرط المحمول كما في قولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ج" "د" "أ""، فنقيضها الحقيقي ليس كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" ما دام "ج" "د" "أ". ولازمه إما دائماً بعض ما هو "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بـ"ج" "د" "أ" وإما دائماً بعض ما هو "ب" هو "ج" وإما ليس "ج" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" وإما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس "د" وإما ليس "د" في بعض أوقات كونه "د" وإما دائماً بعض "ب" هو "أ" أو ليس "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "أ" وإما دائماً بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" أو ليس "ج" "د" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" "د" وإما دائماً بعض "ب" هو "ج" "أ" أو ليس "ج" "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "ج" "أ" وإما دائماً بعض "ب" هو "د" "أ" أو ليس "د" "أ" في بعض أوقات كونه "د" "أ".
وإن كان الشرط هو الوقت فسواء كان معيناً أو غير معين فالنقيض الحقيقي له كما عرف ولازمه فعلى ما فصلناه في لازم المشروط بشرط المحمول.
وإن كانت الجهة هي جهة الإمكان فإما أن يكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص. فإن كان بالمعنى العام كقولنا: "كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان العام"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، وقد عرف أن لازمه عند تجرد المحمول جزئي سالب بالدوام، فيكون اللازم هاهنا إما بالدوام بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ".
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الخاص كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان الخاص"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان الخاص كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما بالضرورة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما "ج" أو ليس وإما "د" أو ليس وإما "أ" أو ليس وإما "ج" "د" أو ليس وإما "ج" "أ" أو ليس وإما "د" "أ" أو ليس.
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الأخص وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان المذكور"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان الأخص كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة المطلقة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة الوقتية بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ".
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الآخر من الأخص وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية في مستقبل الحال، كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان المشروط"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان المشروط كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة المطلقة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة الوقتية بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ".
ومن أحاط بما حققناه في الموجبة الكلية لم يخف عليه النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة، وإذ أتينا على ما أردناه من تحقيق معنى التناقض فلا بد من الإشارة إلى أن التعاند بين الشيء وضده أتم كما العدل والجور، أو بينه وبين نقيضه كما بين العدل ولا عدل. والحق أن التعاند بالمعنى الثاني أتم من حيث التصديق به والحكم.
And what entails it when the predicate is stripped has already been known, and that is that it is a particular permanent affirmative or negative. So the necessary entailment here is either permanently some of what is 'B' is not permanently 'J' 'D' 'A' or permanently not 'J' or permanently not 'D' or permanently not 'A' or permanently not 'J' 'D' or permanently not 'J' 'A' or permanently not 'D' 'A'. And if the condition is the condition of the predicate, as in our saying: 'Every B is J D A as long as J D A', then its real contradictory is 'Not every B is J D A as long as J D A'. And its entailment is either permanently some of what is 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'not J' at some times of its being qualified by 'J' 'D' 'A' or permanently some of what is 'B' is 'J' or 'not J' at some times of its being 'J' or permanently some of 'B' is not 'D' or 'not D' at some times of its being 'D' or permanently some of 'B' is 'A' or 'not A' at some times of its being 'A' or permanently some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' or 'not J' 'D' at some times of its being 'J' 'D' or permanently some of 'B' is 'J' 'A' or 'not J' 'A' at some times of its being 'J' 'A' or permanently some of 'B' is 'D' 'A' or 'not D' 'A' at some times of its being 'D' 'A'. And if the condition is time, whether determined or undetermined, then its real contradictory is as known, and its entailment is according to what we detailed in the entailment of the conditional with the condition of the predicate. And if the mode is the mode of possibility, then it is either in the general sense or the special or the more special. If it is in the general sense, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with general possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with general possibility every B is J D A', and it has been known that its entailment when the predicate is stripped is a particular negative with permanence, so the entailment here is either with permanence some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And if the possibility is in the special sense, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with special possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with special possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either necessarily some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or necessarily some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or not or 'D' or not or 'A' or not or 'J' 'D' or not or 'J' 'A' or not or 'D' 'A' or not. And if the possibility is in the more special sense, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are removed, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with the mentioned possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with the more special possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either permanently some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And either with absolute necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And either with temporal necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And if the possibility is the other meaning of the more special, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are removed in the future, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with conditional possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with conditional possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either permanently some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And either with absolute necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And either with temporal necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And whoever encompasses what we have verified regarding the universal affirmative will not find hidden the real contradictory and its entailment in the remaining quantified and particular propositions. And since we have come to what we intended from the verification of the meaning of contradiction, it is necessary to point out that the opposition between a thing and its opposite is more complete, as between justice and injustice, or between it and its contradictory, as between justice and not justice. And the truth is that opposition in the second sense is more complete in terms of assenting to it and judging.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical exposition of the real contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative modal proposition and the necessary entailments (lawaazim) that follow from that contradictory. The author systematically covers several cases: first, when the proposition has no explicit condition on the predicate (i.e., the predicate is stripped bare), then when the condition is attached to the predicate (shart al-mahmul), then when the condition is time (shart al-waqt), and finally when the mode (jiha) is possibility (imkan) in its various senses: general (amm), special (khass), more special (akhass) with two sub-types (the one where absolute and temporal necessity are removed without future qualification, and the one where they are removed specifically for the future, called imkan mashrut). For each case, the author states the original universal affirmative proposition, its real contradictory, and then lists the possible entailments—which are particular propositions (positive or negative) that follow necessarily from the contradictory. The logical structure is that the contradictory of a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Not every B is A') entails a particular negative (some B is not A), but in modal logic the entailments become more complex because the modality (permanence, necessity, possibility) and the conditions (predicate-condition, time-condition) affect the quantification and tense. The author concludes by noting that from the treatment of universal affirmative, one can infer the contradictory and entailment for other quantified propositions (universal negative, particular affirmative, etc.). He then adds a brief philosophical note on the nature of opposition (ta'aanud): opposition between a thing and its opposite (e.g., justice and injustice) is more complete than opposition between a thing and its contradictory (justice and not-justice) in terms of the strength of certainty in judgment. This is because the opposition between opposites is a stronger form of contrariety, while the contradictory is a weaker logical relation. The passage is highly technical and assumes familiarity with the theory of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as developed in the Islamic tradition.

اللازم
الدوام

A) Some B is permanently J D A
B) Some B is not permanently J D A
C) Every B is not permanently J D A
D) No B is J D A

Key Takeaway
The real contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, and its entailments are a set of particular negative propositions covering each predicate component.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Real Contradictory with Predicate Condition
Author enumerates the real contradictory and necessary entailments for universal affirmative propositions with various conditions and modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the real contradictory of a universal affirmative when the condition is attached to the predicate (shart al-mahmul) and enumerate its entailments.

Core Idea
When the predicate is conditioned, the contradictory becomes 'Not every B is A as long as A remains'.

The author now considers propositions where the predicate carries a condition: 'as long as it is J D A'. The contradictory and its entailments become more complex because the condition itself must be negated appropriately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
'Every B is J D A as long as J D A'. The predicate condition is that the predicate applies for the duration that the predicate applies (a reflexive condition).
2
State the real contradictory
'Not every B is J D A as long as J D A'. This universal negative is the contradictory.
3
List the entailments
The author gives a long list: some B is J D A permanently, or not J at some times, etc. Each entailment is a particular proposition with a temporal or permanent modifier.
4
Understand the pattern
The entailments cover all possible simple and compound predicates (J, D, A, J D, J A, D A) with either permanent or temporal negation/affirmation.

And what entails it when the predicate is stripped has already been known, and that is that it is a particular permanent affirmative or negative. So the necessary entailment here is either permanently some of what is 'B' is not permanently 'J' 'D' 'A' or permanently not 'J' or permanently not 'D' or permanently not 'A' or permanently not 'J' 'D' or permanently not 'J' 'A' or permanently not 'D' 'A'. Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical exposition of the real contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative modal proposition and the necessary entailments (lawaazim) that follow from that contradictory. The author systematically covers several cases: first, when the proposition has no explicit condition on the predicate (i.e., the predicate is stripped bare), then when the condition is attached to the predicate (shart al-mahmul), then when the condition is time (shart al-waqt), and finally when the mode (jiha) is possibility (imkan) in its various senses: general (amm), special (khass), more special (akhass) with two sub-types (the one where absolute and temporal necessity are removed without future qualification, and the one where they are removed specifically for the future, called imkan mashrut). For each case, the author states the original universal affirmative proposition, its real contradictory, and then lists the possible entailments—which are particular propositions (positive or negative) that follow necessarily from the contradictory. The logical structure is that the contradictory of a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Not every B is A') entails a particular negative (some B is not A), but in modal logic the entailments become more complex because the modality (permanence, necessity, possibility) and the conditions (predicate-condition, time-condition) affect the quantification and tense. The passage is highly technical and assumes familiarity with the theory of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as developed in the Islamic tradition.

The Real Contradictory for Possibility Modalities
Author enumerates the real contradictory and necessary entailments for universal affirmative propositions with various conditions and modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the real contradictory of a universal affirmative proposition with general, special, or more special possibility and list the corresponding entailments.

Core Idea
Each type of possibility (general, special, more special) yields different entailments from the contradictory.

The final section of the passage deals with modal propositions involving possibility (imkan). The author breaks down possibility into three types: general, special, and more special (the latter with two subtypes). For each, he gives the contradictory and its entailments, which involve necessity and permanence in different ways.

Guided Path
1
Identify the possibility type
For each case: imkan 'ammi, imkan khass, imkan akhass (two variants). The proposition is 'Every B is J D A with that possibility'.
2
State the contradictory
For general: 'Not with general possibility every B is J D A', etc. The modality is part of the proposition.
3
List the entailments
For general, entailments are particular negatives with permanence; for special, entailments are necessity (bil-darura) propositions; for more special, both permanent and necessity entailments appear.
4
Compare across types
Notice how the modality of the contradictory changes the modality of the entailments: necessity arises when the possibility is special.

وإن كانت الجهة هي جهة الإمكان فإما أن يكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص. فإن كان بالمعنى العام كقولنا: "كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان العام"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان العام كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، وقد عرف أن لازمه عند تجرد المحمول جزئي سالب بالدوام، فيكون اللازم هاهنا إما بالدوام بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ".
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الخاص كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان الخاص"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان الخاص كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما بالضرورة بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما بالضرورة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما "ج" أو ليس وإما "د" أو ليس وإما "أ" أو ليس وإما "ج" "د" أو ليس وإما "ج" "أ" أو ليس وإما "د" "أ" أو ليس.
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الأخص وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان المذكور"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان الأخص كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة المطلقة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة الوقتية بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ".
وإن كان الإمكان بالمعنى الآخر من الأخص وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية في مستقبل الحال، كقولنا: "كل "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" بالإمكان المشروط"، فنقيضها الحقيقي "ليس بالإمكان المشروط كل "ب" "ج" "د" "أ"، ولازمه إما دائماً بعض "ب" ليس هو "ج" "د" "أ" وإما ليس "ج" وإما ليس "د" وإما ليس "أ" وإما ليس "ج" "د" وإما ليس "ج" "أ" وإما ليس "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة المطلقة بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ". وإما بالضرورة الوقتية بعض "ب" هو "ج" "د" "أ" أو "ج" أو "د" أو "أ" أو "ج" "د" أو "ج" "أ" أو "د" "أ".
ومن أحاط بما حققناه في الموجبة الكلية لم يخف عليه النقيض الحقيقي ولازمه في ما بقي من القضايا المحصورة والمخصوصة، وإذ أتينا على ما أردناه من تحقيق معنى التناقض فلا بد من الإشارة إلى أن التعاند بين الشيء وضده أتم كما العدل والجور، أو بينه وبين نقيضه كما بين العدل ولا عدل. والحق أن التعاند بالمعنى الثاني أتم من حيث التصديق به والحكم.
And what entails it when the predicate is stripped has already been known, and that is that it is a particular permanent affirmative or negative. So the necessary entailment here is either permanently some of what is 'B' is not permanently 'J' 'D' 'A' or permanently not 'J' or permanently not 'D' or permanently not 'A' or permanently not 'J' 'D' or permanently not 'J' 'A' or permanently not 'D' 'A'. And if the condition is the condition of the predicate, as in our saying: 'Every B is J D A as long as J D A', then its real contradictory is 'Not every B is J D A as long as J D A'. And its entailment is either permanently some of what is 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'not J' at some times of its being qualified by 'J' 'D' 'A' or permanently some of what is 'B' is 'J' or 'not J' at some times of its being 'J' or permanently some of 'B' is not 'D' or 'not D' at some times of its being 'D' or permanently some of 'B' is 'A' or 'not A' at some times of its being 'A' or permanently some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' or 'not J' 'D' at some times of its being 'J' 'D' or permanently some of 'B' is 'J' 'A' or 'not J' 'A' at some times of its being 'J' 'A' or permanently some of 'B' is 'D' 'A' or 'not D' 'A' at some times of its being 'D' 'A'. And if the condition is time, whether determined or undetermined, then its real contradictory is as known, and its entailment is according to what we detailed in the entailment of the conditional with the condition of the predicate. And if the mode is the mode of possibility, then it is either in the general sense or the special or the more special. If it is in the general sense, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with general possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with general possibility every B is J D A', and it has been known that its entailment when the predicate is stripped is a particular negative with permanence, so the entailment here is either with permanence some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And if the possibility is in the special sense, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with special possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with special possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either necessarily some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or necessarily some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or not or 'D' or not or 'A' or not or 'J' 'D' or not or 'J' 'A' or not or 'D' 'A' or not. And if the possibility is in the more special sense, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are removed, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with the mentioned possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with the more special possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either permanently some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And either with absolute necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And either with temporal necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And if the possibility is the other meaning of the more special, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are removed in the future, as our saying: 'Every B is J D A with conditional possibility', then its real contradictory is 'Not with conditional possibility every B is J D A', and its entailment is either permanently some of 'B' is not 'J' 'D' 'A' or not 'J' or not 'D' or not 'A' or not 'J' 'D' or not 'J' 'A' or not 'D' 'A'. And either with absolute necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And either with temporal necessity some of 'B' is 'J' 'D' 'A' or 'J' or 'D' or 'A' or 'J' 'D' or 'J' 'A' or 'D' 'A'. And whoever encompasses what we have verified regarding the universal affirmative will not find hidden the real contradictory and its entailment in the remaining quantified and particular propositions. And since we have come to what we intended from the verification of the meaning of contradiction, it is necessary to point out that the opposition between a thing and its opposite is more complete, as between justice and injustice, or between it and its contradictory, as between justice and not justice. And the truth is that opposition in the second sense is more complete in terms of assenting to it and judging.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a detailed technical exposition of the real contradictory (al-naqid al-haqiqi) of a universal affirmative modal proposition and the necessary entailments (lawaazim) that follow from that contradictory. The author systematically covers several cases: first, when the proposition has no explicit condition on the predicate (i.e., the predicate is stripped bare), then when the condition is attached to the predicate (shart al-mahmul), then when the condition is time (shart al-waqt), and finally when the mode (jiha) is possibility (imkan) in its various senses: general (amm), special (khass), more special (akhass) with two sub-types (the one where absolute and temporal necessity are removed without future qualification, and the one where they are removed specifically for the future, called imkan mashrut). For each case, the author states the original universal affirmative proposition, its real contradictory, and then lists the possible entailments—which are particular propositions (positive or negative) that follow necessarily from the contradictory. The logical structure is that the contradictory of a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Not every B is A') entails a particular negative (some B is not A), but in modal logic the entailments become more complex because the modality (permanence, necessity, possibility) and the conditions (predicate-condition, time-condition) affect the quantification and tense. The author concludes by noting that from the treatment of universal affirmative, one can infer the contradictory and entailment for other quantified propositions (universal negative, particular affirmative, etc.). He then adds a brief philosophical note on the nature of opposition (ta'aanud): opposition between a thing and its opposite (e.g., justice and injustice) is more complete than opposition between a thing and its contradictory (justice and not-justice) in terms of the strength of certainty in judgment. This is because the opposition between opposites is a stronger form of contrariety, while the contradictory is a weaker logical relation. The passage is highly technical and assumes familiarity with the theory of modal propositions in Aristotelian logic as developed in the Islamic tradition.

الإمكان الخاص
الإمكان الأخص
النقيض الحقيقي
اللازم

The type of possibility in the original proposition determines whether the entailments of its contradictory are permanent or necessary.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
10 min
Contradiction vs. Contrariety: Which Opposition is Stronger?
Author argues contradictory opposition is more complete than contrariety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish contradictory from contrary opposition and explain why contradiction is more fundamental using the essential/accidental distinction.

Core Idea
Contradiction cuts to the essence; contrariety scratches the surface.

In this passage, the author demonstrates a key logical distinction: the opposition between affirmation and negation (contradiction) is more complete and fundamental than the opposition between two positive terms (contrariety). Using the example of 'good' and 'evil', he shows that belief in 'good' and 'not-good' cannot coexist, while belief in 'good' and 'evil' might. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications for logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of opposition
Find where the author mentions tadadd (contrariety) and tanaqud (contradiction). Contrast 'good' vs. 'evil' (contraries) with 'good' vs. 'not good' (contradictories). Note that contradictories are affirmation/negation pairs.
The Arabic terms are تضاد and تناقض.
2
Analyze the impossibility of coexistence
The author argues that contradictory beliefs (e.g., 'it is good' and 'it is not good') cannot be held simultaneously, but contrary beliefs ('it is good' and 'it is evil') may be possible in different respects. Check the text for this reasoning.
This shows contradiction has stricter logical impossibility.
3
Examine the essential vs. accidental distinction
The author distinguishes truth/falsity about the essence (e.g., 'good' is true of the essence of good) from truth/falsity about accidents (e.g., 'not evil' is accidental). Contradictory opposition involves essential properties, making it stronger.
Essential opposition is more fundamental.
4
Recognize the universality of contradiction
The author states that every proposition has a contradictory (affirmation vs. negation), but not every proposition has a contrary. This makes contradiction a universal and thus more complete form of opposition.
This is a structural argument.
5
Conclude with the author's verdict
Synthesize: the author concludes that the opposition of negation and affirmation (salb wa ijab) is more complete (atamm) than contrariety. This conclusion follows from the prior steps.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك اعتقاداً أو لفظاً، والواجب إنما هو النظر في ذلك من جهة الاعتقاد، إذ اللفظ تابع له. إما أولاً: فنقول إذ اعتقدنا في شيء أنه خير وأنه ليس بخير وأنه شر، فلو لم يكن الشر ليس بخير لما استحال اعتقاد أنه خير وشر، ويستحيل اعتقاد أنه خير وأنه ليس بخير. وأيضاً فإن الخير تصدق عليه إيجابات ككونه محموداً مختاراً، ويصدق عليه سلوب ككونه ليس بمذموم ولا مكروه، ويكذب عليه إيجابات ككونه مذموماً ومكروهاً، وسلوب ككونه ليس بمحمود ولا مختار. وليست حقيقة التضاد واقعة بين كل ما لا يجتمع منها كيف اتفق، فإن الواحد إنما يضاده بالحقيقة واحد، فيجب أن يكون الضد منها بالحقيقية ما يعمها، وإنما يعم جميع الإيجابات والسلوب الكاذبة على الخير أنها ليس بخير، فأي إيجاب أو سلب منها صدق عليه أنه ليس بخير كان مبايناً، وأما الأخير فهو مباين لنفسه، وما لا يحتاج في مباينته إلى غيره وذلك الغير لا تباين دونه فهو أقدم، وما هو أقدم في المباينة فمباينته لذاته، والسالبة أقدم من الضد. وأيضاً فإنا إذا قلنا للخير أنه خير صدقنا، وكذا إذا قلنا ليس بشر، لكن الأول يصدق عليه لشيء في ذاته، والثاني بما ليس في ذاته من جهة أن الخير خير لذاته وليس شر عارض من جهة أنه مقابل لأمر ليس في ذاته وهو الشر. وبإزاء هذين الصدقين كذبان: أنه ليس بخير ومقابله أمر في الذات، وكذب أنه شر وهو مقابل لأمر عارض. ولما كان اعتقاد أنه خير صدق في أمر ذاتي، وكان اعتقاد أنه ليس بشر صدق في أمر عرضي، كان اعتقاد ليس بخير كذب في أمر ذاتي مقابل لاعتقاد أنه ليس بشر، والكذب في الأمر الذاتي أشد معاندة من غير الذاتي. وأيضاً فإنا إذا اعتقدنا الخير خير لا نفتقر إلى فهم أنه ليس بشر لكونه عرضياً، والأول ذاتي، فإنا إذا قابلنا العقد الأول بقولنا شر وبقولنا ليس بخير وجدنا أنه شر لا يتم إلا بأنه ليس بخير؟ إذ الكذب المقابل للصدق العرضي لا يتم إلا أن يخطر بالبال الكذب الذاتي. وأيضاً فإن كل قضية فلها مقابل من باب التناقض وهو السلب والإيجاب وليس كل قضية لها ضد مقابل. وأيضاً فإنا إذ اعتقدنا في ما ليس بخير أنه ليس بخير لا يمكننا أن نورد بإزائه عقائد أخرى من الجنس الذي نحن فيه، إلا أن نقول فيه أنه شر، وأنه ليس بشر، وأنه خير، لكن اعتقادنا فيه أنه شر، قد يصدق مع هذا الاعتقاد في كثير من الأمور، فلا يكون معانداً مطلقاً، واعتقادنا فيه أنه ليس بشر، يصدق أيضاً معه في كثير، فبقي أن تكون معاندة أنه خير، وتبيّن أن التعاند السلب والإيجاب أتمّ منه بين الأضداد.
That is either belief or utterance, and the consideration is only from the aspect of belief, since utterance is subordinate to it. To begin: we say, when we believe of something that it is good and that it is not good and that it is evil, if evil were not not-good, it would not be impossible to believe that it is good and evil, and it is impossible to believe that it is good and that it is not good. Also, the good has true affirmations like being praised and chosen, and true negations like being not blameworthy and not hated, and false affirmations like being blameworthy and hated, and false negations like being not praised and not chosen. The reality of contrariety does not occur between everything that cannot coexist haphazardly, for one thing is really opposed by only one contrary. So the real contrary among them must be what encompasses them all. What encompasses all the false affirmations and negations of the good is that it is not good. So any affirmation or negation that is true of it as 'not good' is opposed. But the latter (i.e., 'not good') is opposed to itself. What does not need another in its opposition, and that other does not have opposition without it, is more prior. And what is prior in opposition has opposition by itself. The negation is prior to the contrary. Also, when we say of the good that it is good, we are truthful, and likewise when we say 'it is not evil'. But the first is true of it due to something in its essence, and the second due to what is not in its essence, in that the good is good by itself, and 'not evil' is accidental because it is opposite to something not in its essence, namely evil. Corresponding to these two truths are two falsehoods: 'it is not good' (whose opposite is something in the essence) and the falsehood that 'it is evil' (whose opposite is accidental). Since the belief that it is good is truth in an essential matter, and the belief that it is not evil is truth in an accidental matter, the belief that 'it is not good' is falsehood in an essential matter opposite to the belief that it is not evil, and falsehood in an essential matter is more opposed than falsehood in a non-essential matter. Also, when we believe the good is good, we do not need to understand that it is not evil because it is accidental, while the first is essential. So when we compare the first belief with our statement 'evil' and our statement 'not good', we find that 'evil' is not completed except by 'not good', since falsehood opposite accidental truth is not completed unless essential falsehood comes to mind. Also, every proposition has a counterpart from the perspective of contradiction, namely negation and affirmation, but not every proposition has a contrary counterpart. Also, when we believe of what is not good that it is not good, we cannot bring against it other beliefs from the genus we are in, except to say of it that it is evil, that it is not evil, and that it is good. But our belief that it is evil may be true along with this belief in many matters, so it is not absolutely opposed. And our belief that it is not evil is also true with it in many matters. So it remains that the opposition of being good is [the strongest], and it is shown that the opposition of negation and affirmation is more complete than that between contraries.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis comparing two types of opposition: contradiction (al-tanaqud) and contrariety (al-tadadd). The author argues that the opposition between affirmation and negation (the contradictory) is more fundamental and complete than the opposition between contraries like good and evil. The reasoning proceeds step by step: First, belief in good and not-good is impossible together, but belief in good and evil is not necessarily impossible (as they may coexist in some aspects). Second, the author examines the truth and falsity of various predicates applied to 'good' and shows that the falsehood 'not good' is opposite to the essential truth 'good', whereas 'evil' is opposite to an accidental truth 'not evil'. Essential opposition (based on essence) is stronger than accidental opposition. Third, every proposition has a contradictory (affirmation vs. negation), but not every proposition has a contrary. Finally, when we consider the belief 'not good is not good', the only absolute opposite is the belief 'it is good', whereas 'it is evil' or 'it is not evil' may be compatible in some cases. Thus contradictory opposition (salb wa ijab) is more complete than contrariety (tadad). The author uses the example of 'good' and 'evil' as contraries, and the propositional level of 'is good' versus 'is not good' as contradictories.

تناقض
سلب
إيجاب
معاندة

Simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) of categorical propositions
Author defines simple conversion and illustrates it with universal affirmative converted to particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply simple conversion to any categorical proposition and correctly determine its converse, noting when the converse is universal or particular.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving truth, but universal affirmatives become particular unless terms are coextensive.

In this lesson, we explore simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi), a fundamental immediate inference that allows us to derive a new proposition from a given categorical proposition by swapping subject and predicate. The author defines the rule and provides a logical demonstration. Understanding this operation is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the type of categorical proposition
Determine if it is universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. This passage focuses on universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every B is A').
The quality (affirmation/negation) and quantity (universal/particular) matter for the conversion rule.
2
Swap subject and predicate
Exchange the positions of al-mawḍū' and al-maḥmūl. The original subject becomes the new predicate and vice versa.
The quality remains unchanged: affirmation stays affirmation.
3
Adjust quantity based on predicate breadth
If the original predicate (now subject) is broader than the original subject, the converse becomes a particular affirmative ('Some A is B'). If they are coextensive, the converse remains universal, but this is rare.
The author illustrates with 'Every human is an animal' – the predicate 'animal' is broader, so the converse is 'Some animal is a human'.
4
Understand the supposition demonstration
The author proves the conversion by picking an individual D that is B. Since every B is A, D is also A. Thus some A (namely D) is B, and some B (namely D) is A.
This logical step grounds the inference in a concrete mental representation.
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'. Since 'forbidden' is broader, the converse is 'Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant'. Verify using the supposition method.
This tool is used in fiqh to derive rulings from general principles.

Source Text

الفصل الثامن في تعاكس القضايا الحملية وهو عين العكس المستوي وعكس النقيض العكس المستوي: فأما العكس المستوي، فعبارة عن جعل الموضوع محمولاً والمحمول موضوعاً مع بقاء الكيفية والصدق والكذب بحاله هذا في الحَمْليات، وأما على وجه يعم الحَمْلية والمتصلة، فعبارة عن جعل كل واحد من جزأي القضية المترتبة طبعاً مكان الآخر، واحترزنا بالترتيب الطبيعي عن القضية المنفصلة إذ لا عكس لها، ومهما كان كذلك لزم من صدق الأصل صدق عكسه، وعند هذا فالقضية الحملية إما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فإما كليّة وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة كقولنا: (كل "ب" "أ") وسواء كان المحمول مساوياً للموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان ناطق (وكل ناطق إنسان فليس ذلك إلا باعتبار خصوص المادة ولا يطرد فإنه عسى أن يكون المحمول أعم من الموضوع، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون العكس كلياً كما في قولنا: (كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان مستيقظ فإنه لا ينعكس كل حيوان إنسان ولا كل مستيقظ إنسان فعكسها اللازم لذاتها دائماً جزئي موجب كقولنا: (بعض "أ" "ب") وبيان ذلك بالتعيين والافتراض، وهو أن يقال إذا كان كل "ب" "أ") فلنفرض واحداً من الموصوفات بـ "ب" وليكن مثلاً "د" و"د" موصوف بـ "ب" وهو بعض موصوف بـ "أ" وهو بعض "أ" فبعض ("ب" "أ") وبعض "أ" "ب".
Chapter Eight: On the conversion of categorical propositions, which is identical to simple conversion and contrapositive. Simple conversion: as for simple conversion, it is the making of the subject into the predicate and the predicate into the subject while the quality and truth/falsity remain as they are — this for categoricals. But in a manner general to categoricals and conditionals, it is the making of each of the two parts of the proposition, which are naturally ordered, into the place of the other. And we restricted by 'natural ordering' to exclude the disjunctive proposition, for it has no conversion. And whenever it is thus, the truth of the original necessitates the truth of its converse. And upon this, the categorical proposition is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, as our saying: 'Every B is A' — whether the predicate is coextensive with the subject, as in our saying: 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human' — but that is only by consideration of the particular matter and is not universal; for it may be that the predicate is more general than the subject. And if so, then the converse is not universal, as in our saying: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is awake' — for it does not convert as 'Every animal is a human' nor 'Every awake is a human'. So its converse, necessary for its essence, is always a particular affirmative, as our saying: 'Some A is B'. And the demonstration of that by individuation and supposition is that it is said: If every B is A, let us suppose one of the individuals described by B, let it be for example D. D is described by B — and it is some — and described by A — and it is some A. So some B is A and some A is B.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage comes from the eighth chapter on the conversion (ta'ākus) of categorical propositions, which the author identifies with both simple conversion (al-aks al-mustawi) and contrapositive (aks al-naqīḍ). He first defines simple conversion: it is swapping the subject and predicate while preserving the quality (affirmation/negation) and truth-value. For categorical propositions, this is straightforward; for conditional propositions, conversion involves swapping the two parts, but only if they are naturally ordered. Disjunctive propositions have no conversion because their parts are not naturally ordered. The key rule: if the original is true, its converse is also true. Then he applies this to categorical propositions: they are either affirmative or negative. For affirmative universal propositions (like 'Every B is A'), the converse is not always universal because the predicate may be broader than the subject. For example, 'Every human is an animal' does not convert to 'Every animal is a human'. Instead, the correct converse is always a particular affirmative: 'Some A is B'. He provides a demonstration using a supposition: if every B is A, take a particular D that is B; then D is also A (since all B are A). Hence D is both some B and some A, proving that some A is B. This logical step is the foundation for immediate inferences in syllogistic.

Key Terms
العكس المستوي (al-aks al-mustawi): Simple conversion: swapping subject and predicate while keeping quality and truth-value. [Note: No significant shift — stable across the tradition.]
الطبع (al-tab'): Natural order; the order of the parts in a conditional proposition according to natural priority. [Note: This definition is specific to the author's explanation of conversion; other texts may not mention 'natural order'.]
الكيفية (al-kaifiyya): The quality of the proposition: affirmation (ījāb) or negation (salab). [Note: Standard term across logic texts.]
الموضوع (al-mawḍū'): The subject term in a proposition. [Note: Stable.]
المحمول (al-maḥmūl): The predicate term in a proposition. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the correct simple converse of 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'?
A) Every forbidden thing is an intoxicant
B) Some forbidden thing is an intoxicant
C) No forbidden thing is an intoxicant
D) Some intoxicant is forbidden

Key Takeaway
Simple conversion of a universal affirmative yields a particular affirmative unless the terms are coextensive, preserving truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Simple Conversion and the Universal Affirmative
Defines simple conversion and its application to universal affirmatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform simple conversion on a universal affirmative categorical proposition and explain why the converse is particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Simple conversion swaps subject and predicate; for a universal affirmative, the converse is always particular.

In this passage, the author defines simple conversion and then applies it to the universal affirmative proposition. He warns that while the converse may sometimes appear universal due to coextensive terms, the logically necessary converse is particular. Understanding this rule is essential for correct reasoning in Islamic sciences, where propositions are often transformed in arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation of simple conversion
Notice that conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving both the quality (affirmation/negation) and the truth value. The author gives a general definition covering categorical and conditional propositions but excludes disjunctive ones because their parts are not naturally ordered.
2
Analyze the rule for universal affirmative
The author states that the converse of a universal affirmative is always a particular affirmative. He provides examples: 'Every human is an animal' does not convert to 'Every animal is human' but to 'Some animal is human'.
3
Examine the proof by specification
The author proves the rule by supposing an arbitrary individual D that is B. Since every B is A, D is both B and A, establishing that some B is A and some A is B. This is a standard proof by instantiation.
4
Distinguish general rule from special cases
When the predicate is coextensive with the subject, such as 'Every human is rational', the converse happens to be universal. But this is coincidental and not guaranteed. The logical rule remains that the converse is particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن في تعاكس القضايا الحملية وهو عين العكس المستوي وعكس النقيض العكس المستوي فأما العكس المستوي، فعبارة عن جعل الموضوع محمولاً والمحمول موضوعاً مع بقاء الكيفية والصدق والكذب بحاله هذا في الحَمْليات، وأما على وجه يعم الحَمْلية والمتصلة، فعبارة عن جعل كل واحد من جزأي القضية المترتبة طبعاً مكان الآخر، واحترزنا بالترتيب الطبيعي عن القضية المنفصلة إذ لا عكس لها، ومهما كان كذلك لزم من صدق الأصل صدق عكسه، وعند هذا فالقضية الحملية إما موجبة أو سالبة، فإن كانت موجبة فإما كليّة وإما جزئية، فإن كانت كليّة كقولنا: (كل "ب" "أ") وسواء كان المحمول مساوياً للموضوع كما في قولنا كل إنسان ناطق) (وكل ناطق إنسان فليس ذلك إلا باعتبار خصوص المادة ولا يطرد فإنه عسى أن يكون المحمول أعم من الموضوع، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون العكس كلياً كما في قولنا: (كل) إنسان (حيوان وكل إنسان مستيقظ فإنه لا ينعكس كل حيوان إنسان ولا كل مستيقظ إنسان فعكسها اللازم لذاتها دائماً جزئي موجب كقولنا: (بعض "أ" "ب") وبيان ذلك بالتعيين والافتراض، وهو أن يقال إذا كان كل "ب" "أ") فلنفرض واحداً من الموصوفات بـ "ب" وليكن مثلاً "د" و"د" موصوف بـ "ب" وهو بعض وموصوف بـ "أ" وهو بعض "أ" فبعض ("ب" "أ") وبعض "أ" "ب"
Chapter Eight on the conversion of categorical propositions. It is essentially simple conversion and contraposition. As for simple conversion, it is the act of making the subject predicate and the predicate subject while preserving the quality and truth and falsehood as they were. This is in categoricals. And as for a general definition covering categorical and conditional, it is the act of putting each of the two parts of the proposition that are naturally ordered in the place of the other. We stipulated 'natural order' to exclude the disjunctive proposition, for it has no conversion. And whenever it is like that, it follows from the truth of the original the truth of its converse. And at this point, the categorical proposition is either affirmative or negative. If it is affirmative, then either universal or particular. If it is universal, like our saying: 'Every B is A.' And whether the predicate is coextensive with the subject, as in our saying 'Every human is rational' and 'Every rational is human' – that is only by consideration of the particular matter and is not general, for it may be that the predicate is more general than the subject, and then the converse would not be universal, as in our saying 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every human is awake' – for neither 'Every animal is human' nor 'Every awake human' follows. So its converse that is necessarily entailed by its essence is always particular affirmative, like our saying: 'Some A is B.' And the proof of that is by specification and supposition: It is said: If every B is A, then let us suppose one of the things described by B, let it be for example D. D is described by B – that is some – and is described by A – that is some A. So some (B is A) and some A is B.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage defines the logical operation of simple conversion ('aks mustawi) and applies it to universal affirmative categorical propositions. The author begins by stating that this chapter is about the conversion of categorical propositions, which includes simple conversion and contraposition (عكس النقيض). He then defines simple conversion: exchanging the subject and predicate while keeping the quality (affirmation/negation) and truth value unchanged, for categorical propositions. He also gives a more general definition covering conditional propositions, but notes that disjunctive propositions have no conversion because their parts are not naturally ordered (the order is not hierarchical). He asserts that if the original proposition is true, then its converse is also true. Then he proceeds to categorical propositions, dividing them into affirmative and negative, and then affirmative into universal and particular. For universal affirmatives, he gives the rule: the converse is always a particular affirmative, because the predicate may be broader than the subject. He illustrates with coextensive terms (human and rational) where the converse is also universal, but notes that this is coincidental (bi i'tibar khusus al-madda) and not generally true. The correct general rule is that the converse of 'Every B is A' is 'Some A is B'. He then provides a proof using a hypothetical individual D: if every B is A, then pick any B, call it D; D is B (so some B exists) and D is A (so some A exists that is B), hence some A is B. This is a standard proof by instantiation. The passage is foundational for understanding immediate inference in Aristotelian logic as adopted by Muslim logicians.

الحملية
الكلي الموجب

Modal Conversion of Universal Affirmatives & Refuting the Necessity Claim
Author articulates modal conversion rule and refutes a counter-claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct modal converse of any universal affirmative proposition in Islamic logic and refute the claim that it must preserve necessity.

Core Idea
The converse of a universal affirmative is always in absolute possibility, not necessarily necessary.

In the previous lesson, we learned the simple conversion of universal affirmatives to particular affirmatives. Now we introduce modality: what happens when the original proposition is necessary or possible? The author presents the rule that the converse of any universal affirmative is always in absolute possibility (al-mumkin al-amm), regardless of the original's modality. He then refutes a common mistake: thinking that the converse of a necessary proposition must itself be necessary. Understanding this rule is essential for correct reasoning with modal propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its modality
Determine whether the universal affirmative is necessary (e.g., 'Every human is rational') or possible (e.g., 'Every human is a writer'). Note the modal term: ضروري or ممكن.
2
Apply the conversion rule for universal affirmatives
The simple conversion of 'Every B is A' (modalized or not) yields 'Some A is B'. According to the author, the converse is always in the mode of absolute possibility (الممكن العام). This means it is not necessarily true, but could be true.
3
Examine the four modal combinations given by the author
The author provides four examples showing how the modality of the converse depends on the specific matter (خصوص المادة), not on the original's modality. Work through each example to see that the converse is always possible, never necessarily necessary or necessarily possible.
4
Analyze the mistaken argument for necessity
The mistaken claim: If 'Every B is A necessarily', then its converse 'Some A is B' must also be necessary. Their reasoning uses a reductio: if not necessary, then some A is not necessarily B, contradicting the original? The author shows this is invalid: stripping necessity from B-to-A does not strip necessity from A-to-B.
Use the writer-human example: 'Every writer is human' is necessary, but human is not necessary for writer; it's possible.
5
Generalize the principle
The author concludes that just as the converse of a necessary is not necessary, the converse of a non-necessary is not non-necessary. The modality of the converse is only determined by the specific matter (coextensiveness of terms).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان المحمول عند كونه ضرورياً للموضوع لا يمنع من كون حال الموضوع بالنسبة إلى المحمول تارة بالضرورة وأخرى بالإمكان، ولم يكن أحدهما متعيناً، والعام لهما جميعاً وهو الممكن العام كان عكس الكلي الموجب مطلقاً وموجهاً جزئياً موجباً بالإمكان العام، ولئن تشوّقت إلى إيضاح ذلك بالمثال فمثال ما حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع الضرورة، وكذا حال الموضوع بالنسبة إلى المحمول قولنا: كل إنسان ناطق فإن الناطق ضروري للإنسان كذا بالعكس، ومثال ما حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع الضرورة، وحال الموضوع بالنسبة إلى المحمول الإمكان قولنا : (كل) كاتب (إنسان فإن الإنسان ضروري للكاتب، وليس الكاتب ضرورياً للإنسان بل ممكن، ومثال ما حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع ،بالإمكان، وكذا حال الموضوع بالنسبة إلى المحمول فقولنا: (كل) ضاحك (كاتب فإن كل واحد منهما ممكن للآخر وليس بضروري، ومثال ما حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع الإمكان وحال الموضوع بالنسبة إلى المحمول الضرورة قولنا: (كل إنسان (كاتب فإن الكاتب ممكن للإنسان، وإن كان الإنسان ضروريا للكاتب فلا تظن تعين جهة ما في العكس إلا باعتبار خصوص المادة، كما قد يخيل بعض المقصرين عن هذا البيان إن عكس الضروري الموجب الكلي يجب أن يكون ضرورياً، معتمداً في ذلك على أنه إذا لم يكن عكس (كل "ب" "أ" بالضرورة) بالضرورة بعض "أ" "ب" فبعض "أ" ليس بالضرورة "ب" وكان كل "ب" "أ" بالضرورة)، ولم يعلم أن سلب الضرورة عن نسبة "ب" إلى بعض "أ" مما لا يوجب سلبها عن نسبة "أ" إلى بعض "ب" كما أوضحناه من نسبة الكاتب إلى الإنسان، فإنه لما سلبت الضرورة عن نسبة الكاتب إلى الإنسان لم يلزم سلبها عن نسبة الإنسان إلى الكاتب في البيان إنما يتم بذلك، كيف وأنه إذا لم يكن لزوم الضرورة بيناً كيف يكون لزوم لا ضرورة بيناً؟ ثم وإن استمر ذلك في الضروري فهلا قيل مثله في غير الضروري أن عكسه غير ضروري، وعلى ما أوضحناه فتعلم أنه كما لا يجب عكس الضروري إن عكس عن الضروري فلا يجب عكس غير الضروري عنه. ولا عكس الممكن عن الممكن ولا غير الممكن عن الممكن، لكنه ربما عقب ما ذكرناه من مثال الكاتب والإنسان بأن قيل إن نسبة الإنسان إلى الكاتب بالإمكان كما أن نسبة الكاتب إلى الإنسان بالإمكان، وذلك لأن معنى قولنا: الكاتب إنسان بالضرورة أي ما هو موصوف بالكتابة بالفعل عيناً أو عقلاً لا ما دام كاتباً، ولا من
And since the predicate, when it is necessary for the subject, does not prevent the state of the subject relative to the predicate from being sometimes necessary and sometimes possible, and neither is determined, and the common factor for both is absolute possibility (al-mumkin al-amm), therefore the converse of the universal affirmative, both absolute and modal, is a particular affirmative in absolute possibility. And if you wish clarification by example: an example where the predicate relative to the subject is necessary, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate is necessary, is our saying: 'Every human is rational,' for rational is necessary for human, and likewise vice versa. And an example where the predicate relative to the subject is necessary, but the state of the subject relative to the predicate is possible, is our saying: 'Every writer is human,' for human is necessary for the writer, but the writer is not necessary for human; rather it is possible. And an example where the predicate relative to the subject is possible, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate is possible, is our saying: 'Every laughing being is a writer,' for each one of them is possible for the other and not necessary. And an example where the predicate relative to the subject is possible, and the state of the subject relative to the predicate is necessary, is our saying: 'Every human is a writer,' for the writer is possible for human, even though human is necessary for the writer. So do not think that a specific modality is determined in the conversion except by consideration of the particular matter, as some inadequate ones may imagine that the converse of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary, relying on the argument that if the converse of 'Every B is A necessarily' is not 'Some A is B necessarily', then some A is not necessarily B, and yet every B is A necessarily. And they did not know that stripping necessity from the relation of B to some A does not necessitate stripping it from the relation of A to some B, as we have explained in the relation of writer to human: for when necessity was stripped from the relation of writer to human, it did not necessitate stripping it from the relation of human to writer in the explanation; it is only achieved by that. Moreover, if the entailment of necessity is not evident, how can the entailment of non-necessity be evident? Then if that holds for the necessary, why not say the same for the non-necessary, that its converse is non-necessary? And according to our explanation, you learn that just as it is not necessary that the converse of a necessary be necessary, so it is not necessary that the converse of a non-necessary be non-necessary. Nor is the converse of the possible from the possible nor the non-possible from the possible. But perhaps after our example of writer and human it might be said that the relation of human to writer is possible just as the relation of writer to human is possible, because the meaning of our saying 'The writer is human necessarily' means that which is actually described by writing, essentially or intellectually, not as long as he is a writer. And not from...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of simple conversion for universal affirmative propositions, focusing on modal conversions (where necessity or possibility is attached). The author argues that the converse of any universal affirmative, whether unmodalized or modalized, is a particular affirmative in the mode of absolute possibility (al-mumkin al-amm). This is because the predicate may be necessary for the subject, but the subject may be only possibly related to the predicate (as in 'every writer is human': human is necessary for writer, but writer is not necessary for human). The author gives four modal combinations: (1) both relations necessary (human and rational), (2) predicate necessary, subject possible (writer and human), (3) both possible (laughing being and writer), (4) predicate possible, subject necessary (human and writer). The crucial point is that the converse does not preserve necessity automatically. The author then refutes a mistaken view held by 'some inadequate ones' who think that the converse of a necessary universal must also be necessary. Their reasoning is: if the converse of 'every B is A necessarily' is not 'some A is B necessarily', then some A is not necessarily B, which contradicts the original? Actually the author explains their mistake: stripping necessity from B to some A does not entail stripping necessity from A to some B. He uses the writer-human example to show that the converse is only in absolute possibility. He further argues that if the necessity is not entailed in the conversion, then the absence of necessity is also not entailed. The passage ends with a subtle point about the meaning of necessity: saying 'the writer is human necessarily' means that the subject is essentially human as long as it is actually a writer, not merely at the time of writing. This is a sophisticated discussion of modal logic in the Islamic tradition, correcting a common error.

الضروري
خصوص المادة

Modality in Conversion: The Four Combinations
Author explains conversion rules for necessary and possible propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the modality of the converse of a modal categorical proposition based on the subject-predicate modal relations.

Core Idea
The modality of a converse depends on the specific terms, not the original modality.

In this passage, the author presents four modal categorical propositions illustrating different combinations of necessity and possibility between subject and predicate. He then warns that the modality of the converse is not fixed but depends on the specific matter (madda). This lesson will guide you through these examples and help you understand how to determine the correct converse modality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four examples
In the source text, locate the four examples given by the author. They are: (1) 'Every human is a speaker' – both necessary; (2) 'Every writer is a human' – predicate necessary to subject, subject possible to predicate; (3) 'Every laughing thing is a writer' – both possible; (4) 'Every human is a writer' – predicate possible to subject, subject necessary to predicate.
Notice that the author uses the terms 'ضرورة' and 'إمكان' to mark modality.
2
For each example, note the modal relation from subject to predicate and from predicate to subject
Write down the modality of the original proposition (e.g., from subject S to predicate P) and also the modality of the converse relation (from P to S) as given in the text. For instance, in the second example: from writer to human is necessary; from human to writer is possible.
The author explicitly states these relations.
3
Determine the modality of the converse proposition
The converse proposition is formed by swapping subject and predicate. Using the modal relations you noted, decide what modality the converse should have. For example, from 'Every writer is human necessarily', the converse is 'Some human is writer possibly' because the relation from human to writer is possible.
The converse is always a particular affirmative by general possibility, but its modality may be necessary or possible. The author says the converse of a particular modal is also particular by general possibility.
4
Generalize the rule
Based on the examples, state when the converse preserves necessity and when it becomes possible. The author emphasizes that there is no fixed rule; it depends on the specific terms.
Contrast with the mistaken view that the converse of a necessary proposition must be necessary.

Concerning the subject [having] necessity, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate: our saying 'Every human is a speaker' – then 'speaker' is necessary for a human, likewise conversely. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is necessity, and the state of the subject relative to the predicate is possibility: our saying 'Every writer is a human' – then 'human' is necessary for the writer, and 'writer' is not necessary for the human but possible. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is possibility, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate: our saying 'Every laughing thing is a writer' – then each of them is possible for the other and not necessary. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is possibility and the state of the subject relative to the predicate is necessity: our saying 'Every human is a writer' – then 'writer' is possible for the human, even though 'human' is necessary for the writer. So do not think that a particular modality is fixed in the conversion except by considering the specific matter, as some shortsighted ones might imagine regarding this explanation that the conversion of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary, relying on the fact that if the conversion of 'Every B is A necessarily' is not 'Some A is B necessarily', then 'Some A is not necessarily B' and 'Every B is A necessarily'. And he did not know that negating necessity from the relation of B to some A does not entail negating it from the relation of A to some B, as we clarified from the relation of writer to human. For when necessity was negated from the relation of writer to human, it did not necessitate its negation from the relation of human to writer in the explanation – it only follows that. How so? And if the necessity of implication is not evident, how is the necessity of non-implication evident? Then if that persists in the necessary, why not say similarly in the non-necessary that its converse is non-necessary? And according to what we clarified, you learn that just as it is not necessary that the conversion of the necessary be necessary, so it is not necessary that the conversion of the non-necessary be non-necessary. Nor is the conversion of the possible from the possible, nor the non-possible from the possible. But perhaps after our example of writer and human it might be said that the relation of human to writer is possible just as the relation of writer to human is possible. That is because the meaning of our saying 'The writer is human necessarily' is that which is described by writing actually, essentially or intellectually, not as long as he is a writer, nor insofar as he is a writer, but whether writing exists for him or not, and if it exists, whether it is necessary for him or not. So then the thing described by writing is necessarily human, not the writer insofar as he is a writer. So if what necessitates necessity is not the writer insofar as he is a writer, then it is not necessary that the writer be human. So some writer insofar as he is a writer is possibly human, not necessarily human. And the truth is that even if it is conceded that the writer insofar as he is a writer requires by his essence that he be – with the possibility of dispute in it – we do not mean by our saying 'The writer is necessarily human' from the aspect of his being a writer, but rather that thing described as being a writer is necessarily human, and there is no doubt about it. And it is not hidden that the human, i.e., the thing described as being human, is not necessarily a writer, even if it is established that the supposed conversion is shown by the contradiction [method]. I said: If it is not by general possibility that some A is B, then necessarily no A is B, and its conversion is necessarily no B is A, as you know, and 'Every B is A' is not true with it, whether that is absolute or modal in every respect. And even if we have not yet explained the syllogism by contradiction, it does not harm its use here, for its validity is known to [sound] nature, and where the First Teacher [Aristotle] explained it to us in his books, that was not by way of teaching and making understood, but by way of reminding and alerting. As for if the modal proposition is particular, like our saying 'Some B is A', whether it is also absolute or modal, its conversion is like itself, particular affirmative by general possibility. And your method in the explanation is what your method was in the universal affirmative other than the contradiction [method], for it is only completed by conversion of the universal negative, and the conversion of the universal negative is not completed without conversion of the particular affirmative, as we will explain – and that is a circularity. As for if the proposition is negative, it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, it is either necessary or non-necessary. If it is necessary, like our saying 'Necessarily no B is A', then its conversion...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conversion ('aks) of modal categorical propositions. The author begins by giving four examples illustrating different combinations of modality between subject and predicate: (1) 'Every human is a speaker' – both predicate necessary to subject and subject necessary to predicate; (2) 'Every writer is a human' – predicate necessary to subject but subject possible to predicate; (3) 'Every laughing thing is a writer' – both possible; (4) 'Every human is a writer' – predicate possible to subject but subject necessary to predicate. The key point is that the modality of the converted proposition depends on the specific terms (madda) not merely on the modality of the original. The author then criticizes a mistaken view that the conversion of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary. He refutes this by using the example of 'writer' and 'human': it is necessary that every writer is human, but not necessary that every human is writer; the necessity attaches to the subject as a thing described by writing, not to the writer insofar as he is writer. Thus, negating necessity from one relation does not entail negating it from the converse. The author then explains the use of reduction by contradiction (khulf) to demonstrate conversions, noting that its validity is known naturally and Aristotle only reminded of it. He then discusses conversion of particular modal propositions, which yield a particular affirmative by general possibility. Finally, he begins the discussion of negative propositions, but the text cuts off. The author's approach is to clarify that conversion rules are not mechanical but depend on the actual relation of the terms. A fiqh analogy: if we say 'Every intoxicant is haram necessarily', its converse 'Some haram is intoxicant' is not necessarily true because something can be haram for other reasons.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct converse of 'Every writer is a human necessarily'?
A) Every human is a writer necessarily
B) Some human is a writer necessarily
C) Some human is a writer possibly
D) No human is a writer necessarily

Key Takeaway
The modality of a converse is not determined by the modality of the original alone; it depends on the actual relation between the terms in the converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the four examples given by the author, write down the converse proposition and its modality. Then explain your reasoning based on the modal relations provided in the text.
?
Given the proposition 'Every human is capable of speech (kalam) necessarily' (assuming both directions are necessary), state its converse and its modality. Justify.

Immediate Inference
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Mistaken View: Conversion of Necessary Universal Affirmatives
Author refutes the view that the converse of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary, using the writer-human example and khulf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the claim that the converse of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary, using the author's argument and the reduction by contradiction (khulf).

Core Idea
The necessity of the converse does not follow from the necessity of the original; it depends on the terms.

In this passage, the author directly addresses a mistaken view held by some: that the conversion of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary. He uses the example of 'writer' and 'human' to show that this is false. He also employs the method of reduction by contradiction (khulf) to demonstrate the correct conversion. This lesson will guide you through his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
In the source text, locate the claim: 'عكس الضروري الموجب الكلي يجب أن يكون ضرورياً'. This is the view that the converse of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary.
2
Find the author's counterexample
The author provides the example of 'Every writer is a human necessarily'. Its converse is not 'Some human is writer necessarily' but 'Some human is writer possibly', because not every human is a writer.
This shows that the opposite of the claim is true.
3
Understand the author's explanation of necessity
The author explains that when we say 'The writer is human necessarily', we mean the thing described as writer is necessarily human, not the writer insofar as he is a writer. So the necessity is about the subject's essence, not the description. Thus, the converse does not inherit the necessity.
This is a key subtlety: the modality attaches to the subject's essence, not the accident of being a writer.
4
See the use of khulf (reduction by contradiction)
The author shows that to prove the converse, one can assume the opposite and derive a contradiction. For example, if it were not possible that some human is writer, then necessarily no human is writer, which contradicts the original that every writer is human. This validates the converse.
The author notes that khulf is naturally valid and Aristotle only reminded of it.
5
Generalize the lesson
The author concludes that just as it is not necessary for the necessary, it is also not necessary for the non-necessary to have a non-necessary converse. There is no fixed rule.
The modality of the converse must be evaluated case by case.

Concerning the subject [having] necessity, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate: our saying 'Every human is a speaker' – then 'speaker' is necessary for a human, likewise conversely. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is necessity, and the state of the subject relative to the predicate is possibility: our saying 'Every writer is a human' – then 'human' is necessary for the writer, and 'writer' is not necessary for the human but possible. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is possibility, and likewise the state of the subject relative to the predicate: our saying 'Every laughing thing is a writer' – then each of them is possible for the other and not necessary. An example where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is possibility and the state of the subject relative to the predicate is necessity: our saying 'Every human is a writer' – then 'writer' is possible for the human, even though 'human' is necessary for the writer. So do not think that a particular modality is fixed in the conversion except by considering the specific matter, as some shortsighted ones might imagine regarding this explanation that the conversion of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary, relying on the fact that if the conversion of 'Every B is A necessarily' is not 'Some A is B necessarily', then 'Some A is not necessarily B' and 'Every B is A necessarily'. And he did not know that negating necessity from the relation of B to some A does not entail negating it from the relation of A to some B, as we clarified from the relation of writer to human. For when necessity was negated from the relation of writer to human, it did not necessitate its negation from the relation of human to writer in the explanation – it only follows that. How so? And if the necessity of implication is not evident, how is the necessity of non-implication evident? Then if that persists in the necessary, why not say similarly in the non-necessary that its converse is non-necessary? And according to what we clarified, you learn that just as it is not necessary that the conversion of the necessary be necessary, so it is not necessary that the conversion of the non-necessary be non-necessary. Nor is the conversion of the possible from the possible, nor the non-possible from the possible. But perhaps after our example of writer and human it might be said that the relation of human to writer is possible just as the relation of writer to human is possible. That is because the meaning of our saying 'The writer is human necessarily' is that which is described by writing actually, essentially or intellectually, not as long as he is a writer, nor insofar as he is a writer, but whether writing exists for him or not, and if it exists, whether it is necessary for him or not. So then the thing described by writing is necessarily human, not the writer insofar as he is a writer. So if what necessitates necessity is not the writer insofar as he is a writer, then it is not necessary that the writer be human. So some writer insofar as he is a writer is possibly human, not necessarily human. And the truth is that even if it is conceded that the writer insofar as he is a writer requires by his essence that he be – with the possibility of dispute in it – we do not mean by our saying 'The writer is necessarily human' from the aspect of his being a writer, but rather that thing described as being a writer is necessarily human, and there is no doubt about it. And it is not hidden that the human, i.e., the thing described as being human, is not necessarily a writer, even if it is established that the supposed conversion is shown by the contradiction [method]. I said: If it is not by general possibility that some A is B, then necessarily no A is B, and its conversion is necessarily no B is A, as you know, and 'Every B is A' is not true with it, whether that is absolute or modal in every respect. And even if we have not yet explained the syllogism by contradiction, it does not harm its use here, for its validity is known to [sound] nature, and where the First Teacher [Aristotle] explained it to us in his books, that was not by way of teaching and making understood, but by way of reminding and alerting. As for if the modal proposition is particular, like our saying 'Some B is A', whether it is also absolute or modal, its conversion is like itself, particular affirmative by general possibility. And your method in the explanation is what your method was in the universal affirmative other than the contradiction [method], for it is only completed by conversion of the universal negative, and the conversion of the universal negative is not completed without conversion of the particular affirmative, as we will explain – and that is a circularity. As for if the proposition is negative, it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, it is either necessary or non-necessary. If it is necessary, like our saying 'Necessarily no B is A', then its conversion...
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conversion ('aks) of modal categorical propositions. The author begins by giving four examples illustrating different combinations of modality between subject and predicate: (1) 'Every human is a speaker' – both predicate necessary to subject and subject necessary to predicate; (2) 'Every writer is a human' – predicate necessary to subject but subject possible to predicate; (3) 'Every laughing thing is a writer' – both possible; (4) 'Every human is a writer' – predicate possible to subject but subject necessary to predicate. The key point is that the modality of the converted proposition depends on the specific terms (madda) not merely on the modality of the original. The author then criticizes a mistaken view that the conversion of a necessary universal affirmative must be necessary. He refutes this by using the example of 'writer' and 'human': it is necessary that every writer is human, but not necessary that every human is writer; the necessity attaches to the subject as a thing described by writing, not to the writer insofar as he is writer. Thus, negating necessity from one relation does not entail negating it from the converse. The author then explains the use of reduction by contradiction (khulf) to demonstrate conversions, noting that its validity is known naturally and Aristotle only reminded of it. He then discusses conversion of particular modal propositions, which yield a particular affirmative by general possibility. Finally, he begins the discussion of negative propositions, but the text cuts off. The author's approach is to clarify that conversion rules are not mechanical but depend on the actual relation of the terms. A fiqh analogy: if we say 'Every intoxicant is haram necessarily', its converse 'Some haram is intoxicant' is not necessarily true because something can be haram for other reasons.

Key Terms
عكس
ضرورة
إمكان
خلف
