The converse of a necessary proposition is not necessarily necessary; it depends on the terms, as shown by the writer-human example and proven by khulf.

Proving Conversion of Universal Affirmative by Reductio
Author proves conversion of universal affirmative by reductio

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the conversion of a universal affirmative proposition (Every B is A to Some A is B) using reductio ad absurdum (qiyās al-khalf).

Core Idea
Reductio converts 'Every B is A' to 'Some A is B' by showing its denial leads to contradiction.

This passage demonstrates a foundational proof in immediate inference: converting a universal affirmative. The author does not simply state the rule; he proves it by reductio, assuming the opposite and deriving a contradiction. This method, known as qiyās al-khalf, is central to Islamic logic and is used across sciences to validate inferences. Understanding this proof equips you to construct and evaluate similar arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and desired converse
The original is 'Every B is A' (universal affirmative). The goal is to prove 'Some A is B' (particular affirmative). Note the modal nuance: the author uses 'by general possibility' (bi-al-imkān al-ʿāmm) to express the conclusion as possible, not assertoric.
In the passage, the original is not explicitly stated but is the premise; the conversion is about 'Every B is A' implying 'Some A is B'.
2
Assume the negation of the desired conclusion
Assume 'It is not the case that by general possibility some A is B'. This is equivalent to 'Necessarily no A is B' (since if not possibly some, then necessarily none). This sets up the reductio.
The author says: 'If it is not the case that by general possibility some A is B, then necessarily no A is B'.
3
Apply the conversion rule for the universal negative
From 'Necessarily no A is B', convert to 'Necessarily no B is A' using the already-accepted conversion of the universal negative (E-conversion). This is a standard immediate inference.
The author writes: 'the converse of necessarily no A is B is necessarily no B is A'.
4
Derive a contradiction with the original premise
The original premise is 'Every B is A' (or its modal equivalent). But we have derived 'Necessarily no B is A'. These cannot both be true (they contradict each other under the same modality). Therefore, the assumption (step 2) is false.
The author says: 'and with it every B is A is not true' meaning the conjunction is impossible.
5
Conclude the conversion holds
Since the denial of the converted proposition leads to a contradiction, the converted proposition must be true: 'By general possibility, some A is B'. This completes the proof that 'Every B is A' implies 'Some A is B'.
The author does not state the final conclusion explicitly, but the logical structure implies it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت: إن لم يكن بالإمكان العام بعض "أ" "ب"، فبالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وعكس بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب" على ما تعرفه بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ) ولا يصدق معه كل "ب" "أ" كان ذلك مطلقاً أو موجهاً بكل اعتبار. "أ") ونحن وإن لم نبين بعد قياس الخلف فليس بقادح في استعماله هاهنا فإن صحته معلومة للطباع، وحيث بينه المعلم الأول لنا في كتبه لم يكن ذلك على سبيل التعليم والتفهيم بل على سبيل التذكير والتنبيه.
I say: If it is not the case that by general possibility some 'A' is 'B', then necessarily no 'A' is 'B', and the converse of 'necessarily no A is B' according to what you know is 'necessarily no B is A', and with it 'every B is A' is not true, whether that is absolute or modal in every respect. And even if we have not yet explained the syllogism of reductio, that is not a flaw in using it here, for its validity is known to human nature, and when the First Teacher explained it to us in his books, that was not by way of teaching and making understand, but by way of reminding and alerting.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe demonstrating the conversion of a universal affirmative proposition via reductio ad absurdum (qiyās al-khalf). The author starts by negating the converted form: if it is not possible that some A is B (i.e., no A is B), then the universal negative holds. Then he converts that universal negative (to no B is A) using the already accepted conversion rule for universal negatives. But since the original premise was 'Every B is A' (universal affirmative), this results in a contradiction: both no B is A and every B is A cannot be true simultaneously. Hence the original assumption that no A is B is false, so it is possible that some A is B. The author notes that the validity of reductio is innate and does not require prior proof; the First Teacher (Aristotle) only reminded rather than taught it. In Islamic logic, this method is analogous to establishing that if nabidh were not intoxicating, it would not be forbidden, but since it is forbidden (by analogy to khamr), it must be intoxicating.

Key Terms
بالإمكان العام (bi-al-imkān al-ʿāmm): general possibility
بالضرورة (bi-al-ḍarūrah): necessarily
القياس الخلف (al-qiyās al-khalf): reductio ad absurdum

Conversion of Particular Affirmative and Circularity
Author states that particular affirmative converts to itself and notes circularity with universal negative conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conversion pattern of a particular affirmative modal proposition and explain why its proof involves circularity with the universal negative conversion.

Core Idea
Particular affirmative converts to itself, but its proof is circular with universal negative conversion.

In this passage, the author examines how modalized particular affirmative propositions like 'Some B is A' convert. He states that the converse is identical in quantity and quality: a particular affirmative. However, he warns that proving this conversion leads to a circularity when relying on the conversion of the universal negative, which in turn needs the particular affirmative conversion. This observation shows that some immediate inferences must be taken as basic or justified by other means.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
The proposition 'Some B is A' is a particular affirmative, possibly modalized. Recognize it as asserting that at least one B is A.
2
State the conversion
Its converse is 'Some A is B', also a particular affirmative. The author says it is 'like itself' — same quantity and quality, with general possibility.
Conversion preserves truth for particular affirmatives.
3
Examine the proof requirement
The author notes that proving this conversion requires the conversion of the universal negative. Think: why might a universal negative be needed?
In classical logic, universal negative conversion is often used in proofs by contraposition.
4
Notice the circularity
The conversion of the universal negative itself depends on the conversion of the particular affirmative, creating a circular dependency. No non-circular proof is possible.
This is the dawr mentioned. It means the two conversions cannot be proven without each other.
5
Understand the implication
This shows that some immediate inferences must be accepted as primitive axioms. In Islamic logic, conversion rules are often taken as self-evident or demonstrated by reductio ad absurdum.
Reductio is mentioned as an alternative method.

Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise that explains the conversion of a particular affirmative proposition. The author states that 'Some B is A' converts to 'Some A is B' (same quantity and quality) with general possibility modality. However, to prove this conversion, one would need to use the conversion of a universal negative, but that in turn requires the conversion of the particular affirmative. This creates a circular argument (dawr). The author notes that non-reductio proof is impossible without circularity. In Islamic logic, this is an important observation: immediate inferences must rely on self-evident or assumed rules. For example, converting 'Some scholar is knowledgeable' to 'Some knowledgeable person is a scholar' seems intuitive but its formal proof involves a circle.

Key Terms

دور (dawr) - circularity in proof

Conversion of Universal Negative Necessary: Reductio and Critique
Author proves conversion of universal negative necessary using reductio and critiques an alternative proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate the conversion of universal negative necessary propositions using reductio and identify the circularity in the alternative 'mubāyin' proof.

Core Idea
Necessary universal negatives convert by reductio; the 'mubāyin' proof fails due to equivocation.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that a universal negative necessary proposition ('Necessarily no B is A') converts to another universal negative necessary ('Necessarily no A is B') using a reductio argument. He then critiques a popular alternative proof that relies on the term 'mubāyin' (separate), exposing it as circular because 'mubāyin' is a homonym. Understanding this distinction safeguards against flawed inferences in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition and its converse
The universal negative necessary proposition is 'Necessarily no B is A'. Its converse should be 'Necessarily no A is B'. Note the quantity and modality remain the same.
Conversion preserves truth in necessary negatives.
2
Follow the reductio proof
Assume the converse is false: it is not the case that necessarily no A is B. That implies it is possible (in the general sense) that some A is B. But this contradicts the original necessity that no B is A, because if some A could be B, then some B could be A. The contradiction shows the converse must be true.
The reductio uses the relationship between necessity and possibility.
3
Understand the alternative 'mubāyin' proof
Some proponents argue: 'If no B is A, then B is separate (mubāyin) from A, and if B is separate from A, then A is separate from B, so no A is B.'
This seems intuitive but the author will critique it.
4
Critique the alternative using the ambiguity of 'mubāyin'
The author points out that 'mubāyin' is a homonym. It can mean separate in definition, place, or time, or simply 'not being the other'. The argument assumes that being separate is self-evident, but it actually defines itself: 'no A is B because it is separate from B' is circular. The conclusion is not evident, so the proof fails.
Circular reasoning and equivocation are fallacies in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for the negative proposition, it is either universal or particular. If it is universal, then either necessary or non-necessary. If it is necessary, like our saying: 'Necessarily no B is A', then its converse is like itself in quantity and modality, i.e., a universal negative necessary: 'Necessarily no A is B'. Otherwise it would be possible in the general sense that some B is A, while 'Necessarily no B is A' is true, which would entail 'Some A is necessarily not A' – a contradiction. Also, it has been shown that the conversion of the particular affirmative, whatever its modality or absoluteness, is a particular affirmative with general possibility. So (it would be possible that some B is A by general possibility) while it was said 'no B is A by necessity' – a contradiction. And perhaps another explanation is followed in the well-known [works]: that if (if B is separate from A, and A is separate from B, then no A is B. But this is mistaken, for 'separate' is a homonym that can mean separate in definition, place, time, etc., and separate in the sense that it is not what is said to be separate from it. The intended meaning of 'separate' here is not other than that. So if it is said 'no B is A', then no A is B because it is separate from B – this defines itself, since there is no meaning to our saying it is separate except that it is not B and none of it. If its being separate from B is evident, then 'no A is B' would be evident, needing no explanation, but 'no A is B' is not evident but needs explanation. So A's being separate from B is not evident but needs explanation.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe that first proves the conversion of universal negative necessary propositions via reductio, then critiques an alternative proof using the concept of 'mubāyin' (separate). The author argues: if 'Necessarily no B is A' is true, but its converse 'Necessarily no A is B' were false, then it would be possible for some B to be A. That would conflict with the original necessity. Also, the conversion of particular affirmative (which would result if the converse were false) contradicts the universal negative. The author then criticizes a popular proof that says if B is separate from A, then A is separate from B, so no A is B. He points out that 'separate' is equivocal; the argument is circular because it assumes what it tries to prove. In Islamic logic, this distinction matters for necessary negative conversions, e.g., 'No animal is a stone' necessarily converts to 'No stone is an animal'.

Conversion of Durative Universal Negative Propositions
Author expands on conversion rules for absolute universal negatives with durative condition, distinguishing constant vs non-constant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a universal negative proposition with a durative condition (mā dāma) converts validly, by distinguishing constant from non-constant conditions.

Core Idea
A durative universal negative converts only if the judgment holds constantly for the whole duration.

In immediate inference, conversion is a standard operation, but when propositions include a durative condition like 'as long as' (mā dāma), additional nuance applies. This lesson explores the author's expansion on the conditions under which such propositions convert, focusing on the distinction between constant (dā'ima) and non-constant judgments. You will learn to identify when the converse of a durative universal negative is valid and when it is not, using examples from the natural world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the durative universal negative
Locate the proposition with the form 'No B is A as long as B' (lā shay'a min B A mā dāma B). This is your source material for conversion.
The condition 'as long as' restricts the judgment to the duration of the subject's attribute.
2
Determine if the condition is constant (dā'ima)
Check whether the judgment holds perpetually for the entire duration of the subject's attribute, or only for some portion. A constant condition means 'No B is A' is true at every moment B exists.
The author uses 'dā'ima' to mean perpetual or constant.
3
Apply conversion for constant condition
If the condition is constant, the proposition converts validly: 'No A is B as long as B' is true. The converse preserves the truth. The author provides a reductio proof.
Also note the alternative: 'otherwise some A is B in some times of its being B'.
4
Recognize non-constant condition blocks conversion
If the condition is not constant (ghayr dā'ima), the proposition does not convert. For example, 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving' is true, but its converse 'No stationary thing is moving as long as it is moving, not perpetually' is false because some stationary things (like the earth) are perpetually stationary.
The author notes some later scholars allow conversion even for non-constant, but the dominant view is prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي معنى الكليّة الضرورية السالبة الكلية المطلقة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع والحكم دائم بدوامه، وسواء كان ذلك الوصف دائماً للموضوع أو غير دائم له كما حققناه، وهو كما في قولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "ب")، فعكسه (لا شيء من "أ" "ب" ما دام "ب") و (إلا فبعض "أ" "ب" في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") وعكسه (بعض) ما هو متصف بـ "ب" حالة اتصافه بـ "ب" "أ") وكان (لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "ب") وهو خلف هذا كله إذا كانت السالبة المشروطة هي دائمة. وأما إن كانت غير دائمة فلا تنعكس، فإنا إذا قلنا (لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً لم ينعكس (لا شيء من الساكن متحرك ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً) فإنه قد يكون بعض الساكن ساكناً دائماً لا يتحرك كـ (الأرض) مثلاً غير أن من المتأخرين من مثل عكس قولنا: (لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا
And the meaning of the universal necessary negative includes the universal absolute negative of the type where the subject is described by what is posited and the judgment is perpetual with its perpetuity, whether that description is perpetual for the subject or not perpetual, as we have verified. It is like our saying: 'No B is A as long as B'. Its converse is 'No A is B as long as B', and (otherwise some A is B in some times of its being B) and its converse is (some) what is described by B in the state of its being described by B (is A), while (No B is A as long as B) would be a contradiction. This is all if the conditional negative is constant (dā'ima). But if it is not constant, then it does not convert. For if we say 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving, not perpetually', it does not convert to 'No stationary thing is moving as long as it is moving, not perpetually', for some stationary things are perpetually stationary, never moving, like the earth, for example. However, some later scholars allow the conversion of our saying 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving, not
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage expands on the conversion rules for universal negative propositions that are absolute but qualified by a durative condition (mā dāma). The author distinguishes two types: (1) Constant (dā'im) conditional: the judgment 'No B is A as long as B' holds perpetually for the whole duration of B. In that case, the converse 'No A is B as long as B' is valid, proven by reductio. (2) Non-constant condition: the judgment does not hold perpetually (e.g., 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving' is true only during motion, but not perpetually because the moving thing may later be stationary). Such a proposition does not convert because the converse would require the same temporal restriction. The author gives the example of 'earth' that is perpetually stationary. The passage ends with a reference to later scholars who allowed conversion even for non-constant conditions. In Islamic logic, this has implications for propositions about changing attributes, e.g., 'No person in a state of prayer is speaking as long as he is in prayer' may convert only under constant conditions.

Key Terms

Conversion of Universal Negative Absolute with Conditions
Author explains conversion of universal negative absolute propositions and the role of permanence of the condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a universal negative absolute proposition with a condition can be validly converted and to identify incorrect conversions of conditioned propositions.

Core Idea
Only permanent conditions preserve truth in conversion of universal negatives; non-permanent conditions break it.

In this passage, the author explains a critical nuance in immediate inference (conversion) of universal negative propositions that carry a condition attached to the subject. Understanding when the condition is 'permanent' (دائمة) versus 'non-permanent' is essential to avoid fallacious conversions. The author also corrects an error made by some later logicians who mishandled the condition in conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its condition
Find a universal negative proposition with a condition on the subject, e.g., 'لا شيء من ب أ ما دام ب'. Note the condition: 'ما دام ب' (as long as it is B).
2
Check if the condition is permanent (دائمة)
Determine if the condition holds for every instance of the subject at all times. For example, 'لا شيء من الميتة طاهر ما دامت ميتة' – carrion is always carrion when it is carrion, so the condition is permanent.
If the condition is not permanent (e.g., 'لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً'), conversion is not valid.
3
Apply conversion rules for permanent conditions
For a permanent condition, convert to a universal negative with the same condition: 'لا شيء من أ ب ما دام ب'. For non-permanent conditions, the author states it does not convert (لا تنعكس).
The author also mentions a particular case where non-permanent yields a particular proposition, but the main point is that universal conversion fails.
4
Recognize the error in the later scholar's conversion
Some later scholars incorrectly converted a non-permanent conditional by transferring the condition from subject to predicate, e.g., converting 'لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً' into 'لا شيء من الساكن متحرك ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً'. This is wrong because the condition of conversion requires that the subject (with its condition) becomes the predicate, and the predicate (with its state) remains the subject – not swapping conditions.
5
Verify with a real example
Test with the moving/stationary example: some stationary things (like the earth) are permanently stationary, so the incorrect conversion would yield a false statement. This confirms the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي معنى الكليّة الضرورية السالبة الكلية المطلقة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع والحكم دائم بدوامه، وسواء كان ذلك الوصف دائماً للموضوع أو غير دائم له كما حققناه، وهو كما في قولنا: (لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "ب")، فعكسه (لا شيء من "أ" "ب" ما دام "ب") و (إلا فبعض "أ" "ب" في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") وعكسه (بعض) ما هو متصف بـ "ب" حالة اتصافه بـ "ب" "أ") وكان (لا شيء من "ب" "أ" ما دام "ب" وهو خلف هذا كله إذا كانت السالبة المشروطة هي دائمة. وأما إن كانت غير دائمة فلا تنعكس، فإنا إذا قلنا (لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً لم ينعكس (لا شيء من الساكن متحرك ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً) فإنه قد يكون بعض الساكن ساكناً دائماً لا يتحرك كـ (الأرض) مثلاً غير أن من المتأخرين من مثل عكس قولنا: (لا شيء من المتحرك ساكن ما دام متحركاً لا دائماً) بقوله: (لا شيء من الساكن متحرك ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً، وليس بحق من حيث إنه نقل شرط الموضوع إلى المحمول في العكس، وشرط العكس أن يحول الموضوع بشرطه محمولاً والمحمول بحاله موضوعاً.
And regarding the meaning of the necessary universal negative absolute proposition that belongs to the category of the subject's being described by what is posited and the judgment is permanent as long as that description lasts, whether that description is permanent for the subject or not, as we have verified. It is like our statement: 'No B is A as long as it is B'. Its conversion is 'No A is B as long as it is B'. And if not, then 'Some A is B in some times of its being B', and its conversion is 'Some of what is described by B while being described by B is A', and it would be that 'No B is A as long as it is B' is a contradiction. All this when the conditional negative is permanent. But if it is not permanent, then it does not convert. For if we say 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving, not permanently', it does not convert to 'No stationary thing is moving as long as it is moving, not permanently', because some stationary things are permanently stationary, never moving, like the earth, for example. However, some later scholars illustrated the conversion of our statement 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving, not permanently' by saying 'No stationary thing is moving as long as it is stationary, not permanently'. But this is not correct because it transfers the condition of the subject to the predicate in conversion, and the condition of conversion is that the subject with its condition becomes the predicate, and the predicate with its state remains the subject.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion of universal negative absolute propositions where the judgment is conditional on the subject's description. For example, 'No dead animal is pure as long as it is dead' (a permanent condition) converts to 'No pure thing is a dead animal as long as it is dead'. If the condition is not permanent, conversion fails. The author gives the example of moving/stationary: 'No moving thing is stationary as long as it is moving, not permanently' does not convert to 'No stationary thing is moving as long as it is stationary, not permanently', because some things (like the earth) are always stationary. A later scholar attempted such a conversion but erred by swapping the condition incorrectly.

C) Yes, it converts to 'بعض الطاهر ليس بميتة ما دامت ميتة'.

Key Takeaway
A universal negative proposition with a permanent condition converts to a universal negative with the same condition; a non-permanent condition blocks valid conversion.

Conversion of Non-Necessary Universal Negatives
Author discusses conversion of universal negatives that are not necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which universal negative propositions can be converted (necessary) and which cannot (absolute, contingent), and identify the special case of possibility in the special sense.

Core Idea
Absolute and contingent universal negatives do not convert; only necessary ones do—except possibility in the special sense.

In this passage, the author of Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explicates a crucial rule of immediate inference: conversion is not valid for all universal negatives. He clarifies that only necessary (modal) negatives convert directly, while absolute and contingent negatives do not, and he exposes a fallacy that tries to force conversion. A special case involving possibility in the special sense is also examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the universal negative is necessary (ضرورية) or non-necessary. Non-necessary includes absolute (مطلقة) and directed (موجهة) which are contingent. Example: 'No human is actually a writer' is absolute.
Necessary means the denial holds necessarily; absolute means it holds in actuality without necessity.
2
Apply the conversion rule
For non-necessary universal negatives, the rule states that no conversion is valid—neither universally nor particularly—except by matter, which is not uniform. That is, you cannot formally infer 'No writer is human' from 'No human is a writer' if the proposition is absolute.
'By matter' means considering the actual content might accidentally make it true, but that is not a logical rule.
3
Examine the fallacy
The author presents a fallacious argument: if 'No animal is walking' is true, then if the negation of animal from walking does not hold, some walking would be animal, leading to 'Some animal is walking', which seems to contradict the original. The fallacy lies in assuming that the original is necessary; with general possibility, the contradiction does not arise.
The fallacy is called تغليط (misrepresentation).
4
Handle the special possibility case
If the universal negative is 'possible in the special sense' (e.g., 'No human is a writer with special possibility'), it means each human is possibly not a writer. Since special possibility entails equal possibility of being and not-being, 'every human is possible not to be a writer' converts to 'some writer is possible not to be a human', a particular negative conversion.
Special possibility (ممكنة بالمعنى الخاص) means contingent—true and false are equally possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت الكلية السالبة غير ضرورية ولا بأحد المعنيين المذكورين فإما مطلقة أو موجهة وعلى كلا تقدير فلا عكس لها لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، إلا باعتبار المادة، وذلك مما لا يطرد فإنه قد يصدق قولنا بالإطلاق العام الوجودي أو الوقتي أو بالإمكان العام الخاص أو الأخص (لا شيء من الإنسان كاتب بالفعل ولا شيء من الحيوان ماش بالفعل، ولا يصدق سلب الحيوان عن الماشي بالفعل، ولا سلب الإنسان عن الكاتب بالفعل لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، لكنه ربما عقب هذا المثال بتغليط، وهو أنه إذا صدق لا شيء من الحيوان ماش لو لم يصدق سلب الحيوان - أي الذي جعل موضوعاً لسلب الماشي عنه - عن الماشي المسلوب وإلا كان بعض الماشي المسلوب هو بعض الحيوان الموضوع للسلب بالضرورة، وعكسه بعض الحيوان (ماش) وفيه تكذيب (لا) شيء من الحيوان (ماش خلف، وهذا ليس بحق، فإنك إن تنبهت لما حققناه علمت أنه ليس من كون بعض الماشي المسلوب هو بعض الحيوان الموضوع للسلب كذب لا شيء من الحيوان ماش) لأن عكس بعض الماشي (حيوان هو أن بعض الحيوان ماش بالإمكان العام فلا تكاذب وهو. فإن قيل : إذا كانت السالبة ممكنة بالمعنى الخاص كقولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان كاتباً بالإمكان الخاص فلا يخفى أن كل ما هو ممكن أن لا يكون فهو بعينه ممكن أن يكون، فإذا كان كل إنسان يمكن أن يكون كاتب فـ كل إنسان ممكن أن يكون كاتباً وعكسه (بعض الكاتب إنسان، وهو ممكن أن يكون فممكن أن لا يكون فـ (بعض) الكاتب ممكن أن لا يكون إنساناً وهو عكس قولنا: (كل إنسان ممكن أن لا يكون كاتباً. قلنا: كل ما هو ممكن أن لا يكون بالمعنى الخاص وإن كان ممكناً أن يكون
And if the universal negative is neither necessary nor had one of the two mentioned meanings, then either absolute or directed, and in both cases it has no conversion, neither universally nor particularly, except by matter, and that is not uniform. For it may be true by absolute existential or temporal generality, or by general, special, or more special possibility: 'No human is actually a writer' and 'No animal is actually walking', but the negation of animal about the actually walking is not true, nor the negation of human about the actually writer, neither universally nor particularly. However, this example may be followed by a fallacy, namely that if 'No animal is walking' is true, then if the negation of animal – which is made the subject of the negation of walking – from the negated walking is not true, then some negated walking would be some animal subject to negation by necessity, and its converse is 'Some animal is walking', and in that there is a falsification of 'No animal is walking', which is a contradiction. But this is not correct, for if you attend to what we have verified, you know that the fact that some negated walking is some animal subject to negation does not falsify 'No animal is walking', because the conversion of 'Some walking is animal' is that some animal is walking with general possibility, and there is no contradiction. If it is said: If the negative is possible in the special sense, like our statement: 'No human is a writer with special possibility', it is clear that everything that is possible not to be is itself possible to be. So if every human can be a writer, then every human is possible to be a writer, and its conversion is 'Some writer is human', and since he can be, he can not be, so 'Some writer is possible to not be human', which is the conversion of our statement 'Every human is possible to not be a writer'. We say: Everything that is possible not to be in the special sense, even though it is possible to be
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage addresses two points. First, universal negatives that are not necessary (absolute or contingent) have no formal conversion; examples like 'No human is actually a writer' do not convert. The author refutes a fallacious argument that tries to force conversion. Second, a question is raised about possible negatives in the special sense: if 'No human is a writer with special possibility', does it convert? The author responds that such possibility entails double possibility, leading to a particular negative conversion. The discussion is technical and concerns modal logic.

The Rule of Conversion: Keeping Conditions with the Subject
Author corrects a statement about transferring subject's condition to predicate in conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct a conversion that improperly transfers the subject's condition to the predicate.

Core Idea
In conversion, the subject keeps its condition; the predicate stays as it is.

This passage addresses a common mistake in applying conversion (al-'aks). When a proposition includes a condition attached to the subject, that condition must remain with that term when it becomes the predicate. The author presents an erroneous example and clarifies the correct rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the erroneous conversion
The statement 'Nothing stationary is moving as long as it is stationary' is presented as a conversion, but the author says it is wrong. Note that the condition 'as long as it is stationary' originally qualifies the subject 'stationary'. In the erroneous version, this condition has been moved to the predicate position.
The error is transferring the subject's condition to the predicate.
2
State the correct rule of conversion
The author declares: 'The condition of conversion is that the subject with its condition becomes predicate, and the predicate as it is becomes subject.' This means any qualification of the subject must stick with that term throughout the conversion process.
The term's condition is inseparable from it.
3
Apply the rule to a concrete Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Every intoxicant is haram as long as it intoxicates' (in Islamic law). The condition 'as long as it intoxicates' qualifies 'intoxicant'. When converting to 'Some haram things are intoxicants', the condition must remain with 'intoxicant' (the former subject), yielding: 'Some haram things are intoxicants as long as they intoxicate.' If the condition were moved to 'haram', the conversion would be wrong.
This example mirrors the author's correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائماً) بقوله: (لا شيء من الساكن متحرك ما دام ساكناً لا دائماً، وليس بحق من حيث إنه نقل شرط الموضوع إلى المحمول في العكس، وشرط العكس أن يحول الموضوع بشرطه محمولاً والمحمول بحاله موضوعاً.
(Always) by his statement: 'Nothing stationary is moving as long as it is stationary, not always.' And it is not correct because it transfers the condition of the subject to the predicate in conversion. The condition of conversion is that the subject with its condition becomes predicate, and the predicate as it is becomes subject.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage corrects a mistaken formulation of conversion. Someone said: 'Nothing stationary is moving as long as it is stationary, not always' — meaning the negation holds only during the time it is stationary, not always. The author points out that this statement errs by moving the subject's condition ('as long as it is stationary') to the predicate. The proper rule of conversion (عكس) is that the subject, together with its condition, becomes the predicate, and the predicate remains unchanged in its own condition. This rule ensures that the modality or temporal qualification stays with the same term.

Key Terms
العكس
شرط الموضوع

Key Takeaway
When converting a proposition, any condition on the subject must stay with that term in its new position as predicate.

Conversion of Non-Necessary Universal Negatives
Author states rule and refutes misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a universal negative proposition can be validly converted and explain why non-necessary ones cannot.

Core Idea
Only necessary universal negatives convert; temporal ones fail.

Understanding conversion of universal negative propositions is crucial for correct syllogistic reasoning. In this lesson, we examine why non-necessary universal negatives—those that are absolute or modal—do not yield a valid converse, and we refute a common sophistical objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of universal negative
Determine whether the universal negative proposition is necessary or non-necessary. The passage distinguishes necessary from absolute/temporal.
Necessary: always true. Non-necessary: true at some times or under some conditions.
2
Test conversion by substituting terms
Take the example: 'No human is actually a writer' (true at a moment). Try converting to 'No writer is actually a human'. Is that necessarily true? No, because some writers are humans (e.g., at other times).
Material truth does not guarantee logical conversion.
3
Analyze the sophistical objection
The objection claims: If 'No animal is walking' is true, then negating animal of walking must hold, else some walking is animal, leading to 'Some animal is walking', which contradicts the original. The author refutes this by noting the converse yields possibility, not actuality.
The fallacy lies in assuming the converse must be actual, not merely possible.
4
Formulate the rule
State the rule: Non-necessary universal negatives do not convert to any universal or particular negative. At best, they yield a particular affirmative with possibility (e.g., 'Some writer is a human' is possible, not necessary).
This rule is essential for assessing the validity of syllogisms involving such propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت الكلية السالبة غير ضرورية ولا بأحد المعنيين المذكورين فإما مطلقة أو موجهة وعلى كلا تقدير فلا عكس لها لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، إلا باعتبار المادة، وذلك مما لا يطرد فإنه قد يصدق قولنا بالإطلاق العام الوجودي أو الوقتي أو بالإمكان العام الخاص أو الأخص (لا شيء من الإنسان كاتب بالفعل ولا شيء من الحيوان ماش بالفعل، ولا يصدق سلب الحيوان عن الماشي بالفعل، ولا سلب الإنسان عن الكاتب بالفعل لا كلياً ولا جزئياً، لكنه ربما عقب هذا المثال بتغليط، وهو أنه إذا صدق لا شيء من الحيوان ماش لو لم يصدق سلب الحيوان - أي الذي جعل موضوعاً لسلب الماشي عنه - عن الماشي المسلوب وإلا كان بعض الماشي المسلوب هو بعض الحيوان الموضوع للسلب بالضرورة، وعكسه بعض الحيوان (ماش) وفيه تكذيب (لا) شيء من الحيوان (ماش خلف، وهذا ليس بحق، فإنك إن تنبهت لما حققناه علمت أنه ليس من كون بعض الماشي المسلوب هو بعض الحيوان الموضوع للسلب كذب لا شيء من الحيوان ماش) لأن عكس بعض الماشي (حيوان هو أن بعض الحيوان ماش بالإمكان العام فلا تكاذب.
And as for the universal negative that is not necessary and not in either of the two mentioned senses, it is either absolute or modal. In both cases it has no conversion, neither universally nor particularly, except materially, and that is not regular. For it may be true to say with absolute universal existence or temporal or with general specific possibility or more specific: 'No human is actually a writer' and 'No animal is actually walking', but it is not true to negate animal of the actually walking, nor to negate human of the actually writer, neither universally nor particularly. However, this example might be followed by a misinterpretation, which is: if 'No animal is walking' is true, if the negation of animal from the walking negated were not true, then some of the walking negated would necessarily be some of the animal subject to negation, and its converse would be 'some animal is walking', which would falsify 'No animal is walking' – this is a contradiction. And this is not correct, for if you attend to what we have verified, you will know that it is not necessary that because some of the walking negated is some of the animal subject to negation, 'No animal is walking' becomes false, because the converse of 'some walking is animal' is 'some animal is walking' with general possibility, so there is no contradiction.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage explains the conversion rule for universal negative propositions that are not necessary (i.e., absolute or modal). The author states that such propositions have no valid conversion, whether the converse is universal or particular, except when the material content accidentally makes it true, which is not a logical rule. He gives examples: 'No human is actually a writer' is true at a given moment, but its supposed converse 'No writer is actually a human' is false because some writers are humans. Likewise, 'No animal is actually walking' does not convert. He then refutes a sophistical objection: if 'No animal is walking' is true, someone might argue that negating 'animal' from 'walking' must also be true, else some walking things would be animals, leading to 'some animal is walking', contradicting the original. The author responds that the converse of 'some walking is animal' is 'some animal is walking' with general possibility, not necessarily actual, so no contradiction arises. The key point: non-necessary universals do not yield a universal or even particular negative converse; at best they yield a particular affirmative with possibility, which does not conflict.

Key Terms
الكلية السالبة
المطلقة
الإمكان العام

Key Takeaway
Non-necessary universal negatives do not convert to any universal or particular negative; at best they yield a particular affirmative with possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Modal Conversion: From Specific to General Possibility
Author answers an objection: universal negative possible (specific) converts only to particular affirmative with general possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct converse of a universal negative proposition with specific possibility and explain why the modality weakens to general possibility.

Core Idea
Converse of universal negative specific possibility is particular affirmative general possibility, not specific.

In modal logic, converting propositions requires careful attention to the modality. A common mistake is to assume the modality of the premise carries over unchanged to the converse. This lesson examines an objection and its answer concerning the conversion of a universal negative with specific possibility. You will learn the correct form of the converse and why the modal scope shifts from specific to general possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original proposition is a universal negative with specific possibility: 'No human is a writer with specific possibility.' That is, it is possible (in the specific sense) for every human to not be a writer.
Specific possibility means the thing is neither necessary nor impossible; it is contingent.
2
State the mistaken inference
The objector argues: since what is possible not to be is also possible to be, from the original we can infer 'Every human is possibly a writer' (universal affirmative with specific possibility), then convert to 'Some writer is human' (particular affirmative with specific possibility), then convert again to 'Some writer is possibly not human' (particular negative with specific possibility).
The objector attempts to preserve specific possibility throughout.
3
Apply correct conversion rule
The correct conversion of a universal negative (with any modality) is a particular affirmative (contraposition or simple conversion? Actually universal negative converts to particular affirmative with the same terms but the subject and predicate swap? Standard conversion: universal negative converts to universal negative? Wait, in Aristotelian logic, universal negative (E) converts to universal negative (E). But here the source says it converts to particular affirmative with general possibility. So the rule here is different due to modality. Actually the source says: the converse is 'Some writer is human' with general possibility. That is, from 'No human is a writer (with specific possibility)' we get 'Some writer is human (with general possibility)'. So the universal negative converts to a particular affirmative. That is because the original universal negative is about non-being, and the converse is about being. This is a specific conversion rule for modal propositions.
Check the text: 'its converse is only particular affirmative with general possibility'.
4
Check the modal change
The original premise has specific possibility, but the converse has only general possibility. General possibility includes everything that is not impossible, including necessary existents. The conversion weakens the modality.
A necessary existent is possible in the general sense but not in the specific sense.
5
Validate with an Islamic science example
Consider the Kalam proposition: 'Every temporally originated thing is possibly non-existent (specific possibility).' Its valid converse is 'Some possibly non-existent thing is temporally originated (general possibility).' This prevents modal errors in theological arguments.
The converse does not assert that the temporally originated thing is contingent in the specific sense; it only says it is not impossible that it is temporally originated.

So 'some writer can possibly not be human' which is the converse of our saying 'Every human can possibly not be a writer.' This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the conversion of a universal negative proposition with specific possibility (e.g., 'No human is a writer with specific possibility'). The objector argues: since what is possibly not is also possibly is, from 'Every human possibly is not a writer' we get 'Every human possibly is a writer' (by obversion? Actually the objector seems to derive an affirmative universal from the negative universal with specific possibility, then convert to 'Some writer is human' (particular affirmative with specific possibility), then convert to 'Some writer possibly is not human' (particular negative with specific possibility). The answer corrects: the converse is only particular affirmative with general possibility, not specific. That is, from the original, the valid converse is 'Some writer is human' with general possibility (i.e., possibly in the general sense), not specific. Furthermore, the general possible affirmative does not entail the negative; it might even be necessarily existent (e.g., if the writer is a human necessarily).

Key Terms

Particular Negative Propositions: No Conversion
Author states that particular negative propositions have no conversion, with separate reasoning for necessary and non-necessary cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why particular negative propositions cannot be validly converted and detect the fallacy of attempting such conversion.

Core Idea
Particular negatives never convert – the truth does not reverse.

In this lesson, we examine a key rule of immediate inference: particular negative propositions have no valid conversion. The author explains that whether the proposition is necessary or not, conversion fails. Understanding this prevents a common logical error when reasoning about categories in Islamic sciences, such as fiqh rulings and theological classifications.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four categorical forms
Warm up: A, E, I, O. Which ones convert? (A and I convert, E converts to E, O does not.)
2
Examine the author's claim
The passage states that particular negatives (O) have no conversion absolutely, except material accidents which are irrelevant.
3
Test the non-necessary case
Example: 'Some student is not present' – trying to convert to 'Some present is not student' might be false if all present are students.
4
Test the necessary case
The author's example: 'Some animal is necessarily not human' – true for horses. But the converse 'Some human is not animal' is false because all humans are animals.
5
Generalise the rule
For any O proposition, the converse does not logically follow. This is because O propositions have existential import only for the subject, not the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القضية جزئية سالبة فلا عكس لها مطلقاً، إلا باعتبار خصوص المادة ولا اعتبار به وأما إذا كانت غير ضرورية فلما عرف في الكلية السالبة إذا لم تكن ضرورية بالاعتبارين المذكورين وأما إذا كانت ضرورية فلأنه لا يلزم من صدق بعض الحيوان ليس إنساناً بالضرورة) صدق (بعض الإنسان ليس حيواناً).
And as for the particular negative proposition, it has no conversion absolutely, except by consideration of the material, and no regard is given to that. As for when it is not necessary, it is according to what is known in the universal negative when it is not necessary by the two mentioned considerations. And as for when it is necessary, it is because from the truth of 'some animal is necessarily not human' it does not follow the truth of 'some human is not animal'.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage gives the rule for particular negative propositions (e.g., 'Some animal is not human'). The author declares that they have no conversion at all, neither to universal nor to particular, except in rare material cases which are disregarded. He then explains separately for non-necessary and necessary particular negatives. For non-necessary ones, the reason is the same as for universal negatives – they involve temporal or modal conditions that prevent regular conversion. For necessary ones (e.g., 'Some animal is necessarily not human' – true because horses are necessarily not human), the converse 'Some human is not animal' is false because every human is essentially an animal. Thus conversion fails.

Key Terms
الجزئية السالبة
الضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because conversion only works for universals
C) Because the proposition is affirmative
D) Because necessity prevents conversion

Key Takeaway
Particular negative propositions (O) never have a valid conversion – the truth of an O proposition does not justify the truth of its converse.

Refuting the Fallacy in Converting Particular Negative Necessary Propositions
Author refutes an objection on conversion of particular negative necessary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the error in the claim that particular negative necessary propositions are convertible, and explain why the conversion fails.

Core Idea
Conversion of particular negatives fails because 'some' is indefinite and the subject in the converse is not the same individual.

In this passage, the author examines an objection that would allow converting 'Some A is necessarily not B' into 'Some B is not A'. The objection seems plausible but confuses the indefinite 'some' with a determinate individual. Understanding this nuance is crucial for mastering the logic of immediate inferences in categorical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Define conversion
Recall that conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving quality and truth. For categorical propositions, the truth must be preserved.
Refer to the definition given in the passage: 'جعل الموضوع محمولاً، والمحمول موضوعاً مع بقاء الكيفية والصدق'.
2
Examine the objection
The objector argues from 'Some animal is necessarily not human' that the part of animal that is not human must be something else (e.g., horse), so that something should be negated from human, yielding 'Some human is not that animal'.
The objector assumes that the 'some' refers to a specific individual.
3
Identify the hidden assumption
The hidden assumption is that the definite particular in the premise ('some animal') is a determinate entity that can be referred to in the converse. But 'some' is indefinite; it does not pick out a specific individual across both propositions.
The author refutes by pointing out that if it were determinate, it would be a singular proposition, not a particular.
4
Apply the refutation
The truth of 'some animal is necessarily not human' is satisfied if any animal (like a horse) is not human. But when we try to convert, the converse would be 'some human is not animal', which is false because all humans are animals. The connection fails because the same individual is not used.
The conversion would require the individual horse to be the predicate of 'human', i.e., 'some human is not horse', but that is not the intended conversion.
5
Generalize the rule
Thus, particular negative necessary propositions are not convertible. Convertibility for particular negatives only holds in special cases (like possible in the specific sense), but not for necessary ones.
This aligns with standard Aristotelian logic: O propositions are not convertible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Rather it is other than it, and that thing must be negated from human just as human was negated from it, even if negating absolute animal from it is impossible. So it is not that thing from which we negated human necessarily, for it is not valid to negate human from absolute animal as known. Therefore, it is not hidden that if our statement is true:

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the conversion of particular negative propositions that are possible in the specific sense. The objector recalls the rule: conversion swaps subject and predicate while preserving quality and truth. He argues from 'Some animal is necessarily not human': the particular animal from which human is negated is not itself the human species (otherwise the negation would be false), so it must be something else. Therefore, that something must be negated from human, just as human was negated from it, leading to 'some human is not that animal', i.e., a particular negative converse. Thus, the objector claims, particular negatives should be convertible. The answer refutes: The truth of 'some animal is necessarily not human' does not require that the specific animal (like a horse) be the one that appears in the converse. The converse would be 'some human is not animal', which is false. The mistake is assuming the subject is a determinate individual, but 'some animal' is indefinite and can refer to different individuals. The conversion fails because the same particularity is not preserved. The text cuts off, but the refutation is clear.

Conditions for Contrapositive and Errors in Conversion
Author explains condition for contrapositive and demonstrates errors when condition is violated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct contrapositive of a categorical proposition and detect errors caused by violating the condition of transferring the predicate in its entirety.

Core Idea
For valid contrapositive, the entire predicate must be moved with all its parts.

In this passage, the author explains that the contrapositive (aks) may be false if we do not observe the condition that the predicate must be transferred in its entirety along with any attached particles. Using examples from universal affirmative, particular affirmative, and universal negative propositions, he shows how common mistakes arise when part of the predicate is left behind. Mastering this condition ensures you can correctly convert propositions in logical reasoning, a skill essential for rigorous argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is universal affirmative (A), particular affirmative (I), universal negative (E), or particular negative (O). Each type has a specific contrapositive pattern.
The author covers A, I, and E types in the passage.
2
Extract the subject and predicate
Note any attached prepositions or particles that form part of the predicate. For example, in 'Every peg is in the wall', the predicate is 'in the wall' —not just 'wall'.
The preposition 'in' is an integral part of the predicate.
3
Apply the condition: transfer the entire predicate
When swapping subject and predicate, ensure the entire predicate phrase, including particles, moves as a unit. The predicate becomes the new subject, and the original subject becomes the new predicate.
If any part is left behind, the contrapositive may be false.
4
Verify with the author's examples
For 'Every peg is in the wall', the correct contrapositive is 'In the wall is every peg'. Notice the preposition 'in' remains with 'wall'. For 'Some knife is in the watermelon', the correct is 'In the watermelon is some knife'. For 'Nothing of the watermelon is in the knife', the correct is 'Nothing in the knife is of the watermelon'.
These preserve the entire predicate with its particles.
5
Spot the common mistake
The mistake occurs when part of the predicate is separated. For example, thinking the contrapositive of 'Every peg is in the wall' is 'Some wall is in the peg'—this is false because the preposition 'in' was not transferred with 'wall'.
The same error pattern appears for I and E propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أنه قد يكذب العكس في كل صورة قضينا بانعكاسها بسبب الغلط في شرط العكس، وهو نقل المحمول بكليته والموضوع بكليته بأن نقل بعض أجزاء الموضوع إلى المحمول والمحمول إلى الموضوع، أما في الكلية الموجبة فكما لو قلنا (كل وتد في الحائط) فقد يظن أن عكسها بعض الحائط في الوتد)، وهو كذب وليس ذلك إلا بسبب أن المحمول هو في الحائط) و (في) جزئه، وهي لم تنقل مع المحمول عند جعله موضوعاً، بل نقلت إليه حين كان موضوعاً وجعلت جزءاً منه عند جعله محمولاً، ولو نقلت مع المحمول عند جعله موضوعاً لقد كان العكس صادقاً، وهو وهو أن يقال عكس (كل) الوتد في الحائط (في الحائط كل الوتد). وأما في الجزئية الموجبة فكما في قولنا بعض السكين في البطيخة) فقد يظن : أن العكس بعض البطيخة في السكين وليس بصادق، وإنما العكس في البطيخة بعض السكين. وأما في الكلية السالبة فكما في قولنا: (لا) شيء من البطيخة في السكين) (ولا الحائط شيء من في الوتد فقد يظن أن عكسها لا شيء من السكين في البطيخة و (لا شيء من الوتد في الحائط وهو غير صادق، بل العكس (لا شيء في السكين من (البطيخة و لا شيء في الوتد من الحائط).
Know that the contrapositive may be false in every form for which we judged its conversion, due to error in the condition of contrapositive, which is transferring the predicate in its entirety and the subject in its entirety, by transferring some parts of the subject to the predicate and the predicate to the subject. As for the universal affirmative, for example if we say 'Every peg is in the wall', one may think its contrapositive is 'Some wall is in the peg', which is false, and that is only because the predicate is 'in the wall' and 'in' is part of it, and it was not transferred with the predicate when it was made subject, but was transferred to it when it was subject and made part of it when it was made predicate. If it had been transferred with the predicate when it was made subject, then the contrapositive would have been true, namely that the contrapositive of 'Every peg is in the wall' is 'In the wall is every peg'. As for the particular affirmative, for example our saying 'Some knife is in the watermelon', one may think that the contrapositive is 'Some watermelon is in the knife', which is not true; rather the contrapositive is 'In the watermelon is some knife'. As for the universal negative, for example our saying 'Nothing of the watermelon is in the knife' and 'Nothing of the wall is in the peg', one may think that its contrapositive is 'Nothing of the knife is in the watermelon' and 'Nothing of the peg is in the wall', which is not true; rather the contrapositive is 'Nothing in the knife is of the watermelon' and 'Nothing in the peg is of the wall'.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage explains the condition for valid contrapositive (عكس) in categorical propositions and illustrates common errors. The author begins by stating that contrapositive may be false in cases where we think conversion holds, due to violating the condition: the predicate must be transferred in its entirety and the subject in its entirety. The error occurs when only parts of the subject or predicate are moved. The author gives three examples: (1) For universal affirmative 'Every peg is in the wall', the mistaken contrapositive is 'Some wall is in the peg' (false) because the preposition 'in' is part of the predicate and was not transferred fully. The correct contrapositive is 'In the wall is every peg'. (2) For particular affirmative 'Some knife is in the watermelon', the mistaken contrapositive is 'Some watermelon is in the knife' (false); the correct one is 'In the watermelon is some knife'. (3) For universal negative 'Nothing of the watermelon is in the knife', the mistaken contrapositive is 'Nothing of the knife is in the watermelon' (false); the correct one is 'Nothing in the knife is of the watermelon'. The lesson highlights that in contrapositive, the quantifier and the preposition must be preserved correctly.

Key Terms
العكس (al-aks): Contrapositive: swapping subject and predicate while preserving quantity and quality, transferring the predicate wholly along with any attached particles. [Note: In later logic, 'aks' often refers to simple conversion (swapping subject and predicate) without regard to prepositions; here it specifically includes transfer of attached particles.]
نقل المحمول بكليته والموضوع بكليته (naql al-mahmūl bi-kulliyatihi wal-mawḍūʿ bi-kulliyatihi): Transferring the predicate in its entirety and the subject in its entirety; the condition for valid contrapositive that ensures no part of the expression is omitted or altered. [Note: This precise phrasing is specific to the author's treatment of conversion involving prepositions; standard definitions of 'aks' do not emphasize this condition.]

Key Takeaway
The condition for valid contrapositive is that the predicate must be transferred in its entirety, including any attached particles, otherwise the resulting proposition is false.

Understanding 'Aks an-Naqīḍ (Contrapositive) in Categorical Propositions
Definition and derivation of 'aks an-naqīḍ for universal affirmative with modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct 'aks an-naqīḍ for a universal affirmative proposition with any modality and explain why the common mistake for absolute modality is logically unsound.

Core Idea
The correct contrapositive of an absolute universal affirmative is necessary, not absolute.

This lesson unpacks the second type of immediate inference in categorical logic: ʿaks an-naqīḍ (contrapositive of negation). The author defines it, then applies it to universal affirmative propositions under different modalities. A common error is corrected: many thought the contrapositive of an absolute universal is also absolute; the author shows it must be necessary. We will walk through the derivation and explore the modal implications.

Guided Path
1
Define the operation
ʿAks an-naqīḍ consists of making the negation of the subject the predicate and the negation of the predicate the subject. For example, from 'Every B is J' we aim to get 'Every non-J is non-B'.
This is a form of contrapositive (modus tollens in term logic).
2
Identify the target proposition type
The passage focuses on universal affirmative propositions (كل ب ج). They can be absolute (without modality) or modal (necessary, possible).
Absolute here means unmodalized, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' taken as a simple universal.
3
Spot the mistaken view
For absolute universal affirmative, some erroneously claim the contrapositive is also absolute: 'Every non-J is non-B absolutely'. The author flags this as a confusion.
The mistaken view fails to account for modality in the conclusion.
4
Follow the refutation
The author shows that there is no contradiction between an absolute universal (كل ب ج بالإطلاق) and a particular negative (بعض ب ليس ج) because the latter is only possibly true in general sense. Thus the attempted proof by contradiction fails.
The negation of an absolute universal yields a particular negative with possibility, not necessity.
5
Derive the correct contrapositive
The correct form for absolute universal is: 'Everything that is not J always is necessarily not B' (كل ما ليس ج دائماً فبالضرورة ليس ب). This is because if B entails J absolutely, then anything that always lacks J must necessarily lack B.
The conclusion is necessary even though the premise is absolute — a key insight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني من العكس وهو المسمى بعكس النقيض في الحَمْليات، فعبارة عن جعل نقيض الموضوع محمولاً ونقيض المحمول موضوعاً، وقد تخبّط في ذلك جماعة يؤبه لهم في هذا الشأن سنبينه على وجه تخبطهم في كل موضوع على حسبه. فنقول: القضية إما كليّة موجبة أو كليّة سالبة أو جزئية موجبة أو جزئية سالبة، فإن كانت كليّة موجبة فإما مطلقة أو موجهة، فإن كانت مطلقة كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق العام فقد ظن أن عكس النقيض فيها كل ما ليس "ج" ليس "ب" بالإطلاق) وقرروا ذلك بأنه لو لم يصدق كل ما ليس "ج" ليس "ب") وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو (بعض ما ليس "ج" هو "ب") وعكسه (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وكان (كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف. ولم يعلموا أنه لا مناقضة بين (كل "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق) وبين (بعض "ب" ليس "ج"، إذ هو بالإمكان العام كيف وأنه قد يصدق قولنا: (بالإطلاق العام كل متنفس ولا يصدق (كل ما ليس بمتنفس إنسان بل عكس النقيض كل ما ليس "ج" دائماً فبالضرورة ليس "ب") وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا كل "ب" "ج" بالإطلاق) فقد حكمنا بأن كل "ب" يلزمه الحكم عليه بـ "ج" بالإطلاق، فكل ما ليس "ج" دائماً فقد انتفى عنه الحكم عليه بـ "ج" بالإطلاق فيلزم منه انتفاء "ب" بالضرورة، ضرورة انتفاء الملزوم انتفاء لازمه، ولأنه لو كان ما انتفى عنه "ج" دائماً هو "ب" لما صدق قولنا: (كل "ج" بالإطلاق وهو خلف وعلى هذا إن كانت ضرورية كقولنا: بالضرورة) كل "ب" "ج") فعكس النقيض كل ما ليس "ج" بالإمكان العام فبالضرورة ليس "ب") لأن كل ما يصدق عليه سلب "ج" عنه بالإمكان العام يكذب كونه "ج" بالضرورة، فلو كان ما ليس "ج" بالإمكان العام "ب" لما صدق عليه أنه "ج" بالضرورة وكان (كل) "ب" "ج" بالضرورة) وهو خلف؛ لأن لازم ((كل) "ب" انه "ج" بالضرورة فكل ما انتفى عنه هذا اللازم فبالضرورة لا يكون "ب". وإن كانت ممكنة عامة كقولنا: (كل "ب" "ج" بالإمكان العام فعكس النقيض كل ما ليس "ج" بالضرورة، فبالضرورة ليس "ب") لأنه يلزم من سلب "ج" بالضرورة سلب إيجابه بالإمكان العام، وهو لازم (كل) "ب" ) ويلزم من انتفاء اللازم انتفاء الملزوم؛ ولأنه لو كان ما سلب عنه "ج" بالضرورة "ب" لما كان (كل "ب" "ج" بالضرورة) وهو خلف. وإن كانت ممكنة خاصة فعكس النقيض كل ما ليس "ج" بالضرورة فبالضرورة ليس "ب") لأن كل ما سلب عنه "ج بالضرورة فلا يصدق عليه أنه "ج" بالإمكان الخاص.
And as for the second type of contrapositive, which is called 'aks an-naqīḍ in categorical propositions, it consists of making the negation of the subject the predicate and the negation of the predicate the subject. Some respected people have been confused about this; we will clarify their confusion in each topic accordingly. We say: The proposition is either universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. If it is universal affirmative, it is either absolute or modal. If it is absolute, like our saying 'Every B is J absolutely', it was thought that its 'aks an-naqīḍ is 'Everything that is not J is not B absolutely'. They justified that that if 'Everything that is not J is not B' were not true, then its contradiction 'Something that is not J is B' would be true, and its contrapositive 'Some B is not J' and 'Every B is J' results in a contradiction. But they did not know that there is no contradiction between 'Every B is J absolutely' and 'Some B is not J', since it is possible in the general sense. How? It is possible that our saying 'Every living being is absolutely' is true, but 'Everything that is not living is a human' is not true. Rather 'aks an-naqīḍ is 'Everything that is not J always is necessarily not B'. This is because if we say 'Every B is J absolutely', we have judged that every B is necessarily characterized by J absolutely. So everything that is not J always has the judgment of J absolutely negated from it, which necessitates the negation of B necessarily, because the negation of the necessary entailment necessitates the negation of the entailed. And because if that from which J is always negated were B, then our saying 'Every J absolutely' would not be true, which is a contradiction. According to this, if it is necessary, like our saying 'Necessarily every B is J', then 'aks an-naqīḍ is 'Everything that is not J generally possibly is necessarily not B', because everything of which the negation of J is true generally possibly, its being J necessarily is false. So if that which is not J generally possibly were B, it would not be true that it is J necessarily, and 'Every B is J necessarily' would not hold, which is a contradiction. This is because the necessary entailment of 'Every B is J necessarily' is that everything from which this entailment is negated is necessarily not B. And if it is generally possible, like our saying 'Every B is J generally possibly', then 'aks an-naqīḍ is 'Everything that is not J necessarily is necessarily not B', because the necessary negation of J necessitates the negation of its affirmation generally possibly, which is a necessary entailment of 'Every B', and from the negation of the entailment results the negation of the entailed. And because if that from which J is necessarily negated were B, then 'Every B is J necessarily' would not hold, which is a contradiction. And if it is specially possible, then 'aks an-naqīḍ is 'Everything that is not J necessarily is necessarily not B', because everything from which J is necessarily negated is not true of it that it is J specially possibly.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage defines 'aks an-naqīḍ (contrapositive of negation) and applies it to universal affirmative propositions with various modalities. The author begins by defining the operation: making the negation of the subject the predicate and the negation of the predicate the subject. Then he notes that some scholars have been confused about this. He then classifies propositions into types (universal affirmative, etc.) and focuses on universal affirmative with modalities: absolute, necessary, general possible, and special possible. For absolute universal affirmative 'Every B is J absolutely', some mistakenly thought the contrapositive is 'Every non-J is non-B absolutely'. The author refutes this by showing that there is no contradiction between 'Every B is J absolutely' and 'Some B is not J', because the negation is only possible in general sense. The correct contrapositive for absolute is 'Everything that is not J always is necessarily not B' — a necessary conclusion. This is derived from the fact that if B entails J absolutely, then anything that always lacks J must necessarily lack B. For necessary universal affirmative, the contrapositive becomes 'Everything that is not J possibly is necessarily not B'. For general possible, the contrapositive is 'Everything that is not J necessarily is necessarily not B'. For special possible, the contrapositive is 'Everything that is not J necessarily is necessarily not B' (similar to general possible but with a different justification). The author uses logical entailment and proof by contradiction (خلف) to establish these results.

Key Terms

الإطلاق العام
بالإمكان العام

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of an absolute universal affirmative is necessary, not absolute, because the entailment from subject to predicate is necessary even when the proposition is unmodalized.
