بنفسك.
The disjunctive to the conditional: you remove the disjunctive connector and insert the conditional connector while taking the contradictory of one of the two parts, whichever it may be, if it has only two parts. And if it has more than that, you are free to place the contradictory of one of the parts as the antecedent or its very self. If it is the first, then the antecedent is a categorical and the consequent is a positive disjunctive from the remainder. If it is the second, the antecedent is also categorical but the consequent may be from categoricals if you make it the contradictory of the remainder, and it may be a positive disjunctive from the contradictory of the remainder, preventing emptiness without combination. And if you wish to reverse the situation by making what was the antecedent the consequent, and what was the consequent the antecedent, no problem. And there is no difference in this between positive or negative except by taking the contradictory of the more general from the negative disjunctive parts if it is less than the more specific of the two. As for if the quality differs: if you wish to convert the positive to negative or negative to positive, then either you intend to convert the conditional to disjunctive or vice versa. If you intend to convert the conditional to disjunctive, then if the conditional is positive, remove the conditional connector and insert the disjunctive connector and change the quality. If it is negative, remove the conditional connector and insert the disjunctive connector and change the quality. And if you intend to convert the disjunctive to conditional, then either it has only two parts or more. If it is the first, you do with it as we taught you in the conditional. If it is the second, then either the disjunctive is positive or negative. If it is positive, you place the very self of one of the parts as the antecedent and the consequent is a positive disjunctive from the remainder with change of quality. And if you wish to make the consequent and the antecedent the consequent, no harm. And on both estimates the proposition consists of a categorical and a positive disjunctive. If it is negative, you make one of the parts of the disjunctive the antecedent in the conditional and the consequent is a negative disjunctive from the remainder. And if you wish to advance and delay, no problem. And on both estimates the proposition consists of a categorical and a negative disjunctive. And the types of conversion we have indicated are consistent in all matters except converting the negative conditional to the positive disjunctive in the matter of possibility. For it may be true our saying: (Possibly it is not that if this is a man then he is a writer) while our saying is not true: Either this is a man or he is a writer, neither necessarily nor possibly, because man is necessary for writer, so how can he be a writer and not be? And from here the disjunction is not the negation of conjunction as was thought, although it may follow from negation of conjunction. And if you wish to clarify all that by reductio, you say: If it is not true that the supposed proposition is equivalent to the supposed original, then the true is its contradictory. Then you look at what is equivalent to that contradictory of negatives if it was positive, or positives if it was negative, as we taught you. For you will find it falsifying the original and thereby the reductio becomes clear. And we also clarify that by converting the positive conditional to the positive disjunctive, and you know it in all. So we say: (If we say: If this is a man then he is an animal, if our saying in its equivalency is not true: Either it is a man or it is not an animal) its contradictory is true, which is that it is not the case that either it is a man or it is not an animal. And you have become aware of what we indicated regarding conversion of conditionals to one another, and likewise in disjunctives; you have learned that this contradictory is equivalent to our saying: (Either it is a man or it is an animal). And this opposition contradicts the conditional, a reductio. As for the contradiction of conditionals, it is according to what is known in categoricals. And likewise their conversion – I mean conversion by equality (aks al-istiwa') – which is swapping the antecedent and consequent while preserving the quality and truth and falsity as they are. And conversion by contrapositive (aks al-naqid) is making the contradictory of the antecedent the consequent and the contradictory of the consequent the antecedent. And verify that yourself.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the rules for converting conditional propositions (qalb al-shartiyyat) between connected (muttaṣila) and disjunctive (munfaṣila) forms. The author begins with converting a disjunctive (with multiple parts) to a conditional. The key operation: remove the disjunctive connector ('aw) and insert the conditional connector (in/ida) while taking the contradictory (naqīḍ) of one part as the antecedent, and then the consequent is a disjunction of the remaining parts (preserving the original disjunction's quality). If the original disjunctive has more than two parts, the converter has options: either take the contradictory of one part as the antecedent (then the consequent is a positive disjunction of the remainder) or take the very same part as the antecedent (then the consequent can be either a conjunction of contradictories of the remainder or a positive disjunction from their contradictories, of the type that prevents emptiness 'mani'at al-khuluw' but not combination). The author also notes that the antecedent becomes a categorical (ḥamliyya) and the consequent a disjunctive (or multiple categoricals). He allows reversing antecedent and consequent if desired. The rule applies to positive and negative disjunctives, except when taking the contradictory of the more general part of a negative disjunction. Then the author addresses cases where the quality differs between the original and target: converting a positive conditional to a negative disjunctive or vice versa. For converting a conditional to a disjunctive, one removes the conditional connector, inserts the disjunctive connector, and changes the quality. [The text seems to repeat that for both positive and negative conditionals.] Then for converting a disjunctive to a conditional with the same quality change, he details the two-part case (same as before) and multi-part case: if the disjunctive is positive, place one part as antecedent and make the consequent a positive disjunction of the remainder while changing the quality (likely of the whole or the consequent? The text says 'ma'a taghyir al-kayfiyya' – it might mean the consequent disjunction's quality is changed, but the example should clarify). If the disjunctive is negative, place one part as antecedent and the consequent is a negative disjunction of the remainder. On both cases, the resulting proposition is a compound of a categorical and a disjunctive. The author then states that these conversion rules are consistent across all 'matters' (mawādd) except one: converting a negative conditional to a positive disjunctive in the matter of possibility (imkān). He gives an example: It is possibly true that 'It is not that if this is a man then he is a writer' (i.e., it is possible that the conditional is false), but it is not true (neither necessarily nor possibly) that 'Either this is a man or he is a writer' because the relationship between man and writer is not such that manhood necessarily implies writerhood or exclusion. The author refutes a misconception: disjunction is not simply the negation of conjunction (salab al-ittisāl), although it may entail that negation. He then offers a proof by reductio (khalaf) to test equivalence: assume the original proposition is not equivalent to its converted form, then the true proposition is the contradictory of the supposed equivalent. Then examine what that contradictory is equivalent to, and you will find it falsifies the original. He illustrates with the conversion of positive conditional to positive disjunctive: 'If this is a man then it is an animal' is equivalent to 'Either it is a man or it is not an animal'? Actually, he tests the equivalence: if the conversion claim is false, its contradictory (that it is not the case that either it is a man or it is not an animal) is true; but that contradictory is equivalent to 'Either it is a man or it is an animal' – which contradicts the original conditional (thus proving the conversion valid by reductio). Finally, he briefly mentions contradiction of conditionals (tanāquḍ), which follows the same rules as categoricals, and two types of conversion: 'aks al-istiwa' (simple conversion) where antecedent and consequent are swapped while preserving quality and truth value, and 'aks al-naqid' (conversion by contrapositive) where the contradictory of the antecedent becomes the consequent and the contradictory of the consequent becomes the antecedent. He leaves the verification to the reader.

Key Terms
عكس الاستواء
عكس النقيض
مقدم
تالي
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) If it is an animal, then it is a man.
B) If it is not an animal, then it is not a man.
C) If it is not a man, then it is not an animal.
D) It is not a man or it is an animal.

Key Takeaway
A conditionals can be manipulated by swapping terms or their contradictories, but not all such manipulations preserve logical equivalence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Refuting Circularity in Al-Khalf for Universals
Author refutes the objection that using al-khalf for absolute/possible universals leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a dependence between logical methods constitutes a vicious circle by analyzing the conditions given in the passage.

Core Idea
Not every dependence is a circle; only when proof of each depends on proof of the other.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: using the reductio method (al-khalf) to prove absolute and possible universals seems to require prior knowledge of particular converses, which themselves are proven by al-khalf. The author shows that this is not circular unless the proof of the particular converse itself depends on the validity of that converse. Let's unpack this refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the charge of circularity
What exactly is the objection claiming? It claims that using al-khalf for absolute/possible universals requires prior knowledge of the particular converse, which itself is known only through al-khalf, thus creating a circle.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The author distinguishes between dependence that is not circular (general depends on specific, but specific does not depend on general) and dependence that would be circular (if the specific's proof depends on the general's validity).
3
Locate the condition for true circularity
Find where the author says circularity would occur: only if the parts of al-khalf in explaining the particular converse depend on knowing the validity of that same particular converse.
4
Apply the principle
Given an example from Islamic logic, determine if dependence is circular using the author's condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلف في المطلق العام والممكن العام على معرف العكس الخاص أولاً، حتى يكون الخلف متوقفاً على معرفة ما لا يعرف إلا بالخلف، بل جاز إجراء ذلك فيهما، وإن كان العكس الخاص الحق مجهولاً. نعم غاية ما يقدر أنه إذا صح أجزاء الخلف في بيان معنى الأخص جاز إجراؤه في بيان الأعم، وهو غير مخفض إلى الدور ، ثم إنه وإن توقف إجراء الخلف في المطلق العام والممكن العام على معرفة العكس الخاص والعكس الخاص مما لا يتبين له إلا بالخلف فليس ذلك بدور، إلا أن تكون أجزاء الخلف في بيان العكس الخاص الحق متوقفاً على معرفة صحة العكس الخاص الذي لا تبين له إلا بالخلف، أما إذا كان إجراؤه في المطلق والممكن العام متوقفاً على معرفة صحة عكس الأخص فلا.
Al-khalf in the absolute universal and the possible universal [depends] on knowing the particular converse first, so that al-khalf would depend on knowledge of what is not known except by al-khalf. Rather, it is permissible to conduct it in both, even if the true particular converse is unknown. Yes, the utmost that can be estimated is that if the parts of al-khalf are valid in explaining the meaning of the more specific, then it is permissible to conduct it in explaining the more general, and it is not reduced to circularity. Then, even if conducting al-khalf in the absolute universal and the possible universal depends on knowing the particular converse, and the particular converse is not clarified except by al-khalf, that is not a circularity, unless the parts of al-khalf in explaining the true particular converse depend on knowing the validity of the particular converse which is not clarified except by al-khalf. As for when conducting it in the absolute and possible universal depends on knowing the validity of the converse of the more specific, then no [circularity occurs].
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection about circularity in using the method of reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) to establish absolute and possible universal propositions. The objection claims that al-khalf for a universal term requires prior knowledge of the particular converse, which itself is only known through al-khalf, leading to circularity. The author responds by distinguishing two scenarios: If the parts of al-khalf are valid in explaining a more specific meaning, then it is permissible to apply al-khalf to a more general meaning without circularity. Even if conducting al-khalf for the absolute and possible universal depends on knowing the particular converse, and the particular converse is only clarified through al-khalf, this does not constitute a true circularity unless the parts of al-khalf for the particular converse themselves depend on the validity of that converse. As long as the dependence is only from the general to the particular and not back, there is no circle. The author concludes that if conducting al-khalf for the universal depends on knowing the validity of the particular converse, that is acceptable and not circular.

Key Terms
الخلف
المطلق العام
الممكن العام
العكس الخاص

C) If the possible universal is used in defining the absolute universal.
D) If al-khalf is applied to both universal and particular simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Al-khalf for universal propositions can legitimately depend on particular converses without circularity, as long as the particular converse's proof does not itself depend on the universal.

Refuting the Third Objection on Converses
Author refutes the third objection that the converse of every proposition falls under absolute/possible universal, showing its implications and rebutting an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of the claim that all converses fall under absolute/general possible universals and explain why studying particular converses is necessary.

Core Idea
General knowledge of converses does not replace the need for specific rules.

The logician faces an objection: since every converse is covered by absolute and possible universals, why study converses in detail? The author refutes this as short-sighted, showing that general categories cannot substitute for precise conversion rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The third objection claims that because all converses fall under the absolute universal and the more general possible, there is no need to study particular converses.
The objector assumes general categories provide full knowledge.
2
Understand the author's response
The author declares the objection ignorant and lacking verification.
3
Trace the first reason
The converse of affirmative propositions occurs with general possibility. This modality is not under the absolute universal nor the general possible, because the latter is more general and the more general does not fall under the more specific.
This reverses the objector's claim about universal coverage.
4
Trace the second reason
Even if all converses did fall under those universals, that would not hinder using qiyas al-khalf for true converses; otherwise all methods would be invalid, which is absurd.
5
Summarize the critique
The author dismantles the objection on multiple logical fronts, showing its fallacious reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التشكيك الثالث: فهو وإن أورد على أحسن تنميق فخارج عن التحقيق، وليس يلزم من كون عكس كل قضية داخلاً تحت الإطلاق والإمكان الأعم، وإلا اكتفى به عن معرفة العكس الخاص؛ إذ قد يتعلق بالخاص ما لا يتعلق بالعام فالبحث عن الخاص واجب، وإن كان لا خلو عن أن يكون داخلاً تحت هذا الأعم، كيف التوسع والتحجر بدعوى دخول عكس كل قضية تحت الإطلاق والإمكان العام مما يُشعر بجهل قائله وقلة تدربه، وضعف نظره، فإن عكس القضايا الموجبة يقع بالإمكان العام، وذلك مما لا دخول له تحت المطلق العام ولا الممكن العام، أما أنه لا دخول لها تحت الممكن العام فلكونه أعـمّ منه؛ لتناوله ما لا بد من وجوده وما من وجوده بد، والأعم لا دخول له تحت الأخص. ثم وإن قدر أن المطلقة العامة مما يتناول أيضاً ما من وجوده بد، فهي مساوية للمكن العام والمساوي لا يكون أعمّ مما ساواه. ثم إنه وإن كان عكس كل قضية داخلاً تحت المطلق والممكن العام، فليس ذلك مما وقع لازماً في بيان العكس الحقيقي من استعمال قياس الخلف، فإن كان مما يمنع من به في البيان فهو أيضاً لازم في كل ما يقدر استعماله الطرق، وفيه إبطال الوقوف على العكس الخاص الحقيقي وهو محال. التمسك من وأما ما أورده من المثال، وأشار إليه من الخيال فهو عند من لديه أدنى تحصيل مغن عن الجواب لظهور خلله، ووضوح زلله وبيانه هو أن ما أشار إليه من العكس وإن كان العكس الحق غير داخل تحته فلیس استعمال الخلف فيه إلى المحال، وما أشار إليه وإن كان المحال فيه لازماً فليس إلا للغفلة عما هو نقيض ما فرض عكساً؛ إذ ليس نقيض قولنا: (لا شيء من "ج" "ب") في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بـ "ب" لا دائماً بعض "ج" "ب" في ذلك الوقت فإنه إن قدر كم بها يحتمل أن يكون في الكل أو البعض، ويحتمل أن يكون لقولنا: (في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") ويحتمل أن يكون لقولنا : (لا دائماً) فالنقيض إذ ذاك يجب أن يكون ما مثل قولنا: إما بعض "ج" "ب" غير متيقن والمحال إنما
As for the third objection, although it is presented in the best arrangement, it is outside of verification. It does not follow from the fact that the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the more general possible that one would suffice with that instead of knowing the particular converse; for the particular may involve something that the general does not involve, so research into the particular is obligatory, even if it is not free from falling under this general. How [could one claim] expansion and rigidity [by asserting] that the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the general possible? This indicates the ignorance of its proponent, his lack of training, and weakness of his insight. For the converse of affirmative propositions occurs with general possibility, and that does not fall under the absolute universal nor the general possible. As for it not falling under the general possible, because it [the general possible] is more general than it, since it encompasses what must exist and what may not exist, and the more general does not fall under the more specific. Then, even if it is assumed that the absolute universal also encompasses what may not exist, then it is equivalent to the general possible, and the equivalent is not more general than what is equivalent to it. Then, even if the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the general possible, that is not a necessary consequence of using qiyas al-khalf in explaining the true converse. If it were a hindrance in explanation, then it would also be a hindrance in every method that can be used, and that would invalidate arriving at the true particular converse, which is impossible. As for what he presented as an example and indicated by way of imagination, it is, for one who has the least bit of learning, sufficient to dispense with a response due to its obvious flaw and clear error. Its explanation is that what he indicated as a converse, even if the true converse is not included under it, the use of al-khalf in it does not lead to impossibility. And what he indicated, even if impossibility is necessary in it, that is only due to heedlessness of what is the contradictory of what he assumed as a converse. For the contradictory of our statement: 'No J is B' in some times of its being described by B, not always, [is not] some J is B at that time? For if it is assumed that it [the falsehood] could be in the whole or the part, and it could be due to our statement 'in some times of its being B', and it could be due to our statement 'not always', then the contradictory at that time must be something like our statement: either some J is B as long as it is B, or some J is not B as long as it is B. For if it is possible that falsehood is in attributing B to every J or to some of it, then the affirmative in the part is true, and its taking suffices for the whole. And if it is possible that falsehood is in our statement 'in some times of its being B', then the permanent affirmative is true. And if it [falsehood] is due to our statement 'not always', then the permanent negative is true. And it is not hidden that what is true among these necessary consequences, namely the affirmative (in some part always with the duration of B) or the permanent negative (in some part with the duration of B) is not certain, and the impossibility is only...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is a detailed refutation of the third objection. The objector claimed that since the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and general possible universals, there is no need to study particular converses. The author attacks this as ignorant and refutes it in several steps. First, he notes that the converse of affirmative propositions falls under general possibility (imkan 'amm), not under absolute or general possible. He explains that general possible is more comprehensive than the converse of affirmatives, so the converse does not fall under general possible. Even if we assume absolute universal is coextensive with general possible, they are equivalent, not more general. Second, even if every converse did fall under these universals, that would not be a necessary consequence of using qiyas al-khalf for true converses; and if it were an obstacle, it would invalidate all methods, which is absurd. Third, he addresses a specific example given by the objector involving a proposition 'No J is B' for a limited time. The author shows that the objector's understanding of the contradictory is flawed. The contradictory of a temporally qualified universal negative is not a simple particular affirmative but must account for the time and scope. Depending on where the falsehood lies (in quantity, time, or duration), the true contradictory could be a particular affirmative always, a permanent affirmative, or a permanent negative. The author concludes that what is true among these possibilities is not certain, and the impossibility only [cut off]. This passage demonstrates the nuanced logic of contradictory propositions in temporal modal logic.

العكس الخاص الحقيقي
نقيض
بدوام "ب"

Analyzing the Contradictory of a Temporally Qualified Universal Negative
Author refutes the third objection that the converse of every proposition falls under absolute/possible universal, showing its implications and rebutting an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct contradictory of a temporally qualified universal negative proposition and explain why the objector's example fails.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a temporal universal negative is not a simple particular affirmative.

The objector gave an example involving a universal negative with a temporal qualifier. The author shows that the objector misunderstood the contradictory, leading to a false impossibility. Understanding how to properly negate such propositions is essential for sound logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The proposition is: 'No J is B' in some times of its being described by B, not always. This is a universal negative with temporal qualifications.
The phrase 'in some times' and 'not always' makes it a temporally restricted claim.
2
Understand the objector's attempted contradictory
The objector claims the contradictory is 'Some J is B' in that time. But the author argues this is wrong.
3
Recognize the author's critique
The falsehood of the original could be due to quantity (the whole or part), time (some times or always), or the duration qualifier (not always). So the contradictory must account for all possibilities.
This shows the objector oversimplified.
4
Construct the correct contradictory
The true contradictory is a disjunction: either some J is B always (as long as it is B) or some J is not B always (as long as it is B). This covers the possible sources of falsehood.
The author presents this as the only way to guarantee contradiction.
5
Conclude the proof
Thus, the objector's example does not lead to impossibility because the contradictory is not a single proposition but a disjunction. The impossibility only arises from a mistaken assumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التشكيك الثالث: فهو وإن أورد على أحسن تنميق فخارج عن التحقيق، وليس يلزم من كون عكس كل قضية داخلاً تحت الإطلاق والإمكان الأعم، وإلا اكتفى به عن معرفة العكس الخاص؛ إذ قد يتعلق بالخاص ما لا يتعلق بالعام فالبحث عن الخاص واجب، وإن كان لا خلو عن أن يكون داخلاً تحت هذا الأعم، كيف التوسع والتحجر بدعوى دخول عكس كل قضية تحت الإطلاق والإمكان العام مما يُشعر بجهل قائله وقلة تدربه، وضعف نظره، فإن عكس القضايا الموجبة يقع بالإمكان العام، وذلك مما لا دخول له تحت المطلق العام ولا الممكن العام، أما أنه لا دخول لها تحت الممكن العام فلكونه أعـمّ منه؛ لتناوله ما لا بد من وجوده وما من وجوده بد، والأعم لا دخول له تحت الأخص. ثم وإن قدر أن المطلقة العامة مما يتناول أيضاً ما من وجوده بد، فهي مساوية للمكن العام والمساوي لا يكون أعمّ مما ساواه. ثم إنه وإن كان عكس كل قضية داخلاً تحت المطلق والممكن العام، فليس ذلك مما وقع لازماً في بيان العكس الحقيقي من استعمال قياس الخلف، فإن كان مما يمنع من به في البيان فهو أيضاً لازم في كل ما يقدر استعماله الطرق، وفيه إبطال الوقوف على العكس الخاص الحقيقي وهو محال. التمسك من وأما ما أورده من المثال، وأشار إليه من الخيال فهو عند من لديه أدنى تحصيل مغن عن الجواب لظهور خلله، ووضوح زلله وبيانه هو أن ما أشار إليه من العكس وإن كان العكس الحق غير داخل تحته فلیس استعمال الخلف فيه إلى المحال، وما أشار إليه وإن كان المحال فيه لازماً فليس إلا للغفلة عما هو نقيض ما فرض عكساً؛ إذ ليس نقيض قولنا: (لا شيء من "ج" "ب") في بعض أوقات كونه متصفاً بـ "ب" لا دائماً بعض "ج" "ب" في ذلك الوقت فإنه إن قدر كم بها يحتمل أن يكون في الكل أو البعض، ويحتمل أن يكون لقولنا: (في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") ويحتمل أن يكون لقولنا : (لا دائماً) فالنقيض إذ ذاك يجب أن يكون ما مثل قولنا: إما بعض "ج" "ب" ما دام "ب") و(إما بعض "ج" ليس "ب" ما دام "ب" فإنه إن احتمل أن يكون الكذب في نسبة "ب" إلى (كل "ج") أو إلى بعضه، صدق الإيجاب في البعض، وكان أخذه مغنياً عن الكل، وإن احتمل أن يكون الكذب في قولنا: (في بعض أوقات كونه "ب") صدق الإيجاب الدائم، وإن كان لقولنا: (لا دائماً) صدق السلب الدائم. ولا يخفى أن ما هو الصادق من هذه اللوازم وهو الإيجاب (في البعض دائماً بدوام "ب") أو (السلب الدائم في البعض بدوام "ب") غير متيقن والمحال إنما
As for the third objection, although it is presented in the best arrangement, it is outside of verification. It does not follow from the fact that the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the more general possible that one would suffice with that instead of knowing the particular converse; for the particular may involve something that the general does not involve, so research into the particular is obligatory, even if it is not free from falling under this general. How [could one claim] expansion and rigidity [by asserting] that the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the general possible? This indicates the ignorance of its proponent, his lack of training, and weakness of his insight. For the converse of affirmative propositions occurs with general possibility, and that does not fall under the absolute universal nor the general possible. As for it not falling under the general possible, because it [the general possible] is more general than it, since it encompasses what must exist and what may not exist, and the more general does not fall under the more specific. Then, even if it is assumed that the absolute universal also encompasses what may not exist, then it is equivalent to the general possible, and the equivalent is not more general than what is equivalent to it. Then, even if the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and the general possible, that is not a necessary consequence of using qiyas al-khalf in explaining the true converse. If it were a hindrance in explanation, then it would also be a hindrance in every method that can be used, and that would invalidate arriving at the true particular converse, which is impossible. As for what he presented as an example and indicated by way of imagination, it is, for one who has the least bit of learning, sufficient to dispense with a response due to its obvious flaw and clear error. Its explanation is that what he indicated as a converse, even if the true converse is not included under it, the use of al-khalf in it does not lead to impossibility. And what he indicated, even if impossibility is necessary in it, that is only due to heedlessness of what is the contradictory of what he assumed as a converse. For the contradictory of our statement: 'No J is B' in some times of its being described by B, not always, [is not] some J is B at that time? For if it is assumed that it [the falsehood] could be in the whole or the part, and it could be due to our statement 'in some times of its being B', and it could be due to our statement 'not always', then the contradictory at that time must be something like our statement: either some J is B as long as it is B, or some J is not B as long as it is B. For if it is possible that falsehood is in attributing B to every J or to some of it, then the affirmative in the part is true, and its taking suffices for the whole. And if it is possible that falsehood is in our statement 'in some times of its being B', then the permanent affirmative is true. And if it [falsehood] is due to our statement 'not always', then the permanent negative is true. And it is not hidden that what is true among these necessary consequences, namely the affirmative (in some part always with the duration of B) or the permanent negative (in some part with the duration of B) is not certain, and the impossibility is only...
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is a detailed refutation of the third objection. The objector claimed that since the converse of every proposition falls under the absolute and general possible universals, there is no need to study particular converses. The author attacks this as ignorant and refutes it in several steps. First, he notes that the converse of affirmative propositions falls under general possibility (imkan 'amm), not under absolute or general possible. He explains that general possible is more comprehensive than the converse of affirmatives, so the converse does not fall under general possible. Even if we assume absolute universal is coextensive with general possible, they are equivalent, not more general. Second, even if every converse did fall under these universals, that would not be a necessary consequence of using qiyas al-khalf for true converses; and if it were an obstacle, it would invalidate all methods, which is absurd. Third, he addresses a specific example given by the objector involving a proposition 'No J is B' for a limited time. The author shows that the objector's understanding of the contradictory is flawed. The contradictory of a temporally qualified universal negative is not a simple particular affirmative but must account for the time and scope. Depending on where the falsehood lies (in quantity, time, or duration), the true contradictory could be a particular affirmative always, a permanent affirmative, or a permanent negative. The author concludes that what is true among these possibilities is not certain, and the impossibility only [cut off]. This passage demonstrates the nuanced logic of contradictory propositions in temporal modal logic.

العكس الخاص الحقيقي

B) Some J is B always (as long as it is B) or some J is not B always (as long as it is B).
C) No J is B at all times.
D) Some J is B at some times but not always.

Key Takeaway
Properly identifying the contradictory of a temporally qualified proposition requires considering all possible grounds of falsehood, not just a simple negation.

Deriving Opposite Relations from Converses
Author demonstrates logical relations between four terms with converses and opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the relations of converse and strict opposite to deduce new opposite relations between terms in a chain of logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Strict opposite relations propagate through converse relations between terms.

This passage demonstrates how logical relations can be extended through a chain. Given two pairs of converses and one pair of strict opposites, the author shows that the remaining pair must also be strict opposites. This kind of reasoning is foundational in Islamic logic for establishing equivalences and oppositions between definitions and properties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the given relations
The passage states: B and C are converses (each is predicated of the other universally), A and D are converses, and A and B are strict opposites (cannot coexist nor both be absent from any subject). These are the premises.
Converses here mean 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is B'; similarly for A and D.
2
Prove that D and C cannot coexist
Assume D exists in a subject. Then A must exist (since A and D are converses). Because A and B are opposites, B cannot exist. Since B and C are converses, C cannot exist. Thus D and C cannot be together. The same reasoning from C shows they cannot coexist.
The argument uses the contrapositive: if D then not-C, and if C then not-D.
3
Prove that D and C cannot both be absent
Now assume D does not exist. Then A does not exist (converse). Since A and B are exhaustive opposites, B must exist. Then C must exist (converse). Thus if D is absent, C is present. Similarly, if C is absent, D is present. Therefore, they cannot both be absent.
This uses the 'absolute removal' aspect of the opposite relation.
4
Conclude the opposite relation
Combining the two proofs, D and C are strict opposites: they cannot coexist and cannot both be absent. This conclusion, though cut off in the text, is the logical necessity.
The missing conclusion would explicitly state 'فـ د و ج متقابلان' (so D and C are opposites).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتلازمات والمتقابلات، فإنه إذا كان "ب" و"ج" يتعاكسان في الحمل، وكذلك "أ" و "د" وكان "أ" مقابلاً لـ "ب" مقابلة يمتنع الجمع بينهما والخلو منهما، فيجب أن يكون "د" و"ج" أيضاً كذلك؛ لأنه إذا وجد "د" في موضوعه فقد وجد فيه "أ"؛ لضرورة تعاكسهما، ويلزم من وجود "أ" انتفاء "ب"؛ لضرورة تعاندهما، ومن انتفاء "ب" انتفاء "ج"، وكذلك إذا وجد "ج" في موضوعه فقد وجد فيه "ب"، ويلزم من وجود "ب" انتفاء "أ" ومن انتفاء "أ" انتفاء "د" لما سبق، وكما يلزم من وجود كل واحد من "د" و"ج" انتفاء الآخر، فيلزم من انتفاء كل واحد وجود الآخر؛ لأنه إذا لم يوجد في موضوعه لم يوجد "أ" وإذا لم يوجد "أ" فـ "ب" موجود فـ "ج" موجود، إذ (كل) "ب" "ج")، وكذا إذا لم يوجد "ج" في موضوعه لم يوجد "ب" وما لم يوجد فـ "أ" موجود، ويلزم من وجود "أ" أن يكون "ج" موجوداً، وعند ذلك فيلزم من
As for entailments and opposites: if B and C are converses in predication, and likewise A and D, and A is opposite to B in an opposition that prevents both coexistence and removal, then D and C must also be such. Because if D exists in its subject, then A exists in it, due to the necessity of their being converses; and from the existence of A follows the non-existence of B, due to the necessity of their opposition; and from the non-existence of B follows the non-existence of C. Likewise, if C exists in its subject, then B exists in it, and from the existence of B follows the non-existence of A, and from the non-existence of A follows the non-existence of D, as above. And just as from the existence of each of D and C the non-existence of the other follows, so from the non-existence of each the existence of the other follows. Because if it does not exist in its subject, then A does not exist; and if A does not exist, then B exists, so C exists, since (Every) B is C. Similarly, if C does not exist in its subject, then B does not exist, and if B does not exist, then A exists, and from the existence of A it follows that C is existent. And upon that it follows from
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage from the ninth chapter examines the relationships between four terms when some are converses and others are opposites. The author sets up a scenario where B and C are converses in predication (i.e., 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is B'), and similarly A and D are converses. Additionally, A and B are opposites in a strict sense: they cannot coexist nor both be absent (i.e., they are contradictory or exhaustive opposites). The author then proves that D and C must also be such mutual opposites. The argument proceeds by considering cases: if D exists, then A exists (by their converse relation), which implies B's non-existence (due to opposition), and hence C's non-existence (since B and C are converses). Similarly, if C exists, then B exists, implying A's non-existence and hence D's non-existence. Therefore, D and C cannot coexist. Moreover, the author shows that they cannot both be absent: if D is absent, then A is absent, so B is present (due to the exhaustive opposition), and then C is present (by conversion). Likewise, if C is absent, then B is absent, so A is present, and then D is present (by conversion). Thus D and C are opposites of the same type as A and B. The text ends abruptly at the page break with 'وعند ذلك فيلزم من' indicating the conclusion, which likely states that D and C are also opposites.

متلازمات
متقابلات
تعاكس

Key Takeaway
When two terms are converses and another pair are converses, a strict opposite relationship between one member of each pair forces the same opposite relationship between the remaining pair.

Conversion of Universal Affirmatives: The Coextension Condition
Text explains conversion of universal affirmative predicates under coextension conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a universal affirmative proposition can be validly converted, and identify coextensive terms in an Islamic logical context.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative conversion requires coextension of subject and predicate.

In classical Islamic logic, the operation of conversion (عكس) allows swapping subject and predicate. But not every universal affirmative proposition can be converted without loss of truth. This passage from Daqaiq Haqaiq explains the special condition: coextension. You will learn how to recognize when two terms are perfectly matched in extension, and why that matters for building sound definitions and arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conversion operation
Find where the author states that 'B' is convertible (mun'akis) with 'A' in universal affirmative predication. Note the condition: 'A' is predicated of every 'C' and every 'B' and nothing else, and 'B' is predicated of every 'C'.
This means 'A' covers only B and C, and B covers all C.
2
Understand coextension
Coextension means the two terms apply to exactly the same set of individuals. In the text, 'A' and 'B' become coextensive because everything that is A is either B or C, but since B covers all C, the 'C' portion is included in B. So the extension of A equals the extension of B.
3
Test the conversion
Given that condition, check if the universal affirmative 'كل ب أ' (every B is A) and 'كل أ ب' (every A is B) both hold. If yes, conversion is valid.
4
Apply to Islamic definitions
In defining terms in fiqh or usul, if we claim that the definition applies to exactly the same cases as the definiendum, we must check coextension. For example, if we define 'qiyas' (analogy) as 'extension of ruling from original to branch due to common cause', we must ensure the definition applies to all and only cases of qiyas.
A defective definition leads to wrong legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثامن في اعتبار أمور نافعة الاعتبار في صور الحجج وجود 'ب' انتفاء 'أ' فيلزم انتفاء 'د' ومن نفيه وجوده، وكذا بالعكس؛ لأنه إذا صدق على الموضوع إما 'ب' وإما 'أ' و(كل 'أ' 'د') صدق إما 'ب' وإما 'د'، وكما يلزم التقابل بين 'ب' و'د'، فكذا بين 'ج' و'أ'؛ لأنه إذا صدق على الموضوع إما 'د' وإما 'ج' و(كل 'د' 'أ') فهو إما 'ج' وإما 'أ'، وإما إذا كان 'أ' مقولاً على كل 'ج' وعلى كل 'ب' ولا يقال على غيرهما، وكان 'ب' مقولاً على كل 'ج' فـ 'ب' منعكس في الحمل على كل 'أ'؛ إذ هو مقول على كل ما يقال عليه 'أ' فكل ما هو 'ب' هو 'أ' وكل ما هو 'أ' هو 'ب'، وكذا أيضاً إن كان 'ج' مقولاً على كل 'ب' فهو منعكس أيضاً في الحمل على كل 'أ' لما حققناه في قول 'ب' على كل 'ج'.
The eighth art on considering beneficial matters in the consideration of argument forms: The existence of 'B' [implies] the absence of 'A', so the absence of 'D' is necessary, and from its negation its existence, and likewise conversely; because if either 'B' or 'A' is true of the subject, and (every 'A' is 'D'), then either 'B' or 'D' is true. And just as the correspondence between 'B' and 'D' is necessary, so too between 'C' and 'A'; because if either 'D' or 'C' is true of the subject, and (every 'D' is 'A'), then it is either 'C' or 'A'. And if 'A' is predicated of every 'C' and every 'B' and not predicated of anything else, and 'B' is predicated of every 'C', then 'B' is convertible in predication over every 'A'; because it is predicated of everything of which 'A' is predicated, so everything that is 'B' is 'A' and everything that is 'A' is 'B'. And likewise also if 'C' is predicated of every 'B', it is also convertible in predication over every 'A' due to what we verified in the statement 'B' of every 'C'.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage from Daqaiq Haqaiq is a logical exposition on conversion (عكس) of universal affirmative propositions, framed within the eighth art dealing with argument forms. The author uses variable letters as placeholders for terms. It begins by stating a conditional relationship: if B exists and A is absent, then D must be absent, and vice versa. This is derived from the fact that the subject is either B or A, and since every A is D, the subject must be either B or D, establishing a correspondence between B and D. Similarly, from the subject being D or C and every D being A, we get C or A, establishing correspondence between C and A. Then the author turns to the condition for conversion (انعكاس). If A is predicated of all C and all B and nothing else (making A equivalent to the disjunction of B and C), and B is predicated of every C (so B is broader than C), then B and A are convertible: everything that is B is A, and everything that is A is B. This is because A is predicated only of B and C, and B is predicated of all C, so the extension of A is exactly the union of B and C, but B already covers C, so A's extension equals B's extension. Similarly, if C is predicated of every B, then C becomes convertible with A. In Islamic logic, this mirrors the relationship between a genus and a species: for example, the term 'animal' (A) is predicated of 'human' (B) and 'horse' (C), but if 'human' is predicated of all 'horse' (which is false intellectually, but used here as a hypothetical), then 'human' and 'animal' would be coextensive. The passage teaches that conversion of a universal affirmative is only valid when the predicate applies to exactly the same things as the subject—a concept critical for sound definitions and arguments.

Key Terms
عكس
منعكس
مقول
تقابل

A) When 'A' is predicated of every 'B' and nothing else, and 'B' is predicated of every 'C'.
B) When 'A' is predicated of every 'C' and every 'B' and nothing else, and 'B' is predicated of every 'C'.
C) When 'B' and 'C' are both predicated of every 'A'.
D) When 'A' is predicated of every 'B' and 'C' is predicated of every 'B'.

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative conversion is only valid when the subject and predicate are coextensive—a condition that ensures the definition or argument is sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify a term in fiqh that you suspect might have a different extension than its common definition. For example, the term 'riba' (usury). Use the conversion test: is every instance of riba covered by your definition? And does your definition cover anything that is not riba? Write a short paragraph explaining whether the definition passes the coextension test.
?
2. Consider the following argument: 'Every 'illah (effective cause) leads to a hukm (ruling). Every act that leads to a hukm is an 'illah. Therefore, every 'illah is an act that leads to a hukm, and vice versa.' Is the conversion valid? Apply the coextension condition from the passage.
